THE RED PAGE.
AMONG the realistic pictures in Bush
Studies, take this of the weaning of a
poddied lamb from “ Scrammy ’And”
(the sketch which contains that most
intimate study of a solitary man’s dog, the dog
almost human).
The shepherd is dead in his hut, guarded by
the dog.
Several times that day the ewe and lamb came in,
looked without speculation at the figure on the bunk,
then moved to the dead fire-place. But though the
water in the billy was cold, the dog would not allow
either to touch it. That was for tea when his master
woke. . .
Next day the ewe and lamb came again. The lamb
bunted several irresponsive objects—never its dam’s
udder—baaing listlessly. Though the first day the ewe
had looked at ifce bunk, and baaed, she was wiser
now, though sheep are slow to learn. Around that
dried dish outside the lamb sniffed, baaing faintly.
Adroitly the ewe led the way to the creek, and the
lamb followed. From the bank the lamb looked at
her, then faced round to the hut, and baaing disconso-
latelv, trotted a few paces back. From the water’s edge
the mother ewe called. The lamb looked at her vacantly,
and without interest descended. The ewe bent and drank
sparingly, meaningly. The lamb sniffed the water, and
unsatisfied, complained. The hut was hidden, but it
turned that way. Again the ewe leisurely drank. This
time the lamb’s lips touched the water, but did not drink.
Into its mouth raised to bleat a few drops fell. Hastily
the mother’s head went to the water. She did not drink,
but the lamb did. Higher up, where the creek was dry,
they crossed to tender grass in the billabong, then joined
the flock for the first time. \
Is not that admirably seen and shown ?
The picture of selector Ned drawing a cheque—
Ned was no favourite among women. This was partly
from his being “ flash,” but more from his reputation for
flogging his missus. Ned, moreover, had tried to force
his example on the male community by impressing upon
them his philosophy, that it was the proper thing to Hit
a woman every time you niet her, since she must either
be coming from mischief or going to it. As to his flash-
ness, he considered he had something to he flash about.
He had been twice to Sydney ; and not only could lie
spell by ear, but, given an uncertain number of favouring
circumstances, he could use a pen to the extent of
putting his name to a cheque.
Certainly, before he would attempt this, Liz, his
missus, had to pen up the goats, shut the hut,
and, with the dogs and the kids, drive the fowls
a mile from the house, and keep them there till
Ned fired a gun. Left to himself, Ned would tear out a
cheque, lay it on the table, place a block of wood on the
bottom edge of the paper, to keep his hand from travel-
ling off it to the table below. Then he had to tie his
wrist to the left side of his belt—ho was left-handed—in
such a manner that his hand could not stray to
the foreign region above the cheque, ink the pen
with his right hand, and place it in the left. But
even then the task was often unaccomplished.
Sometimes he would be so intent on trying to keep the
Edward on the line, that it would run to the end of the
paper, excluding the Stennard , and, despite Ned’s pro-
tests anent insufficient space, the bank did not approve
of part of the signature being placed on the back of the
cheque. When he tried to write small and straight, the
result generally seemed satisfactory till a careful analysis
showed a letter or so missing. Or, just as success seemed
probable, his cheque book would give out, or his pen
break.
It was bad for Liz and her own boy Joey when
either of these accidents occurred, for he would fire no
gun, and, despite all the perspiring activity of Liz, the
kids, and the dogs, some of the fowls would make their
way home to roost on the hut when night came. For
allowing him to be disturbed “ jes as I wus gettin’ me
’and in” he would “take it outer” Liz, or what was
worse to her, “ outer ” Joey.
Jt
“Jyne,” a selectorWwife —
The most pronounced feature of Jyhe’s face was her
mouth, and it seenjed proud of its teeth, especially of the
top row. Without any apparent effort, the last tooth
there was always visible. She was a great power in the
bush, being styled by the folk themselves- “ Babbit
Ketcher,” which, translated, means mid-wife. And the
airs Jvne gave herself were justifiable, for she was the
only “ Rabbit Ketcher ” this side of the township. To
bring a qualified mid-wife from civilisarion would have
represented a crippling expenditure to these cookies.
Jyne’s moderate fees were usually four-legged.
Jt <2*
Bush Studies represents one-phase of Australian
life, in one place, at one time ; and it would have
been well if the author had said this in a preface,
for foreign readers are sure to refer the descrip-
tions to Australia generally. Blit the scenes and
people of the book still exist in north-western
’ New South Wales and elsewhere ; and, never has
the desolate barbarism of the Bush been so power-
fully depicted. One revolts in reading; for the
ghastly facts endure within a day’s journey of the
pleasant bustle of Sydney, that great cancer-city
sucking the blood of the Bush for its sustenance.
If ever a statesman arises in Australia, it will be
a cardinal feature of his statesmanship (so far as
Govt, expenditure goes) to starve the gross
cities so that the men and women on the land,
the essential creators of Australia, may be
helped to a life with some breadth, some huma-
nity, .some intellectual aspiration. True : the
author of Busk Studies is a jaundiced observer;
all her experiences have been tragic: all her
impressions are impressions of horror and disgust.
For her, the Bush epithets are natural—
The oaths and adjectives so freely used were surely
coined for such circumstances. “ Damned” the wretched,
starving, and starved sheep looked and were ; “ bloody ”
the beaks of the glutted crows ; “blasted” the whole'of
the plain they drove through !
There is another side to the Bush story: a side
with pleasant homesteads, successful labour,
prosperous seasons ; but in many districts the evil
side is the prominent side, in some districts there
instill no other. Spasmodic charity in time of
drought can help little: there is no redemption in
pauperising doles or in the cast-off dresses of
Lady Bountiful, however useful their momentary
boon, however excellent the sentiment inspiring
them. Only the strenuous and.persistent aid of
Government, directed on a wise policy, can make
much of the life Out-Back worth living. Men on
the land—they can endure ; but the women ? and
the children ? What crop is Australia reaping
while money is lavished on “relief works” and
on city improvement ?
&
Before leaving Bush Studies note two things:
one that its truthful glimpses of Australian life,
graphically expressed, could not (would not) have
been printed in any Australian paper, though they
rank highly as literature and are circulated widely
in book form when issued by an English pub-
lisher. We are too mealy-mouthed (in print),
and stuff far too much “respectable” wadding in
our ears. With all their breadth in some direc-
tions, Australian audiences are still parochial, or
the newspapers make believe they are. The
second thing is this : Busk Studies, written to the
fact, stresses what may be called the pre-
dominantly obstetric quality of typical Bush
life. This quality comes naturally : the increase
of tiocks and herds is the daily business, the source
of living income. There is no prudish shame in
referring to the accidents of begetting and birth :
they form the staple of conversation in many a
home, and the children calmly discuss chances
with their parents. Transference of ideas to the
human species is easy ; for apart from begetting
and birth the Bush yields to many of its denizens
little excitement, scanty diversion of monotonous
occupation. So Busk Studies has already appeared
' ‘ shocking ” to some city critics, smug in the solace
of churches, and theatres, and “ down-the-
harbour” picnics. But the thing goes on.
Criticism begins with prayer and the assertion
that when a novelist pulls your intimate emotions
he ought to hide his strings. When he leaves
the ends hanging out so that . . . (let us mix) . . .
you can watch yourself being cut up on his dis-
secting-table . . . (this is due to the prayer) . . .
how does your disgust differ from that erf the
struck eagle Byron stole from Waller ?
Frank Norris is the god of that American
machine-scnool of writers which tells a stylist like
Joseph Conrad (by the mouth of Munsey’s Maga-
zine) that his work would be much improved by
the free use of a city editor’s blue pencil ! You
can see the machine-school busy every month in
McClure's Maga. —producing articles and stories
which are models of their kind, trenchant and
brilliant as the best product of the Steel Trust.
Journalism admirable, but . . . mechanical, the
literary triumph of a mechanical nation in a
mechanical age, hard, clear, and pitilessly precise.
Yet it is the Amer. Father Tabb who urges
Hozo sterile is the sunshine,
How masculine the blue,
That breeds no shadow, nor betrays
A memory of dew !
Norris’s latest book (and last—though S.M.
Herald encourages him to continue . . . in the next
world, presumably, since he died at San Francisco
last year) is a study of the Chicago wheat-ring,
The Pit (Sydney; Angus and Robertson). The
book was designed as second of a trilogy
(the first was The Octopus, the third is un-
written). It is a fine piece of work, and
holds some breathless pages; but it is nob
as fine as The Octopus, and even The Octo-
pus fell short of Norris’s notion of an epic story
planned in the manner of Zola and executed by
the methods of the U.S.A. The Octopus had a
touch of epic quality ; The Pit is episodical, though
epic references are hammered on with half-ounce
tacks, like the blind on an American spring-roller.
Contemplating the story apd the author’s unerring
handling of the obvious, one is reminded of the
Chicago pork-packer’s pride in his creation-
licking apparatus. In The Pit the reader is the
pig. Here he is stuck ; there he is carried on an
endless belt to be scalded and scraped ; yonder
he is halved and quartered; and at the end
of the book he emerges as pork secundum
artem. If he isn’t pork he ought to be,
for Norris’s apparatus cannot ■ lie any more
than a camera can. He presses the button, and
you have no option but to do the rest—if there is
any faith in formulae. Lo, the characters, scru-
pulously balanced and contrasted ! 10, the motives,
electric-lit, so that even a lady cannot mistake
them ! 10, the phrases, the incidental relief, every-
thing complete and up-to-date, including the
Wagner lead ! and, finally, there is an absorbing
story of a dramatic phase of life. We can con-
ceive the spirit of mechanical America asking, in
amaze : “ What more does the fellow want ? ”
<£*
Yes ... a readable story, a skilful piece of
work; but it is scieuce, not art. If literature
implies good art, then Norris’s is bad science,
since art should conceal science, even if science
do not reveal art. (And that, we flatter our-
selves, is a sentence which will bear some study.)

On Jan. 17 (following a suggestion in The Maga-
zine of Commerce ) bookfellows were asked to throw
into sonnet form this passage, from London
Standard’s commercial column—
Wheat opened steady*and unchanged, and developed a
buoyant tone on firm cables, a bullish “ Price Current
Report,” covering of shorts, and liberal export shipments.
The market later declined on realising, sympathy in corn,
and a slack export demand, and closed efisy at Jc. to
fc. fall. Spot easy.
The task proved too difficult for those who essayed
it —Mitty, Frank R., Hix, E.F.G., E.W.D.—five
stalwarts only. This from E. F. G. Jolley, Mary-
borough, Vic. (to whom 10s. has been forwarded)
is the best sonnet received : Frank R’s ranks next.
Unchanged and steady in the opening .fair
Stands wheat; its buoyant tendency is fanned
By firmness, too, in overseas demand,
A “ Current Price Report ” of bullish air
Towards higher figures.also lends its share,
And eager buyings by the luckless band
Who sold the stocks they never had in hand,
Whilst foreign shipments pi-ices upward bear.
But oh, that steady progress cannot be
Maintained, alas, for holders realise,
And stocks with drooping corn mart sympathise,
While foreign shipments ease, being overshot.
So prices fall from \ c. to 3
With market easy. Fre'e demand for spot.
“Free demand for spot” is not warranted by the
text: “3” is too vague; and there are other
holes: nevertheless, the result is commended to
The Standard, on the ground that such a use of
letters emollit mores of the Stock Exchange, nec
sinit bulls and bears esse feroces. Though a
contrary view may be taken.
dX jx
Rossetti might have dealt with the subject in
this way—
FOR
A LONDON COMMERCIAL
BY OUR REPORTER.
(In The Standard.)
Wheat, for the anguish of the starving : — nay,
The market opens steadily,—but lean
And hark how at. its verge cables sigh in
Determined. Ha ! then buoyantly away ;
For shorts are covered at the brink of day,
And export shipments tug upon the string
That sobs, while operators rise to sing
The Price Report is bullish ! .Do not stray :
On realising, prices downward creep ;
There’s sympathy in com-Mill corn is grass,
And cool again the export side. let be
Say nothing unto bulls for fear they weep
One to three-eighths cent fall. —Close easy was ;
Spot easy. (So is immortality.)
Of course, Rossetti is dead.

Gilbert Murray is Sydney-born (1866) and scion
of a well-known Australian family, so Lond. re-
viewers’ praise of his just-published translation of
Euripides interests us. “He is one of the very
few translators,” writes The Academy, “whose
work gives the effect, not of a translation at all,
but of a substantive poem. More than adequate
in the dialogue, he rises at times in the lyrics to
heights of quite extraordinary' felicity.” And
this version of a chorus from the “ Hippolytus ” is
given in proof—
Could 1 take me to some cavern for mine hiding,
In the hill-tops where the Sun scarce hath trod ;
Or cloud make the home of mine abiding,
As a bird among the bird-droves of God !
Could I wing me to my rest amid the roar
Of the deep Adriatic on the shore,
Where the water of Eridan us is clear,
And Phaethon's sad sisters by his grave
Weep into the river, and each tear
Gleams, a drop of amber, in the wave.
To the strand of the Daughters of the Sunset,
The Apple-tree, the singing and the gold :
Where the mariner must stay him from his onset,
And the red wave is tranquil as of old ;
Yea, beyond that Pillar of the End
That Atlas guardeth, would I wend ;
Where a voice of living ivaters never ceaseth
In God’s quiet garden by the sea,
And Earth, the ancient life-giver, increaseth
Joy among the meadows, like a tree.

“ Nil ” writes :
I complain that many poetasters use constantly the
form a-shine for shining, a-fiow for flowing, and so on.
Instances : A-quiver for quivering, a-roarn for roaminq
( The Bulletin, 7/2/3, p. 3). Others (from memory) are
a-kiss for kissing, a-cry for crying, a-brood for brooding—
and hundreds worse. Some words are used this wav by
time-honoured custom and are euphonious so. But the
habit, as a habit, is inelegant, clum'sy, and so patentlv
the outcome of literary laziness t hat it is time fairly good
rhymesters gave it up. Admittedly it makes a certain
class of words come in suitably in a certain class of
rhythm ; but that rhythm is usually the kind that I have
called to myself “The Australian Metre,” and is the
easiest, “ cheapest” thing of its kind. Here’s a bit of it
written at random :
When the lcookoo-burra’S kookoo comes a-lwugh across the
flat,
And the wattle is a-blossom, and the cricket is a-chat
And the poet is a-scribble and the billy is a-boil,
And the household cat a-kitten and the evening chop
a-spoiL
Oh ! it’s THfix my soul’s a-wobble and my aching head
a-spin,
I’m a-dream, a-gape, a-bubble, and I long to be a-sin !
And so on.
JX
This complaint, generally speaking, is not well
made. A language lives or dies ; but while it lives
it grows, and you cannot stop the growth, though
you may try to direct it. “Nil” confuses cate-
gories when he says that time-honoured usage
makes words euphonious: euphony is independent
of usage. And time-honoured usage began from
some innovator’s usage, good or bad: to-day’s
innovators are creating to-morrow’s timei-honoured
usage.
jx je
Looking to particulars: “Nil? is trying to bar
one of the widest gates that time-honoured usage
has opened to innovators. For many centuries
people have been dealing almost as they chose
with the convenient A shortening in or on or of
into A, Tor example; and some of the usages are
accepted as correct and elegant, The usage that
“Nil” decries is often correct, elegant, and
euphonious: more, it is necessary, for often we
cannot attain our meaning otherwise. Browning’s
A cornfield s ide a-flutter with poppies (Men and,
Women, ii, Ilf!)
does not give to the ear, to the eye, or to the mind
the message given by
A cornfield side fluttering with poppies.
That a-flutter expands the image: it showers
poppies over the field and sets them in motion:
fluttering has comparatively little meaning, yields
comparatively a weak picture. Even in prose you
feel the same need : “rocks awash at low tide”
tells a story: to “rocks washing at low tide”
there are obvious objections.
Then we have abed, ashore, afield, asleep— and
why not a-dream ? Some of “Nil’s ” burlesque in-
stances are accepted as good English : e.g.
a-wobble, agape, even alml (Hugh Miller writes’
“set his Scotch blood aboil’’—and “Nil” is a
Scottish complainant). So that we must take
every case on its merits. A-quiver, a-cry, a-brood
might be correctly used ; a-roarn was certainly in’,
elegant as used by “ Riverina
breezes are a-roam ;
but a-roam and even a-kiss might be justified by a
poet. ■ , v‘ , ’VHTB

The active correspondent—
E.W.D. writes : “ The Canadian Men and Women of
the Time (Toronto ; 1898) says of W. Bliss Carman that
he ‘is descended on both sides from Loyalist stock. One
of his father’s family was an original grantee of Parrtown
(St. John’s, N. 8.), while his mother was great-grand-
daughter of Daniel Bliss, the Tory lawyer, of Concord,
Mass.’ Both Bliss and Carman are common names in
Canada.”
“Ignotus” complains : “ Both N.Z. Times and Welling-
ton Post refused to print this advt., though in the ease of
the Times it was originally accepted and paid for-
ty anted Private Lodgings for married couple. No
children. Limit £3. Smells Of onions or dirty house-
maid absolutely barred. Apply “ Banker,” Times
office.
Will you explain to my dull mind why I may not say I
cannot five in a frowsy slum? What is the etiquette of
the matter? Would The (Iondon) Times have re-
fused the advt. ? Would you ? ” The Times pro-
bably would have refused it, bowing to the fetish
of “ respectable journalism ” : The Bulletin, more
human, would have accepted it. But you have no
grievance. A newspaper proprietor accepts for his news-
paper the advts. he chooses, just as he welcomes to his
home the guests he chopses. You have no more right to
enter his newspaper, uriwelcomed, than you have to enter
his home.

Croyez (a member of the Eyre family), in forwarding a
copy of the family crest, duly headed by the leg in armour,
writes: “ Hume got bis history wrong. The crest given
at Hastings was a helmet or casque ; the leg was lost, and
a second crest given, at a subsequent battle. There is no
‘ fable ’ about the atory.”

“ Quilp N.,” whose verses are nearing a Maoriland port
of publication, was bom in county Durham, England, in
1876. At four years of age he came with his folk to
Dunedin, and four years later to Brisbane, where the
Grammar School claimed him. In 1892 he settled in
Wellington. He has never lived out of sight of salt
water ; his thoughts move with the swing of the seas to
the rumble of shafting ; and he is occupied as an insur-
ance clerk—not even a marine insurance clerk!
President Roosevelt has been forced by an outory of
women to remove Watts’s fine picture, Low and Life,
from a public to a private apartment in the White House.
The picture is designed and executed as purely as a
picture can be ; but prudes object to the mere nudity of
the side-turned female figure. When Watts presented
his picture to the American nation it was hung in the
White House, but similar outcry occasioned its removal
to a museum, whence Roosevelt recently extricated it,
“ We air a great country, sir” ; but 1
Pierre Lafitte, leader of the French Positivists, died
some \Veeks ago. Frederic Harrison, leader of the
Positivists in England, addresses weekly audiences of a
score or so of disciples in Clifford’s Inn, London. From
the great scheme of Comte to Harrison’s conscientious
mumble over his array of four-inch idols—busts of
thought-leaders from M. Aurelius to Auguste himself—is
a sad fall, my regenerative masters !

HANS ANDERSEN.
Dear master of the faint flute of the herbs,
The crystal revel of the stream that .Hows
By magic furroivs where the wind disturbs
Rich, drony mo‘hs upon the plaited rose ;
Alone thou hearest where the wild swan dips
His crest beneath the torrent of the morn ;
Alone thou seest from his tender lips
The sun's last smile to faery Matterhorn /
The nightingale, that in a forest sj ent
Her lovely soul in music for a lord
Of empiry, distilling b’and cortmt,
Threw all herself to thee as thou adored ;
She is not of the earth, and thou art free
From low communion, like her quivering ivings,
That ache for all despair that song can be —
Th’ impenetrable heart of sacred things.
Through thee the loioliest do achieve renown,
The unsought grace of solitmte is theirs,
But thou dost give src\ eremites a crown,
From thee they take our happiness and tears ;
What if the violet leans athwart a stone,
And hears but rivulet or nightingale —
Her secrecies surprised by thee alone
Shall charm young hearts in immemorial tale.
Wh at largesse of all magic ! Does the bird
Lament thee in her thicket ? Shall her note
Fall ivhere alone a stealthy leaf is stirred
By sleeping castle in a sleepy moat ,
Aral never heart be there her song to tell,
Nor any cunning weaver of her brain ?
Shall ive for ever watch the citadel,
And never see th? sanctuary again ? •
M.i,
H.C.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Life.
N.s. w.
Just a little waiting
Ere she came along,
I ace aglow with gladness,
Lips alight with song.
Just a little striving
To keep straight and clean
For the sake of someone
Whom you thought a queen.
Just a little hoping—
Night attuned to day—
Garlands of red roses
Bordering every way.
Just one glad, short, gold year,
All the future bright;
Now the long, long grey years
And the misty night.
Just an idle jesting
Wrought in careless mood—
Thoughtless word ill-chosen
And ill-understood.
J ust a wide, white pathway
Winding down the years,
Strewn with withered roses,
Watered with salt tears.
VELDT.
A Ballade of Brag.
In an age of Bounce and Brag,
Where the Virtues drone and drag,
Every man his jaw must wag
Far and near;
That his merits manifold
May be full and duly told
He must blare—a brazen, bold
Chanticleer 1
If he’s modestly inclined
(Less of Jaw and more of Mind),
He must jdeld to men of Wind
In the fray ;
For your neighbours, fool and sage,
In this great enlightened Age,
All your merits guess and gauge
By your Bray 1
So you need a throat of Brass,
And a fearsome fund of Gas,
And but little—O alas !
Little Brain I
For the brainy men are few,
And their hats are seldom 1 ew,
And their outlook’s black or bl re
In the main !
Note the pressmen’s glad surprise
In this crooked Land of Lies,
How they greet with joyous cries
Yonder elf—
How they sing with heart e ate
“ Lo, he comes in regal state;
He is noble ; he is great;
He hath Pelf ! ”
’Tis the creed of every Star
In the Pulpit— at the Bar-
Spread your virtues near and far
If you’re wise;
Wear a placard on your coat,
Let your praises swell your throat,
Sing vour name in every note—
Advertise ! 1
It is true that flesh is grass
And that mortals quickly pais,
But we still believe in Brass
And in Drum;
And the voices that might guide ua
In the perils that betide us
Now are never heard beside us—
They are dumb !
So the Trumpet we must blow,
Through the Market as we go,
That the crowd may clamor—Lo 1
What a Knave !
And in case we blow it well,
It may echo in our knell,
And a stone the story tell
On our grave !
Q.
P. LUFTIG.
Brought to Earth.
Child of mute wonder and rare dreams,
The stars had governance of his soul,
The vagrant moon his fancies stole
And raced with them through night’s extremes.
Ere he could follow her, rough hands
Had bound him to a sombre creed ;
He cried the pale moon in his need,
But she ranged through her netherlands.
And darkness weaved itself around
Till part of his own life it grew;
His fretted soul no longer knew
The pageantry of light and sound.
No longer fancy went a-soar
In strange excesses of delight ;
For him the night was only night,
The day was day, and nothing more.
Through all his years grey shadows trod
Like wrack above a stagnant tide,
And they who made him so, took pride
For bettering the work of God.
T.
THOROLD DALRYMPLE.

MISERY & HELPLESSNESS.

CHEERFULNESS & ACTIVITY.
A Very Convincing Narrative.
The Case of Mr. P. B. HUNT.
(BY a SYDNEY REPORTER.)
The ready compliance of Mr. Percy
Bordon Hunt, of No. 8 The Terrace,
Bourke-street, Redfem, with the request
of a special interview, illustrates a
series of difficulties and complica-
tions from which there was no immediate
prospect of escape. After mentioning that
he was a native of Loudon and a resident of
these colonies for the last ten years, Mr.
Huut went on to say :
“ It was when I came out to this country
that I had a very bitter experience with bad
health, and I hope I shall never have to go
through anything like it again, as once in a
life-time is quite enough. I had previously
suffering from a complaint called locomotor
ataxia, and I had never felt really well since.
The change in climate evidently did not
suit me, for my head started to ache most
unmercifully, making me feel quite unfit to
take part in an) thing- I was sc drowsy
and low-spirited, too, no p'easant thoughts
ever coming to give me a moment’s happi-
ness, and I did not seem to care what
happened ”
“ Did you receive any medical attention?"
asked the writer.
“Yes. I had been to several hospitals,
where the doctors prescribed physic for me,
but it could not have been suitable for my
complaints, as it took no effect upon me.
Their medicines were sc useless that I might
as well have thrown them down the drain.
The doctors said it was next to impossible
to properly recover from the complaint
which had made a wreck of me, and this was
and awful reflection for one who had always
been so hale and hearty before, and naturally
making me more sad and hopeless than ever ; *
but I thought I would try some other
medicine before giving up all hope, and
when I had done so my condition was just
as bad as ever. I could not get my breath
freely owing to the heavy sensation in my
chest, and 1 could not sleep at all well. If
I did doze off I was made restless by
dreams that were most repulsive, and, once
more finding myself awake, I dreaded the
thought of falling asleep again because the
dreams were so unpleasant. That was
through being sc nervous, I suppose, for I
trembled at the slighest noise an 1 was
excited at every turu. My mouth had a
nauseating taste which I imagined came
from the white coating on my to: gue. and
there was such a strange feeling about my
eyes — a heavy sens-tion as if they had
swollen during the night. YVh nin London
I was secretary to one of the most eminent
Congregationalministeis in the city, and con-
sequenUy had a great amount of brain work
to do, so that when I came here and felt so
brain-w eary a> d facgeo out I felt my position
all the more keen y. There was no life in
me, and I gave myself up as helpless to
throw off the utter deadness of vitality
under which I was existing. Simply through
excessive weakness my limbs were racked
with aches. It was just the same, in the
morning after lying down all night, for my
legs were as tired as if I had been climbing
steep hills instead of trying to rest mvtslf,
and the mere thought of going to work made
me shudder in despair. There was something
radically wurong w ith my loins, too, as the
painful aches across them caused me no end
of inconvenience, and more so whtn I had
to walk to any place. But so far as that
goes there was nothing that I could find any
pleasure in doing, as my loss of energy was
so great that it w as almost a trouble to dress
myself For a man whose temperament is
naturally active and energetic it was a
terrible hardship to be in such a weak and
powerless condition, but the most melancholy
ahase of the situation was that I could get
,n *’■ hi w>nt of mv difficulties.
It seemed a forlorn hope to think of recover-
ing my health again, but was there anything
I had not given a trial to ! I asked myself. A
little reflection told me that Clements Toni<
had escaped my notice up till now, and al
my deplorable state of feebleness required
immediate attention I said I would give it
a trial.”
“ Were you pleased that you did so.”
“My word I was, for Clements Toaij
acted on my system as no other medicine
had done. Do you know that I had no real
appetite from the time I was taken ill until
I took Clements Tonic, which made me so
hungry that I was always wanting food. 1
was overjoyed at this success, especially as
my pains were diminishing with each bottle
of Clements Tonic,which made my digestive
apparatus able and willing to assimilate my
food and fill my veins with pure, rich tlood,
and with my heart strong and vigorous to
pump that blood to every part of my body.
As time went on it became apparent that 1
was on the road to recovery, as my head-
aches and giddiness were gone and my sight
was good. I breathed freely owing to chest
pains being relieved, while the flatulence
which had affected my stomach did not
trouble me after using a few bottles ol
Clements Tonic. Would it be possible for
me to speak too well of a medicine w’hicb
did so much for me when all others failed ’
My opinion is that I will never be able to
liquidate my debt to Clements Tonic for its
miraculous influence over me, for it actually
raised me from misery and helplessness to a
life of cheerfulness and activity. _ This was
done by degrees, of course, for I got
stronger because I was getting plenty of
rest, my sleep being sound and dreamless,
and the morning life found me equipped for
the duties of life as I had not been for many
a day. I was no longer sickened by a bitter
taste and a dry Tongue when I woke up,
neither was I troubled with that weariness
of limb which had made all my movements
so irksome. I had reason to look at life in
an entirely different aspect then, thank
as the pain-killing properties of Clements
Tonic gave such prompt relief thut I .was
soon free from all my torments, and as a
remedy for invigorating the nervous system
I am smethat medicine is the best in the
world. My nerves we eso ready and keen
that my daily work became a pleasure, and
a capacity for physical and mental extrtion
proportionally increased. I did not re-
linquish the use of Clements To ic until my
health was as sound as it had ever been,
being an entirely new man, and as I have
since enjoyed immunity from the oilmen's
waich nothing but Clements Tonic could
cure it is only fair to my fellow colonists
that I should* tell them by what means I
was released from wretchedness/’
“It w ill he necessary to print your
statements, then.”
“Of course it will; and you may publish
them in any way you like.”

SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 28, 1903.
The Possible Public Bankruptcy.
After stating that if evidence were only afforded that
care and economy were exercised no complaints or cri-
cisms would be heard, the London Daily Mail points out
the first suggestion of Australian bankruptcy appeared in
a Sydney weekly newspaper.— Cablegram.
The last few days have been days of storm
and stress in those circles where the great,
melancholy, incessant loan is floated. New
South Wales 3 per cent, securities have
dropped in London to about 90. Maoriland
has floated a 3 per cent, loan of £1,250,000,
and the luckless underwriters who guaran-
teed, for a large commission, to get it off at
£94 10s. have been left with almost the
whole amount on their hands It is reckoned
that Maoriland will get about £9l for every
£lOO of debt incurred, and that London
won’t look at any new Maoriland loan for a
long, long time. Queensland is trying to
borrow £600,000, not for public works,
but to make up revenue deficiencies,
which, considering that Queensland has
already borrowed something approaching
£2,000,000 to make up revenue deficiencies,
is not calculated to inspire respect or good
feeling at this juncture. Premier See, of
N.S.W., has cabled to England a wild and
ridiculously inaccurate yarn about the splen-
did solvency of New South Wales, and
Agent-General Copeland and Attorney-
General Wise are both understood to be
addressing the financiers of London at much
length and with great energy on the same
subject. Australian securities are being
spoken of in England with such disrespect
and contempt as has not previously come
their way since Australia first existed. The
Tory party puts the trouble down to Old
Age Pensions, socialistic legislation, and
the famous Six Hatters. The See Owe’-
Sullivan Government puts it down to
base slanders and unfounded allegations.
Also the fact that “a Sydney weekly news-
paper ” mentioned the possibility of bank-
ruptcy and repudiation is alleged to be
partly responsible for the trouble. The
Bulletin presumes that it is that weekly
newspaper.
And it may be added just here that The
Bulletin is proud of being that weekly
newspaper. It led the way in denouncing
the borrowing policy, the hush-it-up policy,
the policy of drift, the policy of being a
financial ostrich and burying this country’s
joint and several head in the sand. It was
the first paper to persistently declare that
the Australian wasn’t necessarily a poor,
feeble, helpless being unable togeton without
perpetual loans ; that he was as good a man
—if he only knew it—as the Dane, or the
Boer, or the Prussian, and as able to
pay his own way, and keep his national
finances clean; that this “ country of
magnificent natural resources ” could pay
for its own roads and bridges and court-
houses and asylums, and do its repairs to
public buildings, and buy crockery for the
Jenolan accommodation house, without pawn-
ing itself to Bull for the money; and that
a regular annual deficit, and a constant
cooking of the accounts, and an interminable
whining about our inability to pay taxes, and
a persistent lie to the effect that benevolent
asylums and dredging operations and the like
are “permanent and reproductive works,”
were not necessarily a part of the Australian
character. It was the first paper which
pointed out, in and out of season, that
a country with the biggest debt per head on
this earth was in a dangerous situation,
and that the perpetual increase of that
debt must mean national insolvency, just
as surely as the perpetual getting worse
of a man who is already ill must mean death.
It was the first paper to declare that the
helpless frame of mind induced by constant
dependence on loans was preparing the way
for repudiation. For ten years or more
The Bulletin has consistently advocated,
not the repudiation but the repayment of
our public debt, and it has stood almost
alone in holding the theory that the Aus-
tralian was as capable of financial indepen-
dence as the Frenchman, or the Yankee, or
the German. It doesn’t expect any grati-
tude from the great, helpless, borrowing gang
for all this.
• • » *
Now that the financial difficulty has
really arrived, there is nothing to be
gained by any further self-deception. It
isn’t Australia’s Old Age Pension system,
nor its State Railway policy, nor the Six
Hatters that is the cause of the trouble.
The United States has an Immigration
Law which is as stringent as ours and
much more harshly administered, and it has
a pension system which is, and has been for
many years, a vastly heavier burden than
ours ; yet, if the United States wanted
money, England would fall over itself in its
haste to lend hundreds of millions if it had
the money to lend. Germany, Denmark,
and other countries have State railways,
land banks, and as much of the parapher-
nalia of State socialism as we have; and if
they put their I O U on the London money
market, they would get all they are likely to
ask for six times over. The city of Glasgow
has carried municipal socialism to the point
at which it tries to establish the public
ownership of almost everything, and the
corporation of Glasgow can raise money at a
rate of interest vastly below anything that
would be looked at in Australia’s case.
The attempt to put down the trouble to
the policy of State socialism is just one
more lie, and one more attempt to shift the
responsibility from where it rightly belongs.
• • • •
The British money-lender doesn’t care
what is the policy of his debtor, so long as
he is wholly satisfied about the solvency of
his debtor. Nor does any other money-
lender. He lends for war purposes and peace
purposes ; he lends to Catholic, Protestant,
Mohammedan and Buddhist, to republican
and autocrat, all alike —if the security is
good. As to why he isn’t likely to lend to
Australia much longer the reasons are obvious
enough. Here are some of them :
He knows that Australia has, in proportion to popula-
tion, already the Biggest Debt on Earth, and he begins
to wonder how much debt per head a country can carry.
There must be a breaking point somewhere.
He sees that debt increasing faster than it ever in-
creased before, and increasing much faster than popula-
tion does.
He sees a smaller and smaller proportion of the new
loans being applied to reproductive works, and a larger
and larger proportion being applied to unproductive,
every-day purposes that should be a charge on revenue.
He sees the unproductive debt mounting up till even
the war debts of most of the States of Europe aren’t a
circumstance by comparison.
He has seen the scanty sinking funds that once existed
mostly borrowed, and, in the case of most of the States,
all serious attempt at repayment of the debt abandoned.
He knows that the Australian public lands, which are
often described as being a security for his claims, are
being used up as fast as they can be realised on for revenue
purposes, and he wonders how much worse things will be
when they are all used up.
He is perfectly well aware that the Australian railways,
though they don’t represent very much more than half
his claims,’ don’t even pay interest on their nominal
value; that in several cases they have been allowed to
fall into hideous disrepair through the policy of grabbing
every possible penny as revenue and cutting maintenance
expenses down to starvation point; that they are being
saddled with all kinds of charitable expenditure in the
way of starving stock concessions and the like, and are
being further starved to try to make them cover these
extra charges ; and that a vast amount of the rolling-
stock which is supposed to represent his loan money has
long since gone to the scrap heap.
He hears the most railwayed country (in proportion to
population) in the world clamoring for thousands of miles
of new railway, for all of which he is expected to find the
money, and at the same time some existing lines are
being closed because they hardly pay axle-grease. And,
after they are abandoned, they are still quoted to him as
a permanent and reproductive security.
And he hears silly politicians declare that the Australian
railways, which don’t anything like pay interest on the
£130,000,000 they cost, would easily sell for enough to
cover about £210,000,000 of debt, and he begins to wonder
if he has been lending his money to a community in its
right mind.
• • * •
And even these things are not all.
He knows perfectly well that Australia, even after
using its land receipts as revenue and charging a vast
amount of its ordinary expenditure to loans, has, as a
whole, had about 15 deficits in the last 20 years, yet there
is hardly any serious attempt to make ends meet.
He has seen, within the last few weeks, Victoria annex
Turner’s sinking fund and the railway accident fund for
revenue purposes rather than impose a general land tax.
Also he has seen Queensland put a £600,000 deficiency loan
on the market rather than impose a land tax.
He heard, a few days ago, that N.S.W. had accumulated
another million deficit in seven months, and he learned
that not only was the overdraft sufficient to swallow up all
-the available trust funds, but-that the State had bank
overdrafts as well.
He couldn’t but hear Owe’Sulliyan stating proudly
that it was his huge borrowing policy that was keeping
the State going, ana he naturally prefers to lend to some
country that can keep going without loans. Also ne pro-
bably thinks that the Owb’Sullivan’b words sound very
like a warning that when the loans stop N.S.W. won t
keep going any longer. ,
He sees, even in this time of stress, money being scat-
tered in subsidies to ox-shows and grants to schools ot
arts for the purchase of yellow-backed novels. Probata}
he even hears afar-off draper Law clamoring for a grant
for the Balmain Choral Union. Also he may have seen
the plans for “ the most magnificent railway station in
the world.” . ,
He must have heard, over and over again, the proua
boast that this is “ one of the most lightly-taxed countnes
in the world.” And he probably begins to wonder if a
country which is so proud of not paying enough taxes to
keep itself solvent is a desirable debtor.
And he possibly begins to wonder why a country
whose “ magnificent natural resources” are dinned into
his ear with such sickening iteration, can’t get along
without five or six new loans every year, and must use
its land receipts as revenue, and pay a lot of its ordinary
expenses out of borrowed money as well, and annex its
trust funds and sinking funds, and have a chronic deficit
to boot —a deficit that seems to come along in good and
bad years just the same. And he perhaps begins to ask
himself if these stupendous “ resources” are only a con-
fidence trick, after all.
And, amid all the rest, he hears the voice of George
Reid promising, if he gets into power, to reduce taxation,
and make the things that are already bad a vast amount
worse.
These are a few, at all events, of the
reasons for the present difficulty ; and all
the blustering of tenth-rate politicians
about the iniquity of those who refuse to
join in the hush-it-up policy won’t shift one
of them by so much as an inch. There is no
easy remedy for the trouble. It is the result
of 30 years of steadily increasing folly, of
silly braying about imaginary riches, of
refusal to pay sufficient taxes to keep the coun-
try solvent, of insane extravagance and incom-
petence. The only honest way back now is
the long, hard road uphill. If the Australian
Government will take over the existing State
debts (also if the silly, parochial vanity of
the State Governments will let it do so) and
pledge itself (the State Governments joining
in the pledge) to borrow no more till they
are paid oft', and will establish even a small
sinking fund for their repayment, it can
probably renew the old loans even now as
they fall due, at such a reduction of
interest as will itself more than cover the
sinking fund. After that it will remain
for the States to cut off every item of need-
less expenditure (the hospital subsidy and
the Old Age Pension vote are not needless
expenditure and are not included) ; to sack
their Agents-General ; to reduce their Legis-
latures to one House each ; to put up with a
plain Lieutenant-Governor each at £lOOO a
year , to suppress the blatant draper who
wants £75 for the Balmain Choral Union ;
to make paper money do to a great extent
in place of gold, and apply the gold in re-
duction of our outside debts ; to manufacture
more and import less , to tax the big land-
lords of Victoria, Queensland, Tasmania,
and Westralia ; to levy a heavy impost on
the absentee , to promote settlement by
cutting up big estates ; to make the country
districts pay for their own roads and cul-
verts ; and by these and other devices for
the six States to find about £7,000,000 a year
out of revenue for public works purposes.
Such a course as that will “ restore con-
fidence.” All the anger of John See and the
indignant remonstrances of Henry Copeland
won’t. But the habits of helplessness and
dependence which the loan policy has estab-
lished make it very improbable that Aus-
tralia will face the uphill track now.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Et Tu, Brute.
“F.M.” writes from Hobart:—
The Bulletin’s defence of the outgoing Tasmanian
Government comes as a surprise and a shock, and to the
few men in Tasmania who are working zealously and un-
selfishly to help on a better day, as a staggering dis-
couragement and affront. One does not easily suspect
The Bulletin of misinformation, or suspect it of dis-
figuring bias ; but this untimely championing of an un-
speakable gang of shoddy monopolists and shufflers is
inexplicable. Tasmania’s only real hope is in progressive
policy—and The Bulletin turns sharply to smack the
progressives in the face! Surely it cannot be difficult,
even at the distance of Sydney, to learn the facts.
Lewis and his discredited associates are, to put it most
mildly, irreclaimable upholders of re-action. Lewis :
wooden Jingo and Tory, and elephantine dilettant;
Mulcahy : renegade democrat, a frenzied egotist, a wasp,
wrongheaded ; Bird : champion political turncoat of the
Australias, ex parson, ex-everything ; Collins : sawdust
nonentity. Their talk of democracy—they don’t talk of
it more than befits respectable Vandemonians—is the
flimsiest farce. All through, they have been zealous
battlers for the privileges of the Fatman, as against the
rights of the mere perspiring people. The record of Lewis
and co. is a long-drawn sordid tale of tinkering inepti-
tude. Whenever they have dared to introduce any
measure vaguely democratic it has been because they
knew that the Legislative Council would surely butcher
It.
The Bulletin hails the present income-tax as Lewis’s
honest attempt to grapple witha serious financial d ifficulty.
But the Lewis Government is not primarily responsible
for the income-tax. Bird’s proposal was for a tax based
arbitrarily on rent or annual assessment.
To represent the Opposition as unwilling to face
direct taxation is to go very far in misrepresenta-
tion. Opposition members right through even the
limpest of them have demanded direct taxation.
It was only beneath the whip of the Opposition
that Ministers grudgingly introduced this present tax.
But Opposition members said that as a preliminary to
necessary direct taxation, there must be reasonable' re-
trenchment and lopping-off of superfluous State depart-
ments and officials. They said, or some of them said,
that the land-tax was execrably low, and that (in
especial) a big crowd of monopolist absentees should be
dealt with immediately, and as savagely as might be.
They said that the influence of the Legislative Council was
so notoriously damnable, that during the whole life of the
Ministry only the most essential routine legislation had
been allowed to become law, and that it was fully time that
the House ceased from the supine idiocy of meekness that
billed the House of Dry Bones as a place whence no ill
thing could issue.
In short the Opposition made just the demands that
must have been inevitable in the circumstances m any
awakening democracy ; and this Government occupies
exactly the position that plutocratic and conservative
Governments have wallowed in since ever the world was.
And The Bulletin embraces this squalid Lewis Ministrv
as the only potential saviour Of Tasmania !
The Opposition members “ said or some of
them said ” is the one lapse into candor of this bit
of special pleading. If the Opposition, as a body,
had the policy which “ F.M.” alleges, The Bul-
letin would range itself with the Opposition. But
the progressive element in it is so hopelessly small,
that it won’t be the crowd in power if Lewis goes
out, and the crowd that will oe in power looks a
lot worse than Lewis and co. The progressives
are against Lewis, and with some reason. But
they can’t form a Ministry, and they will prob-
ably have more reason to be against Lewis’s suc-
cessors. The “retrenchment” gag of Lewis’s
opponents is a disgraceful farce. Tasmania is so
cheaply-governed now that very little retrench-
ment is possible. The island had a lot of taxation
taken off —taxation that it couldn’t spare—by the
abolition of inter-State customs, and the demand
that the loss should be made good by shameful
sweating (called “ retrenchment ”), instead of by
new taxation, is a disgrace to \democracy—
if any real democrat is behind that wicked
demand. As to the Opposition being in favor
of direct taxation a large section, at all
events, of the Opposition press persistently
demands sweating instead, and the anti-LEWis
“ Reform ” league goes blind for the abolition
of the income-tax ; and no body of Oppositionists
large enough to form a Ministry demanded an
increased land-tax or put forward any promising
financial policy whatever. Of course, every
Opposition says that the good measures of the
Government were “wrung from it” by the said
Opposition ; but that tale is getting to be too
ancient. When an Opposition is strong enough
to dictate a policy to the Ministry it forms a
Ministry itself. The Bulletin doesn’t like Lewis
much, but it regards him as the less of two evils.
And it declines to have anything to do with the
gang of sweaters who are now out on the “re-
trenchment ” game in a State which is already so
cheaply governed that very little further retrench-
ment is possible.

Vindicated.
This is Geobge Reid’s very latest
Mr. Reid says that it is well enough known by this time
that he had to take up the heavy burden which the
Government to which Mr. Lyne belonged was no longer
able to carry. Even Mr. Lynb’s own financial committee
admitted that he (Mr. Reid) did not claim as much of a
surplus as he was entitled to.
It seems really probable that if (impossible
event) George Reid were sentenced to two
years hard labor he would cheerfully allude
to his trial and conviction, in after days,
as an occasion on which he had a gold medal
conferred upon him by an admiring community.
Reid ran up against three financial committees in
all. The first he appointed himself, early in his
career, to report on the absolute accuracy and
honesty of his bogus surplus, and hisown committee
flatly declined to say that the surplus was either
honest or accurate. The blushless Reid presented
that damning document to N.S. W.' /ssembly,
and spoke of it as a complete vindication of his
character, which was probably the most magnifi-
cent sample of cheek even that Assembly had
ever listened to. The next committee came along
some years later, when Lyne was Premier. Reid
was allowed to name two members of it, and
Lyne himself selected the third. That committee
didn't say any nice things about Reid as he
brassily suggests above; in fact, its report \va>
such a scathing denunciation of Reid’s career as a
gjlitical account faker, that in a better-class
arliament it would probably have led to his
prompt retirement. Even the rhiuocerous-hidtd
George felt that report so badly that he appointed
a little committee of accountants all by himself,
and paid it out of his own pocket, to go over the
accounts again. And even his own hired com-
mittee declined to say definitely that his political
finance was clean ; all it would say was something
to the effect that if the matter was looked at from
Reid’s own point of view things were all right.
A financier has not exactly the right to put on Dry
Dog and hauteur when he appoints two committees
and selects the majority of a third committee, and
can’t get a certificate of political honesty from any
of them. When he proudly quotes these disasters
(when people have had time to forget the details)
as proofs of his splendid character, the situation
becomes more comic than the fat old gentleman’s
best joke ever was, even when that joke was new.

The Victorian Reform Position.
As The Bulletin went to press, Victoria was
waiting for Premier Irvine to take up a very
“strong” attitude towards the Upper House. It
was not waiting very hopefully, because the
feeling is growing down south that Mr. Irvine is a
strong man only when the task in hand is to bully a
pioor laborer. However, Mr. Irvine has the chance
now of vindicating himself, or justifying his critics.
The House of Landlords has chewed over his
Reform Bill, rejected from it all that is valuable,
and contemptously spat back at him the husk. It
has added insult to hostility to the popular House
by allowing to stand the provisions for the reduc-
tion of the Assembly in numbers and power, as an
impertinent intimation that it has no objection to
that House diminishing its own importance, but
that the almighty House of Landlords must not be
lightly interfered with. A straight-out rejection
of the whole measure would have put the
Council in a more dignified position. As it
is, the remnant of the Bill cast at Premier
Irvine’s feet is in no sense a “Reform Bill.”
It is not “ reform ” to provide for a huge
reduction of the Assembly, for a very small reduc-
tion of the membership of the Council, and to
grant to the latter body the power to “make
suggestions ” (almost equivalent to making amend-
ments) in money Bills. Mr. Irvine proposed, as a
concession to Tory reaction, to give the Council
power to make suggestions once in regard to money
Bills; the Council struck out the word ‘ ‘once, ” and
by arrogating to itself the right to make an un-
limited number of suggestions, and to make them
over and over again, practically demanded the
right of amendment. That is a right which the
House of Lords in England does not claim, and
which no Australian Upper House outside Victoria
claims. Yet the grant of it to the Victorian
House of Landlords is now the marrow of the so-
called Reform Bill, for the provisions with regard
to the reduction of members are really not im-
portant in any constitutional sense. The
principal things struck out of the Reform
Bill are the liberalising of the suffrage for
the Upper House so that every ratepayer should
have a vote; the broadening of the qualification
for membership of the Upper House (at present
the Victorian Upper House forbids about 95 per
cent, of its constituents the right of election to its
ranks, so the created House poses as being greater
than its creators and reserves the privilege of its
membership to a small ring of extra-rich property
owners); the provision for a dissolution of both
Houses of Parliament upon the passing of the new
franchise law ; the settlement of disputes between
the two Houses of Parliament by a joint sitting;
and the granting of the franchise to women. As
put forward by Mr. Irvine, the Reform Bill was ; a
very moderate measure indeed, and disfigured by
several concessions to re-action—the weak propo-
sals of a weak man. As returned to him,
it has had all the reform rejected and the conces-
sions to Toryism increased. And the question of


the day is: What does Irvine intend to do?
Present indications are that he will ask for a disso-
lution of the Assembly, and leave the question as
to his ultimate attitude unanswered until that
useless excuse for delay has been exploited. There
is no reason why the Assembly electors should be
asked again whether they wish for Reform or not;
they have already said so emphatically, and their
saying so again will not advance matters one inch
towards a settlement. A strong man would say
at once what he intended to do to the Council,
and would then do it. There are several ways by
which that insolent mass of gilded tallow can
be swept out of the path which it obstructs. One,
before suggested by this paper, would be to buy
it off (it is such a sordid, huckstering, unpatriotic
body that this would almost certainly serve, if the
price were made high enough, and hardly any
price would be too dear for the benefit). Another
would be to tack on to the next Appropriation
Bill a genuine reform measure, and insist that the
Council should pass it without amendment, or take
the responsibility of stopping the business of the
country and being ultimately rioted into obedi-
ence ; yet another possible plan to a strong man
would be to call a popular convention and ask it
to abolish the Upper House, and thenceforth
treat that House as abolished, and abolish
all officials who didn’t choose to honor the
authority of the single House. There is no such
thing as an irresistible obstacle in politics.
Before this, in a superstitious age, the apparently
hopeless barrier of a “Divine-right” king has
been swept away ; and, beside that incident, the
removal of a mere mass of stupid, rich, fat
humanity is an easy contract—but not for a man
of the Irvine stamp. The Bulletin predicts,
with some certainty, that he will face the present
situation by doing nothing special in a firm and
masterly fashion, and with frequent assurances
that when he says a thing he says it, and that
when his foot is down it isn’t up, and so on.
Say Labor.
It is necessary for democrats to define their
attitude in regard to the “ day labor ’’controversy.
No class of workers has any claim to “loaf” on
the State by drawing wages which they do not
legitimately earn. The State must not “sweat”
its employees, by giving them less than a fair
wage. These are the two principles by which a
reasonable decision on the subject should be
bounded. There is no duty on the part of the
State to tolerate any “government-stroke” or
“ca-canny” tactics on the part of the men it
employs, whether laborers or lawyers ; there is a
very clear duty not to exact, from the necessities
of the poor, labor at an unfair rate. The Freetrade
contention that the Government should, in its labor
relations, follow the Freetrade theory and “buy in
the cheapest market” needs only to be examined to
be scouted, for its logical conclusion would be the
employment of cheap Coolie labor from Asia in
all manual work. On the other hand, the idea
put forward, more or less clearly, in some inju-
dicious labor quarters that the Government must
be a sort of “ boss that never sacks,” willing to
tolerate anything in the way of bad service, must
be just as clearly rejected. The “day labor”
system is, with proper management, undoubtedly
advantageous. It saves the Government the
middle-man’s profit, it prevents "scamping” of
work, and it gives a guarantee that there will be no
sweating. In practical working its value has been
repeatedly proved : and it is by no means confined
to Government works, but has been pretty
generally adopted for large private enter-
prises. The Railway Commissioners in N.S. W.
have carried out many extensive works with-
out the intervention of the contractor and, almost
invariably, with particularly good results both as
regards economy and quality of workmanship.
But, with all its virtues, the system is extremely
liable to abuse—particularly in the hands of a
Government swayed by the desire to placate
opponents or satisfy supporters. Wasting “ Go-
vernment money” is in almost all quarters looked
upon as a venial sin ; and waste is very easy on
large day-labor works by the employment of un-
skilful workmen or by allowing skilled ones to
“ loaf ”at their tasks. There has been a good
deal of this, too. in N.S.W., and on some Govern-
ment works employees, it is notorious, have made
laziness a practice, devoting much misspent in-
genuity to devising means of wasting time.
The ultimate result is that in some cases
the benefits to be expected from direct em-
ployment of the workers required have been
lost, in others there has been so much
waste that it would have been, on the whole,
better if the work had been carried out by the
contractor. For the success of the day-labor
system there must be adequate skilful super-
vision and no political interference to save the
incompetent or lazy from dismissal. And these
conditions ought to be easy enough to fulfil. It
would be a humiliating confession to have to
make that we, as a community, were not honest
enough for a day-labor system.

The Federal Yes-No Attitude.
BURNIE (Tas.), Wednesday.
At to-night’s meeting Mr, Barton read telegrams that
had passed between Mr. John Henry, of Devonport, and
Mr. G. H. Reid, on the subject of the policy of the Free-
trade party in respect to the imposition of direct taxation
for Common wealth purposes.
Mr. Henry wired to Mr. Reid asking if it was true that
the Freetrade party had declared that their policy was
to reduce the existing customs revenue and impose direct
taxation. Mr. Reid replied, “ Mr. Barton's statement is
absolutely without foundation. We repudiate direct
taxation of the Commonwealth, and always have. Under
a genuine revenue tariff direct taxation is less likely than
under protective duties destroying revenue."
Daily paper.
The above is the nearest Mr. Yes-No Reid has
yet got to defining his Federal fiscal position—
that is to say, to defining what is to be his fiscal
position should he ever, by evil luck and gruesome
ill-fortune, become Premierof the Commonwealth.
As a Freetrade position it doesn’t look up to
much; as a Democratic position it looks up to
much less. Here is what the country has to look
forward to under George Reid’s Premiership of
Australia, should that disaster ever come to pass :
0) There is to be no absentee tax, for that comes under
the head of direct taxation, and the Dry Dog gentleman
absolutely repudiates direct Federal taxation.
(2) There is to be no graduated land-tax directed to
the bursting up of big estates. In such States as Victoria
and Tasmania, where an immovable Upper House is
solidly entrenched behind a property franchise, it is imi
possible to get from the State Legislature a graduated-
land-tax, devised for the express purpose of bursting up
big estates. It would be as reasonable to look for feathers
on a lion as for democratic legislation of that kind from
such a quarter. There is the same trouble in Queens-
land and Westralia, and, in a less degree, in N.S.W. The
only hope of democracy is that the Commonwealth Par-
liament will move in the matter. George Reid assures
the public that, if he is Premier, the Commonwealth Par-
liament will be as immovable as Captain Cook’s statue.
(3) Seeing that there is to be no Federal direct taxation
under George Reid’s Premiership it is evident that, if
Australia is to be kept reasonably solvent, there must be
as much money screwed out of the people by customs
and excise under the Reid Freetrade regime as under the
Barton Protectionist regime. George Reid has assured
the public 1000 times over that all customs taxation is
paid by the consumer, that it is a most costly and most
w * ay of / aism S revenue, and that for every

A Labor Covenant for Land.
Years ago, when the Tory party nodded, the
principle came to be accepted that the man who
owns a mine must work that mine or make way
for somebody else who \Vill. This was merely the
adoption of the communistic doctrine that refusing
to surrender things not being used is equally a
crime with taking things which are being used,
though probably the framers of the labor covenant
were unaware of that fact. The principle was
recognised and has prevailed to the present day,
even the most reactionary of Australian legislators
never daring to question its right. But while
the principle universally governs the search for
mineral wealth, not a single suggestion has been
made that it should apply equally to the much
more profitable and necessary industry of working
the land for food and clothing and habitation.
The man who holds a mineral lease, that he
deems valuable, has, at any to make a pre-
tence of seeking for the wealth that is concealed.
The man who holds a block of land the value of
which is manifest even to the casual street-bred
visitor may hold it for a rise for all eternity, or
may graze a few sheep upon an area that would pro-
vide a living for 10,000 people. Failing nationalisa-
tion of the land, which is a large order, the adop-
tion of a labor covenant scientifically arranged
according to the value of the soil is the fairest
and most easy method of performing the work
that comes (nominally) foremost in the programme
of every Australian Ministry— “ settling the
people on the land.” There is no difficulty about
getting the people there. The trouble is to find
the land for them, and it is the endeavor to
do this which led Victoria to open up inac-
cessible precipices in Gippsland and the desert
stretches in the Mallee which are now about
to cost millions in railways and waterworks.
The whole agricultural population of Vic-
toria might easily have been settled in a small
corner of the Western district, where their
productive power would have been far greater,
and their markets more easily reached—had it not
been for the fact that the Western district is
owned by half-a-dozen Fat Families, who are
satisfied to raise sheep and play polo, pending the
sale of their property at fancy prices. A Labor cove-
nant would end all this. The Vic. western district
sq uatter who pastured 1000 sheep on land capable of
producing 1000 tons of potatoes would be instructed
to comply with the conditions attached to the pos-
session of first-class land, and, failing to do so, his
property would be liable to resumption. This he
would never permit. In place of holding for a
rise, he would either rush his property into the
market in small holdings or work it himself, in
which case he would provide a more comfortable
living for his employes than they would get at
present in the Mallee. His neighbor, who held
third-class land, wholly unused, would be obliged
to pass it over to sheep, and his brother, who was
hanging on to an allotment in Collingwood, would
have to hurry off to an architect to get a
house erected on it. The prosperity of a
nation is wholly dependent upon its productive
power, and the loaf of bread is, all over the
world, the first medium of exchange. The poli-
ticians want to “ settle people on the land ” ; the
people are available, and ready and anxious to be
settled ; and the land is there, only the owner
won’t let people settle on it. An enactment that
so many people must be settled on so many acres,
according to the carrying capacity of the land,
would remove the only obstacle in the way.

Mr. Prynnge, the Politician.
The Australian Parliament is out of session, but
there will evidently be no lack of political discus-
sion whilst the N.S. W. Supreme Court is sitting.
Last week, Mr. Prynnge —forgetting altogether
his character as Justice Pring —had another
outbreak of strong political partisanship on the
Bench. The matter before him as a Judge was a
clause of the Commonwealth Excise Act:
In every excise prosecution, the averment of the prose-
cutor or plaintiff contained in information shall be
deemed to be proved in the absence of proof to the con-
trary.
A conviction under this clause was the subject of
appeal because of the objection that the Legisla-
ture could hardly have meant the word “proof”
to be taken in its absolute sense, but rather in-
tended that it should be read as “ evidence,” or as
“satisfactory evidence”; otherwise a familiar
common-law principle that a man cannot be held
guilty because he cannot absolutely prove his
innocence would be violated. The point was a good
one. Probably the Australian Parliament meant to
enact that if a man, for instance, were found in
possession of an illicit still, that would be evidence
against him of guilt—just as the possession of
stolen goods would be—and would be sufficient to
convict upon, unless he could bring evidence to
show that his holding of the still was an innocent
one. The Judges on the Bench had a perfect
right, of course, to comment on the wording of
this clause, and to blame Parliament for the loose
way in which it was drawn. But that didn’t
satisfy Justice Pring ; he must cast off his judicial
robes and plunge into a general political con-
troversy. To quote from a newspaper report :
Mr. Justice Pring agreed that the section was an in-
novation into English law, and was repugnant to his
mind. Incidentally, he referred to another equally re-
pugnant piece of Commonwealth legislation—namely, a
provision in the Immigration Act that, unless an intend-
ing immigrant could speak 50 words in Russian, though
he might be able to speak English, he would be a pro-
hibited immigrant.
Mr. Prynnge in this statement aired an opinion
which was utterly out of place, and which was pro-
bably founded on a dense ignorance of the subject.
The provision of the Australian Immigration Act
referred to (in a muddled sort of way) was
copied from the Natal Act ; was approved
by the British Government when passed by the
Natal Legislature—in fact, the adoption of the
existing clause, instead of a clause excluding un-
desirable colored races, was forced on Australia by
British dictation ; and was again fully approved
when enacted by the Australian Parliament. Even
if that were not so, if it were entirely without pre-
cedent and utterly opposed to British notions,
it is the law, and Justice Pring’s duty is
to administer and respect the law, whatever
Mr. Prynnge’s views on the subject may happen
to be. If from a deep personal conviction that
the law is unjust, Mr. Prynnge feels that hecannot
conscientiously uphold it as Justice Pring,
resignation from the Bench provides an easy
remedy. Then, in his private capacity, Mr.
Prynnge can speak as freely as he likes against
the law, and, if he chooses, seek to enter Parlia-
ment and have it changed. But while he is on the
Bench he must keep his views to himself ; his
incursions into the region of party politics from
the Bench have become an intolerable scandal.

The Victorian Report.
A very remarkable docu..,eiit indeed is the report
of the Victorian Royal Commission on Factories
and Shops Law. This Commission was appointed
to inquire, in a calm, judicial fashion, into the
merits of the Factories legislation which came
into force under the democratic regime of George
Turner, and it was hoped in many quarters
that it would discredit, if not wholly damn,
Turner’s enactments. That at any rate was the
expectation of the Tories. The Victorian House
of Landlords has been constantly deferring con-
sideration of shops and factories matters * ‘until the
Royal Commission had reported,” looking forward
evidently to that report as a bulwark against labor
demands. If so, the disappointment of Balak,
the king who employed Balaam for a cursing con-
tract on a certain historic occasion, pales into
insignificance, beside the chagrin that the Vic-
torian House of Landlords must feel at the tenor
of the report to which it had been looking for-
ward so eagerly. Mr. A. R. Outtrim, as chair-
man, puts his signature to a document which
recommends a most prudent and admirable plat-
form of reform legislation m a form as concise
as it is clear and convincing. The commission was
not very favorably impressed with the cumbrous
laws which in Victoria strive at the suppression
of sweating, and therefore suggests the abolition
of the Wages Board and the substitution (1) of
courts of conciliation and arbitration—the latter
somewhat on the N.S.W. plan—with power to fix
rates of wages ; (2) of a compulsory apprenticeship
system, _ with safeguards against 4 4 sweating ”
apprentices ; (3) of an Early Closing Act on the
N.S.W. plan; (4) of the strict limitation of
Asiatic labor (to a total of 300 in the furniture
trade and of 150 in the laundry trade). It is a
splendid programme, and its adoption would
place Victoria again in the forefronc of the States
as regards industrial legislation—a position which
it held, or almost held, for a brief period in
Turner’s time, but speedily lost again. But it
must be a bitter pill to Balak.

44 Kiwi ” from his ricketty islands :
The death of William Rolleston, one of the old school
of politicians who might have achieved great things had
he had the courage to emancipate himself from the social
tyranny of his class, has given another excuse for opening
the floodgates of nauseating eulogy. Anyone with
ordinary observation who has studied the stereotyped
obituaries of public men, could have written leading
articles on Rolleston before-hand. One phrase, “ There
were gianis in those days,” alluding to the trood old times
when politicians wept for the public good night and day,
has become almost as time-worn as “ butchered to make
a Roman holiday,” which Mark Twain recoiled from in
his “ Innocents Abroad.” The fact is, newspapers
maunder about the good times that were and the
decadence of modern politicians, pretty much as the old
ladies prattle about the times when they were young—
when girls didn’t walk with sweethearts, and didn’t let
anybody kiss ’em, and wouldn’t dream of marrying
without the consent of mama. I happen to have
known poor old Rolleston very well. He was a man
of rare culture certainly, and a Liberal at heart. In the
early days he became terribly unpopular with the
arrogant Canterbury squatters, because of his sympathy
with the small settlers and his efforts to encourage close
settlement, and the odium of the squatters scared him
into “ respectable ” moderation. Still Rolleston had a
fixed hankering in favor of enlightened legislation, and
when he became Minister of Lands he gave us our per-
petual leasing system, but whatever good he achieved
through this was spoiled by G. F. Richardson, who
followed him in the portfolio of Lands. Richardson got
legislation through permitting the holders of perpetual
leases to purchase the fee-simple, and has thereby earned
the undying ingratitude of every lover of Maoriland.
Rolleston bitterly resented Richardson’s work ; but
both were of the same party, and Rolleston was too
good a party man to be of much use as a reformer. To
the last the old man was deservedly respected, but his
long public career must, on the whole, be counted a
failure. He was socially averse from the class of men
who comprised Ballance’s Liberal party, and though he
was politically at variance with his own party, social ties
were too strong, and he who might have become a leader
of men remained a mere ornamental lieutenant of the
selfish coterie who want to keep Maoriland a land of
squatters and sheep. Rolleston was, to some extent,
the author of the Maoriland education system. In the
da}'S of the provinces he was Superintendent of Canter-
bury, and had great fights with his advisory executive,
practically suspending it an I the constitution. He secured
a public library for the province, was guardian angel of
the museum (now the best in Maoriland) and was a force
on the Senate of the University. At the general election of
1899 he was beaten by one vote, and he was too old and
tired to demand a recount. So William Rolleston
passed out, and his obsolete Gladstonian collar was seen
in politics no more.
