About the most splendid tiling in the way of
impudence that Sydney remembers was the proud
uprising, last Sunday, of the local Faith-Healing
parson to point out that the acquittal of John-
ston (charged with slaying his wife by failing to
provide her with medical attendance) was a
glorious triumph for the Faith-Healing per-
suasion. It was especially cheeky that
Parson M'Cullagh should take for his
text the words : “They shall lay hands on
the sick and they shall recover.” Parson
M'Cullagh and his sect laid hands on Mrs.
JoHnston, and she didn’t recover. They faith-
healed her till she died. But she was strong in her
belief, and refused the medical assistance that
might have saved her. Her husband was
promptly acquitted of the ridiculous charge of
murder brought against him—partly because his
wife refused medical aid on her own responsibility,
and her blind trust in the ridiculous M'Cullagh
was her own fault; partly because, when the
crisis came, Johnston himself threw up the
M'Cullagh superstition and wanted to have
medical aid called in. If the patient had been an
irresponsible child instead of a responsible adult,
and Johnston had allowed it to die without
medical aid according to the teachings of
the M'Cullagh clan, then he would have deserved
to be treated either as a murderer or as some
speoies of mild but dangerous lunatic. When the
humorless M ‘Cullagh, after such a failure,
briskly elevates his tail and preaches from the
text, “They shall lay hands on the sick
and they shall recover he is a sorrowful example
of the witlessness of the sects. But a sect
that can seriously believe in Preacher Dowie
as its head and boss apostle— Dowie who says he
is the resuscitated prophet Elijah, and who had
a pair of purple wings made so that he might
hook them on his back and flap them wildly during
service as if he was an enlarged chookey fowl—is
capable of almost anything.
*
George Reid is practically the only Australian
politician who does his political somersaults in
public and is proud of the fact. His recent
assurance that his “Freetrade” party intends to
rely wholly on the hated and much-denounced
tariff as a source of revenue, moved Premier
Barton to quote some of his previous speeches on
the same subject. And here, in a Sydney evening
paper, is the Fatted Wriggler wanting to know
what in thunder truth or consistency or political
decency has to do with the case:
Mr. G. H. Reid is rather disappointed that Mr. Barton
has not accepted his assurance that the party he leads
does not favor direct taxation for Commonwealth pur-
poses. Mr. Barton has a strong recollection of Mr, Reid
saying in the Convention that he rather liked the Braddon
clause, because its restrictions applied only to customs
and excise, and if he were Commonwealth Treasurer he
would not be afraid to resort to direct taxation. ‘‘Sup-
posing 1 did say it then," Mr. Reid remarked, “ what has
that to do with a definite political state of things, and a
party with a definite policy ? I have laid down the policy
of the party in this way : We take the same view as the
Government that the Federal Parliament should confine
itself to taxation through the Customs House.”
Exactly! Even if G. Reid declared his perfect
readiness to resort to Federal direct taxation and
his entire aversion to customs duties, what have
these things to do with his present assurance that
he won’t have direct taxation at any price, and
that he will rely wholly on customs duties? What
has G. Reid’s “Yes” to do with his “No,” or his
“No” with his “Yes,” or either of them with his
“Yes-No?” Also, what has his left leg to do
with his right, or his Dry Dog with the Sydney
water-supply ?
Current term of N.S.W. Supreme Courtis pro-
viding much good copy to the newspapers, and
Justice G. B. Simpson, in the Central Criminal
Court, is getting as many special paragraphs as
even he could wish for. The Johnston case—an
Elijah Dowie dupe being charged with murder
because his wife had died without having medical
attendance—gave Justice Simpson his first chance.
The prosecution was obviously absurd. The
accused man believed profoundly that his wife’s
illness was more likely to be cured by prayer than
by a doctor ; his wife had the same conviction.
The husband, however, was willing, even anxious,
that a doctor should be called in—on the
principle that it’s just as well to keep your
powder dry even though you trust in Providence ;
but his wife was firm in her faith and she died.
To charge the man with “cruelly and maliciously
murdering her ” was an idiotic action, though he
might have been held in some degree culpable for
not insisting that a doctor should be consulted.
The Crown Prosecutor, who happened to be Mr.
Pollock, Solicitor-general, was very roughly re-
buked by Justice Simpson for the absurd prosecu-
tion. Next day Mr. Pollock was in hot water
again, Justice Simpson denying hisright tofileabill
at all in place of the Attorney-General, and when
Mr. Pollock tried to argue the point the Judge
threatened to send him “somewhere.” “The
profession ” prophesied that there would be some
joy when it was announced that Mr. Pollock
whs to prosecute for the Crown at these assizes
(Pollock, outside of his official position, is re-
garded as “only a junior”), and there has been
joy. By the way, if, as Justice Simpson seems to
assert, the Solicitor-General cannot legally act as
a grand jury and find a true bill, what about
the legality of the prosecutions for the Auburn
murder ?
♦
Sydney Worker re-hashes the old theory of
“ cycles ” of depression to account for the threat-
ened crisis in Australia :
As a matter of hard and dry fact, our by-gone prog-
nostications of evil were based on a belief in the cycle or
vortex theory of commercial disturbances. These recur,
in all countries, once in every 10 years or so. The last
Australian crisis occurred in 1592-1894— the next must be
due now. And these periods return in this regular
fashion, not because—as Wilson imagines—of drought,
borrowing, impeded immigration, and high wages, but
simply because wages are not high enough. Just as soon
as production overtakes and passes consumption, because
wages have not increased in direct proportion to pro-
ductive power, from that moment the vortical motion
begins which ends in social smash and general destruc-
tion.
This, as applied to Australian conditions, is
simply jingling nonsense. It’s not over-pro-
duction that tends to produce crises here, but
under-production—our failure to produce sufficient
goods for export to pay for our imports and for
the interest on our borrowings, still less sufficient
to pay anything off our old debts.

After three years of straight-out Labor adminis-
tration the Broken Hill municipality has gone
back to the old ways. The Labor administration
was an interesting experiment, but, on the whole,
it cannot be said that it was a success. Perhaps
it did not have a fair chance ; anyhow, there was
a hole, and very early in its career the
party fell into it, losing the whole of a year’s
rates from the mines (about .£3500), besides
£7OOO in law. Apart from that, however, most
of the Labor aldermen had not learned that
to make day-labor a success there must be compe-
tent supervision, and that to get good supervision
good money must be offered. The party didn’t
realise these things because, no doubt, it is hard for
a man who has never himself earned more than £3
a week, to understand that anybody at all is
worth £lO or £2O a week. The Broken Hill party,
therefore, “sacked” the town clerk, and for the
latter half of its run also got along without
engineer or overseer; and the results make the
thoughtful members of the party sad.

The Tories of Victoria never weary of babbling of
the alleged truckling to the city workers—the
Trades Hall—by the Turner and Peacock Minis-
tries. Here are a few figures showing what these
allegedly Trades-Hall-dominated Governments
did for the land-owner by the Irrigation and
Waterworks Trusts Relief Acts of ’99—one of the
grossest acts of repudiation ever committed by any
body of men in the State. Three shires, five
boroughs, 32 waterworks trusts, and 27 irrigation
trusts, mostly in the North, had borrowed from
the State £2,500,000, and of this liability no
less than £1,050,000 was wiped out in one
act— i.e., transferred mostly to the shoulders of
the city workers and posterity. In the shameful
list of defaulters, some of whom had every penny
of their debt and interest remitted, the Kyabrama-
pootra’s home at Rodney comes easily first.
Rodney owed £212,099 principal and £52,720
interest, and was forgiven £147,099 principal and
all the interest. That same district, including
Rodney, Echuca, and Waranga, Fragowel Plains,

Perhaps the damp Sydney climate has had
a debilitating effect on “TheWrongMr. Wright”
—or perhaps the man who writes this notice is
older and more soured and less accessible to inno-
cent merriment than the one who discussed the
show on its Melbourne appearance. At any rate,
he finds it difficult to endorse the Melbourne
verdict that in this play Mr. Broadhurst has
equalled, even exceeded, the merit of previous
hilarious farces, such as “What Happened to
.Tones.” As staged at the Sydney Palace,
it appears to be the effort of a playwright with
That Tired Feeling, whose best comedy ideas had
been exhausted, and who was obliged, for lack of
inventive power, to use up again old situations
and old motives. Some of the characters are abso-
lutely colorless; in others the color is bor-
rowed from the author’s previous creations, and
put on rather thinly. There is, of course, a great
deal of fun. With all its faults, “The Wrong
Mr. Wright” is miles ahead of many of the
English and French farces, burlesques and extra-
vaganzas which find ready acceptance ; and there
are flashes of genuine humor as well as shriekful
situations. Still it does not seem that playwright
Broadhurst has maintained his previous high
standard of mirth, and he seems to frivol under
the greenwood tree of his imagination more
laboriously than aforetime.
• • * •
Most of the heat and burden of the piece
fall on George Willoughby, who, as Singleton
Sites (the wrong Mr. Wright), has practically the
only notably original character on the list. He
makes the best of it, forcing the pace at every
opportunity. The feminine broad comedy part
(Arabella Clingstone) is a clear copy from the
Lavinia of “What Happened to Jones”; Miss
Boland Watt-Phillips plays it after the best tra-
ditions of farce, and contributes a good deal to
the fun of the play. As Henrietta Oliver, a lady
detective, Miss Roxy Barton looks charming, and
acts in a semi-serious fashion which is rather
attractive. And Frank Denton, Miss Appleton,
Miss Ascoli, and Tom Cannam deserve special
praise for doing the best possible with parts that
are not too plump.
• • • •
“The Wrong Mr. Wright” is eked out
with a curtain-raiser, “ Counsel’s Opinion ” —a
bit of that sort of sentiment so dear to the
British heart. The rich, stern father who has cast
off his daughter because she married an actor (a
man with any sense would have overlooked such
an evanescent and purely temporary incident);
the actor who has died and left a struggling
widow an 1 a son (latter, in accordance with the
traditions of the theatrical profession, has been
burdened with the awesome names of “Maxi-
milian Arthur Morgan Lovelace ”); this son, who
is the Perfect Prig, softening grandfather’s heart
by tricks that would lead an ordinary man to pray
that Herod might come again and slaughter all
children—they are all there, and the gallery sniffs
and the stalls feel for their handkerchief, and a
generation which considers that Dickens reached
the supreme point of pathos in “Little Nell ” is
vastly pleased. The curtain-raiser is acted very
well, particularly by Mr. Cannam, as the heavy
grandfather, and Sydney Halliday, as the Lord
Faunfcleroy boy, whose father was an actor and
whose name is an outrage and who is the Perfect
Prig.
** * *
Last Saturday, Bland Holt staged a some-
what sad drama, called “ The Cotton King,” at
the Lyceum, and it. wasn't greatly brightened by
humor of an impossible kind, showing Mrs. Bland
Holt as a female Sandow, with alleged ability to
take Bland up in her teeth and throw him over
the fence. We don’t see Mrs. B. perform this
feat, otherwise there would be more excite-
ment. In Act I. we hear that Elsie Kent
is a good girl, and the idol of her “favther’s”
heart, upon which the experienced audience
understands at once that Elsie has been betrayed,
and that the villain will put the blame on Baker
very shortly, that gentleman being kept at the
Lyceum for the express purpose of being wrongly
accused on such occasions. Baker is a Yankee
without an accent and without much business cute-
ness, and his manner of offering to be a brother
to Elsie lacks enthusiasm. Meanwhile Styan
has falsified Baker’s cable message, and
brought him to financial ruin, besides which
he has incited the mill bands to attack Baker in
the interests of morality. However, with the
mismanagement that is characteristic of the lower
classes on the stage, they attack Styan instead,
whereupon Elsie Kent drops down at the end of
Act 11., and in Act 111. is understood to be in
“God’s Acre.” At this juncture Baker departs
to America to be cast by Styan into a
lunatic asylum from which he escapes by
means of a fire, arriving in time to rescue
Frances Ross from beneath some ton weight of
lift, which the villain brings down upon her. She
has overheard the villain's designs, and in the usual
foolish heroine manner informed him that she was
going to bring the police to him, instead of doing it
and saying nothing. However, this gives Frances
an opportunity for writhing beautifully beneath
the impending lift, and later on it is understood
that the clouds have burst asunder (they rolled
past in the last drama, and in the one before that
they cleared away), and that the fire that purifies,
as the programme puts it, has gone out at last.
* t • • *
Baker, as before mentioned, acts the
not over wise hero, while Styan, as a persevering
villain, keeps up to his usual level. Albert
Norman plays quietly and forcefully as a drunken
mill-hand, but Brightie Smith’s Lancashire accent
is an effort. Miss Frances Rutledge acts delicately
as the little deceived girl, while Harry Norman
dances lightly in the picnio scene though sup-
gised to have demolished fourteen pies. The
iss in the Ring episode possesses some irrelevant
interest; the Lift Scene excites enthusiasm,
though not so much as a red and blue electric
chair or some other more unusual method of exe-
cution ; and the Machinery Room of the Mill is
also good in a tame, prosaic way. • Also the real
rain that falls in Act I. descends in a natural, wet,
perpendicular manner, a gaudy section of rainbow
causing some irrelevant moral remarks later on.
m* * *
“Fun on the Bristol ” continues to make
a fair show at Sydney Criterion, where some
bright, new songs enliven the ancient, weather-
worn humor that attaches to widows who have
buried two husbands. The three legitimate objects
of humor in the female sex are the lady who has
not married at all, the one who has married too
much, and the one whose daughter has married.
The widow humor seems in the ascendant at
present.

The “Fortune-Teller” (unless the manage-
ment changed its mind after this went to press)
vanished from the boards of Sydney Royal last
Wednesday evening, and the “Chinese Honey-
moon ” trailed its gilded pigtail and weary mother-
in-law joke over the scene for one night only.
Then, if the advertisement was to be relied upon,
The Players took a hand on Friday ; and to-night
(Saturday), Nellie of the Turquoises reappears as
the frail but winsome fruit-girl who mashed a
king, but died none too well off. The lady who
mashes a monarch should always make the smitten
potentate purchase her an annuity in the A.M.P.
office straight off, and there is no class of the
population which has such an incentive to patro-
nise the Post Office Savings Bank as the best girls
of kings. For the average king lives right up to
his income; and when he finds a new girl, it takes
all his money to keep her, and if the previous girl
hasn’t saved money, then the lookout is dreary in
the extreme.
** * *
Ernest Toy’s concert at Sydney Centenary
Hall, Saturday night, revealed a boyish-looking
artist, capable of really brilliant work. When
Max Schluter was here he gave us every possible
variation of the shriek —the shriek reduced to a
fine art, to perfect music, with all its jagged edges
smoothed away. It fascinated while it did not
charm. The Bananaland player revels in sonorous
chords and rolling notes ; he pours out rich golden
melodies, standing motionless with closed eyes
and bent figure, utterly lost in the sounds he
creates. And he looks such a boy. Henry Weir
and Miss Maud Dalrymple gave valuable support
on Saturday night. Miss Dalrymple’s beautiful
presence would give valuable support to Israfel
and all the angel choir. She can also sing some.
But —all violin music is unsatisfying ; it leaves
an indefinable want; though Toy’s manipula-
tion of the unmasterable instrument does not
leave such a huge gap as most other people’s.

John Coleman had a hearty reception at
Sydney Tivoli last week, when he reappeared,
after a lengthy absence, in various song and dance
items, including the Scarecrow dance. Spry and
Monti are back at this theatre with a dialogue
entertainment which is superior to their previous
inane bar-room sketch ; and the Montroses have
returned to tumble with grace and precision.
Oftierwi.se the Tivoli programme is much as it
was. W. G. Hunter and Professor MacCann are
still two of its chief bulwarks. Interest in variety
business in Sydney is just now very languid, and
there is evident need for some fresh attractions.
m* * *
Veteran Howard Vernon, when last heard
of, was visiting the small towns of Soustralia,
with a small co., under disheartening conditions.
Next time Vernon strikes Sydney and Melbourne
the public, who cherish kind recollections of his
bygone Bunthorne and many another popular
character, would be glad to hear of a big compli-
mentary benefit —say two benefits—for their tall
old friend. Casting its eye back on a long series
of the Firm’s comic opera productions, in which
Howard Vernon “ created ” all sorts of characters
for Australia and stamped his own identity on
everyone of them. The Bulletin can think of no
worthier object for a ben. than the man who
played the old warrior in “Ma Mie Rosette.” In
many cases “Utility” Vernon was the most
successful member of the co., though he didn't
play a “ star ” part or draw a glittering salary.
His Lord Chancellor (“lolanthe”) came next to
BrOugh’s; his Majah General (“Pirates of
Penzance”) was the principal man on the job;
likewise his Koko, in the “Mikado.” And the
“Yeomen of the Guard,” unique among Gilbert
and Sullivan operas, owed much of its Australian
popularity to Vernon’s humorous, gruesome
picture of Wilfred, the jailor, who proposed to
entertain his bride on winter evenings with tooth-
some stories of the torture chamber. Then there
was the Grand Inquisitor, in “The Gondoliers.”
Has he no claim to a benefit ?

The jubilee of Melb. Philharmonic Society
was marked last week by an interesting social
reunion of members old and new. The venerable
Phil., it seems, was originated by the choristers of
Wesley Church, Melb., a little tabernacle which
stood on the site of the present Bank of Australasia
building in Collins-st. Two of the first soloists
of the infant society came up smiling on this
jubilee occasion—W. H. Williams, publisher of
the awful ’Awklet. and F. G. Smith, the
Williamstown town-clerk, who brought along his
diary recording the birth and christening of the
Phil. Armes P.caumont, S. Lamble, and Mrs. R.
S. Smvthe, soloists identified with its early years,
must have felt young again when Williams and
Smith, and Edwin Exon talked of the time before
it was established. Beaumont i a a mere boy in
vocalism compared with W.H.W. who gave ad vice
to the enterprising novice Smythe re his pioneer
venture in concert management. In the course
of this reminiscent evening George Peake, the
modern conductor of the Philharmonic Society,
announced a proposal to start its next half century
with a big oratorio performance in the Exhibition-
building.
** * *
“The Christian” came to pass at Melb.
Bijou on Saturday night, and this new setting of
the old trouble appeared to be taken seriously by
a patient congregation. Miss Maud Williamson's
drama is made comprehensible by a previous
and somewhat gloomier play, which traded on
the notoriety of Hall Caine s novel. The public
hold the key to the mystery. When John Storm
pops up in a long black gown they know that he
has escaped from the Holy Brotherhood of the
Gethsemane, because the previous drama threw a
dim religious light upon John’s sojourn among the
“ poor blokes at the monkery.” The omission of the
monkery scene in this case is only regrettable for
one reason. There was a husky, matter-of-fact
coster who brought vegetables to the back door of
the Gethsemane enclosure at early dawn, and
used to blow on his cold fingers whilst he talked
with Brother Paul concerning women and other
worldly things. The coster, with his sympathetic
irreverence, was a more impressive preacher than
Storm, for he taught the futility of holiness, and
reminded one that all men are but mere blokes
and consumers of ’taters from a barrow.
» • • •
Glory Quayle, who was always a giddy,
full-blooded deceiver, is, in Miss Williamson’s
play, rather more permanently giddy than of
yore. Also, friend John chases her in a more dis-
tinctly hypocritical spirit. John has gone into
business as a General Preserver of Young Girl,
and he takes no Christian interest in his fellow-
man, unless the masculine sinner happens to be
poaching on his (John’s) precious preserves.
John s furious anxiety about Girl terminates at
the point where Glory holds out her arms and
implores him to grasp his opportunity.. Then
he hurls himself into the haven of hug, and
Glory gives him a “ Zaza ” kiss—a kiss so tropical
that the curtain comes down. After his pleasing
lapse. John makes no further attempt to rescue
girls, but he continues his old habit of stoushing
the male species. The drama affords fleeting
glimspes of brother John on the stoush. He
smites the lower orders, and he biffs Lord Robert
Ure, his grievance in each case being due to a
spinster’s baby. Finally he receives a fatal stoush
himself, and dies upon a sofa, after having con-
verted his own auburn girl into a respectable
married woman. The drama doesn’t explain how
John comes to be hurt, but it seems probable that
the middle-classes rise against him in a body and
avenge the indignities which the “ lower orders”
and the long-suffering lordship have endured at
his hands.
* * m *
Miss Maud Williamson puts a lot of work
—light, medium, and strong—into her Glory
Quayle performance, and most of it is well done.
Alfred Woods makes John Storm a beauteous
ranter who shapes himself elegantly in his wrath,
and might easily be mistaken for one of the ewe
lambs he is dragging into the fold. A pair of
mild young men appear as the parties reponsible
for Glory’s stage career; also several other men
come forward to receive vituperation or physical
punishment from John the Smiter. The loudest
of these is Alf. Boothman. Miss May Hesford,
who graduated in amateur comic opera long ago,
turns out to be a capital Mrs. Jane Callender
with a tuneful Eloise Juno accent. “ The
Christian” is a fairly smart show, and quite
likely to prosper. It contains enough cant and
cuddle to satisfy any right-thinking parson.
* * * *
The two musical shows from Sydney fall
due in Melbourne next Saturday (28th). “The
Fortune Teller ” will be staged at the Princess’s,
with Madame Slapoffski and LemprierC Pringle
in the principal parts as before. At Her
Majesty’s, where silence has reigned for three
long weeks, the J.C.W. comic opera co. will
return for the immediate purpose of playing “The
Geisha.” The east thereof will be as it was in
Sydney. This is a time of year when a number of
red-faced men cross the Murray to attend some
races in Melbourne. The language of these rubi-
cund travellers when they find themselves con-
fronted by the shows they’d left behind them will
be very like the ejaculations of the jim-jammed
citizen who wonders whether he has got ’em again.
* * * *
Talking of the awful results of drink—it
seems that a grand temperance drammer entitled
“ Man’s Enemy ” is preparing for production at
Melbourne RoyaL Meanwhile, “ In the Shadow
of the Night ” supplies the weekly want. G. F.
Carey fires the petroleum store, and Willard tries
to roast him in his own furnace, as it happened on
the first night; but since then the conflagration
scene has improved the blazing hour by gathering
new details and effects. The latest effect is a
minor conflagration of Carey himself, whose
clothes are burning in two places when he gets
rescued. One of these places is just where he
couldn t possibly have caught fire if he had been
sitting down on a sheet of iron.
* * * *
Jacques Inaudi, the calculating
started to calculate at Melbourne Opera House
last Saturday. Possibly he is a wonderful person,
although they have set up a boy calculator at the
rival variety show, and the boy rival undertakes
to reveal how lightning calculation is done.
Whether wonderful or otherwise, Jacques is cer-
tainly a bore. He dances not, neither does he
sing. He stands upon a plank and arrives at
arithmetical results by intuition, or something.
As a freak of brainwork his performance is less
interesting than that of the men who can photo-
graph a whole sheet of daily newspaper upon their
memories. The bill at Rickards’ will be changed
considerably on Saturday next.
* * * *
Mdlle. Antonia Dolores, who was always
as charming, but never so popular an artist as
now, is about to give her positively last recitals in
Melbourne ere she returns to Europe. These
Dolores farewells are announced for Saturdav
evening (28th) and the following Thursday after-
noon. There was a time when The Bulletin
made a point of urging an unenlightened genera-
tion to go and hear Dolores, but to-day the genera-
tion needs no urging. It crowds into the Town
Hall, and cheers the singer with almost as mnch
enthusiasm as she is worth.
* * * *
The Adelaide Royal now has “Dick
Whittington ” (with cat) in full swing, contrary
to the local custom of producing pantomime on
Easter Saturday—if at all. H. H. Vincent has
staged the whole thing practically as it was seen
in Melbourne. At the Tivoli, Seeley and West
turn out to be remarkably funny comedians, one
white and one black. The idea of producing
music from all kinds of unlikely implements is
not new, but the white man and the colored
brudder do it with a quaint humor that is their
own copyright. The Schenk brothers, head
balancers, “direct from the Folies, Bergeres,
Paris,” have just been dropped into the pro-
gramme.

The Australian Widows’ Fund Life ASSUR-

1901 .. 183,805 63,293 109,639 56,519 80,940
Contingency Reserve carried to Assurance Fund 9,324
1902 .. 182,091 67,205 138,383 51,718 59,195
The assurance fund now amounts to £1,577,753, and
but for the fact that the claims were heavier than usual
last year, siill better results would have been shown.
Seven years ago the Australian Widows’ Fund wrote off
about £125,000 to cover depreciation of properties during
the boom-burst period. Since then, some of these pro-
perties have been realised and have, in most cases, yielded
more than the written-down valuation. The income from
“ Profits on Realisation of Properties” is not included in
the above list. These profits were kept separate and put
to a reserve, which, in 1901, amounted to £9324. Then
the management yielded to the temptation to make an
extra good showing in its bonus year, and carried this
amount to the life assurance fund, which helped it to
declare £100,049 cash bonus, equal to £160,400 of rever-
sionary bonus. During 1902 “ Profits on Realisation
of Securities” amounted to £4366, and this was put
to a new contingency fund. This time, it is to be hoped,
the contingency reserve will not be absorbed in the as-
surance fund. In fact, it ought to be separately invested
and allowed to accumulate at compound interest, as a
precaution against the next panic. After the experience
of 10 years ago, the life assurance societies of Australasia
should lean to excessive caution rather than to open-
handed recklessness. The Widows’ Fund values on a3£
per cent, basis a safe figure for the present at all
events; but if the fund was wanted for no other pur-
pose, it might be of material use in plaeing the accounts
on a 3 per cent, basis later on.
The expense rate shows as follows !
Expenses Compared with Total Income (Profit on
Securities not Included in Income).
1897.. £lB 4 2 percent. I 1900..£22 5 6 percent.
1898.. £l9 11 2 „ 1901..£22 17 6 „
1899.. £22 0 1 „ | 1902..£20 14 11 „ f
The reduction made in the expense rate this year is very
substantial. At the same time the rate is even now very
high—higher than it was at any time between 1890 and
1898. Still the fact that the upward movement in the
expense* has been arrested counts for a great deal.
Another point on which there has been a veey
substantial improvement is in the rate of interest yielded
by the invested funds. In the following comparison the
whole funds (contingency fund as well as life assuran'-e
fund) are included, in order to put the present'year on
the same basis as its predecessors : -
Interest earned on Average Funds.
1897.. 15 6 per cent. 1900..£4 8 4 per cent.
1898.. 4 11 11 „ 1901.. 459 „
1899.. 410 7 1902.. 4 6 8 „
The assets look, on the whole, a very respectable lot, and
when everything is taken into account—the increased
income, the improvement in the interest returns and the
diminished expense rate—the year 1902 may be regarded
as a very favorable one for the Australian Widows Fund.
In fact, if it were not for the very great foolishness of
carrying the contingency reserve to the general fund and
applying it to bonus-paying purposes, there would be little
to complain of. That act is one that can’t be condemned
too strongly, and it is to be hoped it will not be repeated.

More of the unlucky candour of the Owe’Sul-
livan :
“ He cannot understand the outcry that is being raised
in England and New South Wales against further borrow-
ing. So far as he is concerned in the expenditure, he
states that he always endeavors to use the money with
judgment and discretion, and he /crimes that, but for the
manner in which the Government has proceeded with
public ivories in a time of unexampled distress, there
toould long before now have been a crisis. He blames the
press, both here and in London, for wild and extravagant
statements." ' . J-*.
This constant assuring of Bull that New South Wales is
tottering on the verge of an abyss, and is only being
temporarily kept out of it by big’loans, isn’t calculated
to reassure the British money-lending person. Of course,
the statement may be true enough, but fancy a man talk-
ing that way, while he is blaming other people for saying
the same sort of thing.

second half of 1902 than for the’ first, and. they are
steadily eating into the foundations of t-he business. The
record since the bank wrote its old deficit off capital, and
commenced to construct the new deficit, has Been as

amount of loss on these sales. It seems probable, there-
fore, that other profits on repurchase of debentures and

• • • .
Yh u e bankruptcy examination last week of David
Chenhall, ex-solicitor, of Sydney, was chiefly interesting
aa sb ?wmg h°w a big legal firm may keep its accounts
mostly in its head, or on its shirt-cuff, or chalked up
behmd the door. Chenhall stated quite frankly that,
when he was practising on his own account, he didn't
even keep a cash-book, let alone a ledger. When he took
a P«taer proper books were started, but they soon began
to fall through from sheer weariness. For the last four
years he thought a cash-book was kept intermittently,
put that was about all. The firm sometimes gave receipts'
for money, and sometimes it didn’t. Even Chenhall’s in-
come-tax return was only prepared once ; in other years,
apparently, he and the Commissioners scrambled to a
compromise. He “ drew what money he wanted ” from
the firm, and didn’t know what his partner drew. This sort
of happy-go-lucky firm is more common than is generally
supposed, and is to be found in all countries. Writer
knew years ago of a large English insurance co. which
casually mislaid some of its books, and couldn’t tell to
within £200,000 or so what properties were insured with
it. Another sold its business very cheaply, mainly
because it had lost all hope of finding out from its own
books how it stood. The chief accountant was badly
wanted in the course of the sale to offer some attempt at
tangible explanation, but he had been missing for a week
ever since he “went to the races.” He was found in
Pans (it proved to be the Grand Prix he had gone to),
very drunk and assaulting a gendarme, but quite unable
to explain anything. And one of the biggest rows that
ever happened in a long since dead English bank only
terminated when the manager found about two dozen
lost cheques hanging up in his old coat, and reckoned he
put them there, as a piece of humor, in some of his
hiceupy moments.

Several months ago it was prophesied that
Araluen Valley Consolidated Co. would soon be on the
div. list, and that “an average weekly yield of lOOoz
and that was expected to be exceeded- meant monthly
6d. dividends. A 6d. div. absorbs £l2OO, and at 400 oz
per month, worth, say, £1520, leaves £320 for working
exs. Anticipated lOOoz. per week has not come off—
it never does-but at least 60oz. per week has been
obtained. This, on above basis of £320 working exs ner
month, should leave £590 profit, or £3540 for six months
and not even one dividend has been declared ’

stripping. Platinum, tin, gold, monazite, and iridium

out, and the party is prospecting by means of that in-

sand patches are obviously the effect of natural sluicing

mond showed me splendid wash tin (grey), and a small

Evangelical conference now or Lately
sitting in Sydney has been drawing big audi-
ences to listen to the discussion of the pops*
latioo problem, and such like subjects. Canon
Jones, in a vigorous lecture, objected to the fre-
quent elimination of certain parts of the marrixie
service, and strongly condemned the habit or
“ limiting the family. * There is a carioasly wide-
spread agitation against Xeo-Malthusianism ia
the world just now. The Ftenen Governnsent
has been striving by legislatior to deal with a very
slowly-increasing—almost stationary—population,
and t le strenuous United States President has set
on ibota^social agitation against child leas marrieew.
Public critici'-n and discussion seem to be having
some good effect. In France the population
returns have improved lately, and in Australia
families are becominc the fashion again. Syd-
ney’s birt/i-rate for 19»i3 was the best, numerics y,
sate IAU. and the best relatively to the adult
population since lllU.

FIRST INNINGS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It matters not wko (fences with jet sweet.
With whom she bicycles of afternoons.
Wha; oaa she talks to ia the mid-day street,
Ov ia what aftemoou-tea-shop she spccoa.
These aier. are stale to her. even as the day.
Aid she has heard the words they say before ;
If they make lore, their time is thrown away—
They wakes memories In her. nothing more.
For I was walking with her at sunrise ;
I tasted from her lips the morning dew;
We saw the dawning in each others eyes.

r When the plague last visited Melbourne,
• there was much talk of rat extermination and
garbage destructors, etc.—the town clerk was
even despatched on a European -tour to inquire
.into the habits and customs of these machines.
The plague passed, and with it all good resolu-
tions. T’other day. Health Officer Dr. Morris
tfound the W. Melbourne dock and wharves
“literally swarming with rats, and the tip fairly
.alive with them.”
* * * •
N.S. W. Full Court warily dodged peti-
tioner Chard’s application to fix the civil value
of a wife. Chard had married “in second
nuptials” before a decree absolute, with its
days of grace attached, was complete,
and on the strength of that desired to have
his marriage declared null and void. The court
refused to declare anything of the kind, and
Mr. Chard determined to go farther on—to the
Privy Council. But the Privy Council will not
entertain an appeal on a matter under £5OO
value, unless under certain complicated circum-
stances that don’t apply in this case. That legal
ass’s bridge makes Mr. Chard advance the plea
that a wife is worth £5OO or over. But as cer-
tainly she is sometimes worth less, N.S.W. Full
Court showed sense in keeping silence. No wise
Court would rush into such a discussion when it
could avoid it.

The things that Sydney Harbor Trust
dredged out of the Beautiful Harbor during 1902 :
3944 rats, 2344 dogs, 1631 cats, 2337 fowls, and one
bag of fowls, 415 bags of meat, 442 bags of fish, 19
ducks, 34 pigs, 1 porcupine, 426 bags of rabbits, 1
native bear, 17 calves, 3 kangaroos, 1 wallaroo, 61
hares, 48 sheep, 1 case of sheep’s trotters, 26 goats,
8 bales of hay, 85 bags of chaff, 31 parrots, 3 fly-
ing-foxes, 4 sharks, 2 bullocks, 1 snake, 3 sides of
beef, 16 turkeys, 5 lambs, 4 stingrays, 2 squirrels,
and three crates. This makes no mention of the
half-a-dozen citizens fished up during the year,
they, apparently, not being counted in the “bag.”
Seemingly, the whole index of the living and the
dead is pitched into the beautiful harbor in a short'
twelve-months space. Still there is consolation ;
the harbor is a lot more beautiful with those
things out of it.
INSUFFICIENTLY DECAYED.
HE: “ You think I am too old for love. ”
SHE: “Oh, dear no; hut you are too young for
marriage. Why, you might live another
dozen years.”
The veteran Boucaut, presiding at S.A.
Criminal Sittings, dealt sharply with one or two
appeals from the dock. He developed a fondness
for year-and-a-half sentences, giving these alike
for bicycle-stealing (several cases), a small em-
bezzlement, shop-breaking, and a big embezzle-
ment, the last-named case having the benefit of
an impassioned appeal by Josiah Symon. But a
man who stole a horse from squatter Angas, and
pleaded that he thought, out of so many
hundreds, one would not be missed, was told he
had a “bad mind,” and got two years. And a
humorous reprobate with a bad record, who
suggested that he should be discharged under the
“Old Offenders’ Act,” was called a “licensed
villain,” and a “bad brazen man.” “I would
sooner give you life,” said the indignant Boucaut;
and gave him eight years. Humor is an expensive
luxury—in the dock. Boucaut is largely develop-
ing the habit of lecturing offenders. A young
man who pleaded guilty to libelling his uncles,
two prominent citizens, was released on his own
recognisances; but he was told “if you received
12 months’ imprisonment with a whipping, it
might teach you better manners, and you would
not be getting too much. ... I don’t know
whether you are a little bit touched.” And as
the accused was settling formalities with the
Associate, the Judge leaned over and remarked,
“ It ’s a pity you have not been in the navy and
married the gunner’s daughter once or twice ! ”
which piece of nautical slang seemed to bewilder
the youth completely.
* • • *
A German nine-year-old at a N.S. W.
public-school, asked for an essay on “the most
miserable day I ever spent,” wrote with some
wisdom:
The most miserable day I ever spent is not so very easy
to find out, but I think it is when I was a baby. I only
had to sit down, and keep there. If I wanted to do any-
thing, I had to cry. Another thing, I could not
sit where I liked; I had to stay where I was
put. And when I got bigger enough to be wheeled
about in a purambulator, it was not very pleasant. They
would tip it up side down, and I would get a buster —a
lump on the head or something. And, after they tip me
over, they don’t tell. I think that is my most miserable
day ! ever had.
How’s this for a fine sample of cheek?”
News item from Melbourne (19/2/’O3): —
Those prisoners who were to be tried at the Criminal
Court, some of whom may be acquitted, all had their
trials postponed one day, through Mr. Justice A’Beckett
suspending the sitting of the Court because his son was
to be married in the afternoon.
Doubtless, the marriage of Mr. A’Beckett’s son is
of more importance to Mr. A’Beckett than the
insignificant circumstance of keeping possibly
innocent men in gaol a day more than is absolutely
necessary, but Mr. A’Beckett is paid, not to
attend his son’s marriage, but to sentence or re-
lease those men with the least possible delay. If
Mr. A’Beckett hasn’t noticed this it is time some-
body led Mr. A’Beckett into a quiet spot and
mentioned the fact to Mr. A’Beckett.

British public on Australian customs generally,

meat for the next polka.’ . . . Also —‘Seize your

spent most of his time philandering among the

“ Seraphina ” : At mv boarding-house a
lady laments the murderous tendencies of her
three-year-old son. He kills every living thing
within his province—chickens, kittens, flies, ants,
beetles, etc. He tried a hornet last, but a hornet
is full of “buts.” Later on the lady confided to
me that she “tried all she knew” to destroy the
child before its birth. Cause and effect?

“oecupa -
tion” is un-
known to
the law, as
its use
draws in-
vidious
class - dis -
tinctions
not intend-

it compels the working-

to remove this snobbery
from our new rolls. If “married

iti is certainly good enough for the purposes of an

Dr. Klein, expert to the Local Govt.
Board, London, bought, by direction, two Bd.
pork pies and two 2d. pork, - and analysed them.
“None contained bacillus coli, and none con-
tained spores of any pathogenic aerobe. But all
contained spores of anaerobic non-pathogenic
bacillus babyricus. Also, all contained spores of
bacillus mesentericus vulgatus and staplylo-coccus
albus of two different kinds.” Truly fearsome-
even for a pork-pie 1 But what The Bulletin
wants to know after all this is, were the pies
really bad ?

A letter from “Cheetah”:—
Considering: the numerous blunders of members of the
medical profession, I think we should have a Medical
Judiciary, the members of which might be placed beyond
reproach by reason of their salaries and training. On
account of the number of deaths through the malad-
ministration of chloroform, the cases of septic poisoning
that arise through the use of dirty instruments, the care”
less diagnosis of even the simplest infantile complaints ;
and for a thousand and one other valid reasons—this is’
necessary. At present many medicos are merely legalised
butchers, while the majority are of little use other than
to give “ a certificate of death ” to permit of no delay
over the funeral of the departed. Wanted--an indepen-
dent Court of Medical Inquiry !
Which is interesting, if only to show public mis-
conception on medical matters. Deaths from
chloroform are too common: septic poisoning
from the use of dirty instruments is, however,
rare ; and as to careless diagnosis of the simplest
infantile complaints !!—well, the medical treat-
ment of the adult (who can describe his symptoms
and tell where the pain is) may be generally
described as putting drugs that little is known about
into a stomach of which much less is known ; and
the scientific medical treatment of the “simplest
infantile complaints” is a thing that sanguine
doctors don’t even dream about as yet. “Chee-
tah’s” idea that it is easy to treat an infant
“because it is such a little thing” is almost
equalled in absurdity by the statement that “the
majority of doctors are of “little use other than
to give a certificate of death.” Almost all legally
qualified doctors can be relied upon to be able to
give some help in eases of illness, very great help
m eases of accident. That they can’t perform
miracles and see into the inside of a squalling
infant is not their fault, but the fault of the reli-
gious organisations which let miracles go out of
fashion.

TheN.S.W. magisterial bench which will
be able under B. R. Wise’s new Bill to rope-in the
putative father before the illegitimate is seen
may, according to a veteran stipendiary, meet,
with trouble, There is, it appears, an unrepealed
Act of Charles 11. which, in the event of a
magistrate’s decision in such cases being success-
fully appealed against, decrees that the magistrate
shall support the illegitimate child until a father
for it can be found.

There is quite an appendicitis scare in
Australia: and one effect of it is that passion-
fruit are not popular, it being imagined that the
seeds are liable to cause appendicitis
A man named John Candler was, the other
day, prosecuted at Stawell
(Vic.) by a “ combine ” of
clergymen for sending
what they were pleased
to call blasphemous matter
through-the post. Thisjs
the first result of a very
dubious clause in the Com-
monwealth postal, law. It
seems that Candler had
sent to the clergymen of
Stawell a printed circular
denouncing the Biblical
theory of creation and
Christianity as
preached in these
days. For this he
was fined £lO, with
£3 3s. costs. He was
further prosecuted for
circulating printed
matter not bearing the
name of the printer. This
time he fell in to the extent
of £5 fine. The second,
fine was deserved, although it
would appear that
the meek and
lowly parsons in-
stituted the prose-
cution out of
malice. Although .
Candler was fined ,
for sending blasphemous matter through the post,
it is quite certain that neither the parsons who
prosecuted nor the bench that adjudicated could
define blasphemy. Cardinal Manning tried, arid
only ended in the vague assertion that it was
“ resistance to the known truth.” But 150 years
ago it was a known truth that Slieol was situated
in the interior of the earth—and where is that
known truth now? A much better definition was
given by Ingersoll, when he said : “ Priests have
invented a crime called blasphemy. That crime
is the breastwork behind which ignorance, super-
stition and hypocrisy have crouched for thousands
of years, and shot their poisoned arrows at the
pioneers of human thought.”
* » • *
Writes “Sol. S.’’ on one side (and not
necessarily the right side) of a controversy :
The Maoriland First Offenders Probation Act, well-
meant as it was, is proving not only a dismal failure, but
an incentive to crime, as the juvenile criminal can
generally count on getting off the first time. Few of
this class seem to care for work after conviction, even
when employers are forthcoming. They prefer to hang-
round the country pubs, in some menial capacity, some-
times being useful to the police as spies. But, Sooner or
later, they find their way to gaol under the Vagrant Act. .
The last case on record is that of a bank office-boy, who
opened a letter and spent the contents (some £7O), and
when arrested cheerfully pleaded guilty. The beak, poor
old ex-Judge Kenny, who ought to have known better,
abused prosecutor for not having registered the letter,
and Supreme Court Judge Conolly (another fossil)
admitted the young thief to probation, repeating part of
Mr. Kenny’s remarks to the witness. Parliament ought
to put a new broom in the Justice Department, aiid
squash the Probationers Act.

Adelaide is in danger of losing still more
of its park lands—this time for an extension of the
city cemetery, which is now about as full as it can
he. Over £25,000 was spent on a new ground 12
miles along the north railway line; but it is
growled now that the place is too far out, th.,'b
people can’t afford to die if they have to be buried
there. But that is easily got over; municipalise
the undertaking business. For the time actually
given'to the job there is no trade that pays so well
as the ghoul’s; and with the concentration of the
work the necessary thing could be done, often
more decently, and always for half the money.
HIS TROUBLE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
O, Captain Cook, what ails you on your pedestal so tall.
And what brings upon your face of bronze a frown ?
Your clothes are good as ever, man—what troubles you
at all—
You who are the finest figure in the town 1
Through the night and through the day,
While the seasons pass away,
And the generations rush the gap to fill
That comes quickly in the census,
Through the life that now so tense is
And the strife,
Tooth end claw, and fist and knife,
For a crust of bread, and beer,
You stand stately and austere
On your noble monument —
And you have to pay no rent—
Then, what troubles you, O elevated captain on a hill j
There is nothing that the heart could wish by way of
scenery
That you cannot view when folk are in their beds ;
You are placed in a position whence the blindest man
could see
All the ships of all the world come through the Heads.
In the moonlight weird and wan,
In the rosy light of dawn,
You know well you have the gallantest outlook
On the Harbor blue and sparkling,
On the Harbor grey and darkling,
Night and day
You know well, old sea-dog gray,
That you see with pride profound
All the buildings fine around—
The Cathedral dusky-pale,
The Museum, and the gaol
Then what makes you look so worried, O silent weighty
Cook?
Then Captain Cook made answer in a voice subdued and
low,
And the green, green tears of verdigris he shed ;
His speech was like a speech made through a foghorn
stuffed with tow,
And these are something like the words he said
“ There’s the Harbor that I found !
Search the whole wide world around
And you will not find a Bay with it to cope i
It was once as calm and quiet
As a seaman on low diet
After rum—
But who knows the things to come ?
It is now a wet disgrace,
And a squalid market-place.
And as sure as now I stand
On this pedestal so grand,
With my outstretched bronze right hand
Indicating Pride and Hope,
I shall wake from dreams some night,
And find in that same hand—
Which I wish it held a rope—
• With horror and affiright,
A disgusting big advertisement of Brown’s Gorilla
Soap.”

RAINCOATS.
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, February 24, 1903.
Mr Dear Arini,—
I’ve been on a visit—my first visit—to Hobart. I
mentioned this quite unimportant fact to a young
woman on the deck of the Oonah as we drew into
the wharf, and in a sea-sick undertone wondered
if I should “ see any difference. ” She turned to
me sympathetically, and said, slowly, “I live
here, ana I’ve been seeing Sydney and Melbourne
for the first time, and I don t see much difference.”
She paused, and her fine, bovine eyes rested on
Mt. Wellington’s lapis lazuli head: “Except in
this—you can’t help noticin’ it—the buns in our
confectioners’ winders have four times as many
currants in them as the buns in Sydney and Mel-
bourne.” ‘
That kind of girl must be lovable. Someone
else may discourse on the value of travel to broaden
the mental outlook. This honeymoon land (it is
full of couples newly Wedded, and they walk
about leaning on one another and making human
triangles with the pavement as the base) is afire
With flowers, carpeted riotously with the china
aster, dominated by the giant sunflower and
scented by the mulberry. There is a smell of
crushed mulberries all over the sky-dyed land,
although Ethel Turner says that to her Tasmania
fairly exudes raspberry scent. If you come
early in the summer it is strawberries that per-
fume the land. Hedges are green with hawthorn
“pips,” and fruit of the briar rose. The haw-
thorn crop is so unusually heavy that a severe
winter is anticipated. “One of nature’s pro-
visions,” said the Hobartian, who pointed out
the berry harvest “ for the birds.”
Besides honeymooners, Hobart is still well-
stocked with summer girls and their mothers
from N.S.W. and Vic. You may know them
from the native-born by their sunburnt faces—
noses, cheeks and foreheads one confluent scarlet.
Also may you know them by their louder, sharper
voices, voices with something of the galah-parrot
in them. Hobart women’s voices are softer, more
mezzo, but, great Mount Wellington ! how badly
they have got some of the Cockney pronunciation !
The weather—“the soft, consenting atmos-
phere,” the windless blandness of each day, makes
one think that heaven and earth are holding a
dreamy tryst together. I am told, and I don’t
wonder at it, that lives are longer in Tasmania
than in other Australian States. If you could see
Adye Douglas, now in his 88th year, frisking
down to his office every morning like a boy going
to a cricket-match, youd believe it. Adye has been
married three times —three sisters —and his
voungest child is young enough to wear a Little
Lord Fauntleroy costume.
Very little things minister to pleasure in this
city of dreams, hills, and afternoon-teas. On a
day this week a garden fete was given at the
place of a local Somebody in aid of the homoeo-
pathic hospital, and feminine Hobart was there
selling sweets, playing croquet, or strolling among
the vivid flowers with a Puritan demureness. Old
ladies (they who say there are no old ladies nowa-
days ought to come to Hobart)—such old, old
ladies, beautifully dressed in black —sat round on
rustic seats and talked massage; middle-aged ones
discussed the Melba fiasco—a theme that will last
Tassy for weeks yet. The few husbands present
stood silent and patient. Miss Clarke just
back with her father, Justice Clarke, from
U.S.A., was around. I met her some six or seven
years ago in Melbourne. She then had hair down
to her toes, or thereabouts, and the late Julian
Thomas was guide-booking her. Now she has
Americanitis very badly, for Boston, and especi-
ally the boys of the Stanford Uuiversity, did her
proud.
I was recently asked to a political afternoon-
tea when 23 ladies (and a mere man to steer the
entertainment) assembled in the usual-sized
suburban drawing-room, with the temperature 2
below boiling |>oint, to hear what the referendum
and decision by arbitration were all about. The
young gentleman read his information heavily
from a type-written document, and the ladies
meantime double-banked his gems of knowledge
by teaching one another in an undertone the
mysteries of fish-boning and bird’s-eye stitching.
Tea was wedged in with difficulty—it came in
sideways, so to speak—and everybody agreed that
the proceedings showed woman to have made
great intellectual advances since she got the vote.
One woman was afterwards found looking for the
referendum in the cookery book, but some of the
good seed always falls on barren ground.
Apropos the servant-girl discussion, in England
a number of well-educated women, finding them-
selves alone in a cold world with a few hundred
pounds, which unkind friends advised them to
invest in corner shops where cash is scarce and
credit is long, took counsel among themselves and
founded the Guild of the Dames of the House-
hold. The Guild, taking up pleasant quarters in
London, advertised for pupils to be taught house-
keeping, cooking, parlor and pantry duties on
scientific lines. The idea caught on, and the
Dames and their pupils are in great demand, and
the supply is far from adequate. They have fixed
rates of remuneration below which no dame will
accept service. They will live on the premises
if desired, but prefer to stay in the Guild
House. They have a proper legal agreement
which stipulates the hours and nature of their
duties, the remuneration, and the holidays; and
stipulates that the dames shall be addressed as
Mrs. or Miss, according to circumstances.
In marriage notices done hy country papers,
the bride always “looks charming,” no matter
how she’s “got up” or what her color is. To
look charming is the aim and delight of all brides
at the critical moment of crossing the “ Bridge of
Sighs,” and the country rag daren’t say they
looked any other way. A bit of candor like this
{from a Missouri journal) once in a while would
>e refreshing:
MARRIED. —Miss Sylvia Khades to James Oarnaham,
last Saturday afternoon. The bride is an ordinary local
girl, who doesn’t know any more than a rabbit about
cooking, and never helped her poor mother three days in
her life. She is not a beauty, by any means, and has a
gait like a fat duck. The groom is well-known here as
an up-to-date loafer ; has been living off the old folks all
his life, and don’t amount to anything nohow. They will
have a hard life while they live together, and we hasten
to extend absolutely no congratulations, for we don’t
believe any good can come of such a union.
A Sydneyite writes this :
Three things are certainly on the increase among the
women of Sydney : the drinking of alcoholic liquors in
public places of refreshment, and the smoking of cigar-
ettes and opium. Opium is usually associated with
Chinese, but to-day there are opium parlors (they cannot
be called dens) in Sydney where a pig-tail is never seen,
and where the practice is carried on amid surroundings
altogether beautiful.
A lady from Paniput (India) told me of one opium
parlor, and proposed to take me there. Each member of
the opium club is allowed to introduce a friend, but is
financially responsible for any injury or mischief caused
by that friend.
The cab emptied us out in a narrow lane largely com-
posed of two brick walls with trees hanging over them.
The lady from India produced a latchkey, and with it
opened a door in the wall, and we wandered down a
shaded garden to a rambling house at the bottom, and
the same key opened the front door. An Algerian lamp
swung in the hall, and its rays fell obliquely on a por-
tiere on which a golden dragon sprawled in a field of
poppies—beautiful crimson poppies. “ It’s the only
Chinese thing in the place,” said she, and spoke in a
whisper, as though it were a holy place. She drew back
the portiere and the crimson curtain that hung behind
it. A puff of pungent fume irritated, and then, in a
sense, delighted the nostrils.
Right round the wall ran a dado which was a field of
dreamy crimson poppies. They swayed in no breeze, but
hung there dreamily in the deep hush of the burning
afternoon. Around the wall ran settees—an endless chain
of sofas, whereon men and women, mostly the latter, lay
head to head and feet to feet. In the centre of the room
were ferns and palms, and a fountain sprayed and dripped
—dripped with a monotonous rhythm. On the edge of
the marble basin a bronze bird stood meditatively on one
leg. Beneath, gold fish swam round, and a live frog or
two hopped and splashed in a languorous manner
s ’ -gesting that the fumes of the drug had affected them
also.
they lay, men and women—“ladies and gentle-
men ” rather, for there was not a skirt in the room that
cost less than £6—head to head, feet to feet, with the
tiny lamp on which the opium is cooked between them ;
and while dreamy music floated through the slumbrous
atmosphere they cooked the beautiful golden opium.
Head to head and feet to feet. Their faces dreamy,
languid, mask-like. Over them floated a blue-grey cloud,
and it drifted and swayed and veiled the smokers for a
second, and when they appeared and re-appeared they
were more like masks than human faces—dim ghosts
around whom clung no horror and no terror.
The lady from Paniput smoked a pipe and told me
afterwards of the feeling. “The body became buoyant,
the senses keen and alert to extract the least grain of
pleasure and beauty from sight and sound ; the drifting
vapor came closer and shut out the gross material world,
and one drifted along the strains of music to Eden, but
never quite got to Eden. You breathe the perfume of the
blossoms just inside the gate ; you hear fragments of the
songs they sing ; you see flashes of the glory—and then
the cloud lifts, and the bronze bird standing meditatively
on one leg by the marble basin takes form, and—
’Tis the earth once more, and a terrible headache.”
Three-fourths of the occupants of that drowsy parlor
were women. They lay there smoking serenely. Some
few were really unconsious—they were novices. Most sat
and lounged about, with their bodices unfastened at the
neck. The experienced woman-smoker, I learned, leaves
her corsets at home or in the ladies’ dressing room, and
enters the parlor closely clad. Tight-fitting garments
spoil the real comfort of the smoke. All talk is carried
on in whispers, or in a voice keyed so low as to be
inaudible a yard away. There is nothing ribald, no sign
or sound of abandonment, and if there is a casual
exhibition of an ankle as some woman twists into a more
comfortable position, no opium smoker is interested.
There are subtler delights in the golden opium.
Most of the women patrons are the idle well-to-do—
women whose husbands occupy good positions in the
city and who live in the fashionable boarding-houses that
are now so thick around Potts and Darling Points. They
have no children, no household duties, no hobby, and
time drags wearily. And they turn to opium-smoking
and its dearly-won delights.
Papa Lyne at Sydney Masonic Hall the other
night had a list of 200 articles, “ which are as cheap
and in many cases cheaper than when the tariff
went on.” That speech is causing trouble in the
boarding houses. Some hash ladies have been
declining to give potatoes with the cold meat
lunch; the price absolutely forbade it. Now the
hungry boarders confront them with Lyne’s
figures, and the landlady subsides sullenly, and
remarks that she “ hopes as how the prices won’t
go up agen when Mr. Reid comes back.”
Talking of Mr. Reid, a lovely caricature of that
politician is given by George Willoughby, as
“The Wrong Mr. Wright,” at Sydney Palace.
He has the same sandy hair, cleared off in patches
in the same inartistic fashion, and the portly front
is similar, but not quite so pronounced ; if Mr.
Willoughby wore a monocle the picture would be
complete.
Bert Toy, the Queensland violinist who played
at Sydney Centenary Hall, Saturday night, with
Mr. Henry Weir and Miss Maud Dalrymple, is
boyish-looking with a pleasant smile. He’s pure
merino, born in Vic., and reared in Bananaland.
He has a splendid opportunity to be original; if
he had his haif clipped short he would not only
improve his appearance but attract attention as
the first of a new Australian breed of short-haired
violinists.
The man who recently victimised school marms
in Sydney and Melbourne is nOw operating in,
Auckland, M.L. In that province they grow
ladies of stout minds and generous proportions:
and the man may meet with well-deserved trouble
on his predatory career.

Mr. H. H. Share, who was private secretary to
Sir Lewis Beaumont, returns after a brief visit to
Europe to resume the same position on the staff of
Sir. H. Rawson. Mr, Share took a leaf out of St.
Paul’s book, and was “ all things to all men and
women.” Hence his popularity.

Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“Said that at the Delhi Durbar the Europeans
looked dull and dowdy beside the Orientals. Yet
the Westerners had put their best feet forward.
Viceroy Curzon’s wife, when she first went to
India, took with her a wardrobe worth from
£lO,OOO to £12,000, exclusive of jewellery. She
had a hundred gowns, one valued at £2OO, and a
pearl chain which cost £2500. A woman with
dress traditions of such splendor to live up to
would launch out even more than usual on the
occasion of a Durbar ; but it was the glittering
elephants that seemed to dwarf and fade their
riders into insignificance. When it comes to a
contest of gems and colors, there’s room for so
much more gem on an elephant than a woman.
I often wonder why bookmakers and such don’t
marry elephants.
“A huge crowd took part in the Castles’ ‘fare-
well.’ Rather an angry crowd to start with be-
cause the seats were mismanaged. The balcony
ushers, what there were of them, were mere
children, physically unable to prevent in-
truders from ‘jumping’ reserved seats. It
was quite alarming to hear threats of
eviction, &c., bandied to and fro between
trespassers and lawful owners. One ancient
dame literally keened. She had set her heart
on one of the promised Castles ‘souvenirs,’ and
got in the end neither souvenir, progamme nor her
reserved seat. An enormously fat woman, with
warm-colored hair and a squint, grew so desperate
with standing that she sat right down on top of a
nice young priest, and then apologised and said
that she had not seen that anyone was there. He
squeezed a niche for her between himself and a
jolly old ‘father,’ who chuckled over his blushing
junior’s discomfiture, while everyone within sight
of the trio caught the giggling infection, till in
that part of the hall the seat tragedy resolved
itself into a farce.
“That gifted singer who, as a schoolgirl,
habitually tormented her teachers and teased her
parents, has mounted so high on the ladder of
success, that present-day rompers, in view of the
sometime hoyden’s dazzling achievements (social
no less than professional) are apt to turn a deaf
ear to warnings against ‘loud, unladylike con-
duct.’ The Personage was brought up by a staid
Scotch father on the same lines as her sisters who,
in their salad-days, were fair, slight damsels, given
to sitting up straight and behaving so nicely that
Dad constantly held them up as models to their
dark, rollicking senior. Not that the anxious
parent was less fond of his first-born, but, for her
own sake, he wanted ‘Madcap’ to sober down.
In those days, girl visitors at the house were kept
on pins and needles by their frisky chum’s non-
sense. Dad would scarcely have turned away
after delivering a lengthy, fatherly leoture to the
culprit before Missie s tongue would shoot out
derisively, ‘ I’m looking to see if my tongue
is as long as Dad’s,’ the owner would ex-
plain seriously, squinting horribly in her attempts
to scan the protruded member. At that time she
frequently made one of the family party, which,
headed by her father, weekly attended Sunday
service and choir practice at Scots’ Kirk. The
pilgrims travelled by bus, and the ball was gener-
ally set rolling by Mam’zelle’s naughtiness in
perching beside the driver. After arguments pa
would give in, and, surrounded by the more rest-
ful members of the little flock, would go inside
the vehicle and soon be peacefully pondering
double chants, &c. Then a beady-black eye,
twinkling with mischief, would reconnoitre the
placid scene through the ’busman’s peep-hole, and
next thing an ear-splitting whistle would rouse
the dreamer from his reverie of psalmody to re-
ality, and he would catch his daughter blowing
through her fingers into the eye-chink, just above
his head.
“ A Hobart person forwards this further report
of Melba’s brief and disastrous career in Launces-
ton :
The meteor-like flight of the Diva was too much for
Launceston, and the poor disconsolate mutton-birds are
now hiding their diminished heads under their several
wings, woe being writ large all over them. Nellie was
received at Launceston by a prominent “ interrer of our
dead selves,” and was conducted to an hotel, where a
diminutive slavey after asking “ Who BE you, mum?"
showed her to a room. Nellie arose again after a few
crushed moments and mildly requested—“ Lunch.” A
very magnificent personage announced—“ Lunch will be
served at the usual hour, madam,” and then and there
Nellie, silently ejaculating “ This is no place for Austra-
lia’s noblest daughter,” picked up her skirts and fled.
Mr. Thompson, who arranged the trip, was at first blamed
for not depositing the thousand guineas, but the facts
were that the Diva had a wretched trip over, a bad
throat when she landed, a strong disinclination to be
serenaded by the band when she wanted to sleep, and in
general her “will not” came out in much stronger force
than her “ will.”

“Crowds, indifferently dressed, poured out to
the wind-up of the V.A.T.C. Autumn meeting.
Saturday was one of those boisterous ‘political
weather ’ days, when a variety of changes made
havoc with gowns. A sable-coated woman looked
suitable for one minute, a white muslinite the
next. Mrs. Leslie Macdonald showed resource-
fulness. When the spring sun and south breeze
minuetted on the lawn, she strode forth in a royal
blue canvas garb, roomy but decorative. When
the north-east wind blew, and grey clouds subdued
the emerald-tinted sward, she took a turn in one
of the new three-deck autumn coats. A returned
tourist, Mrs. Toorak Payne, and Mrs. C. Raleigh
tried conclusions in very fine black toilettes —the
latter sporting sky-blue trimmings with success.
“The most daring turn-out was owned by the
wife of a Collins-st. dentist. She struck a bold
note with a black coat covered from collar to
knee-hem with a costly and uncreasing layer of
real lace. This garment hugged the contours of
the figure closely, recalling Zaza s glove-fitting
garb. Mrs. Fred. M'Evoy, of C.O. chorus fame,
wore an aesthetic turn-out of pale blue voile, and
black picture hat.
“There was a sudden break-out of the matri-
monial fever last week, marking the annual
Lenten prelude. In all the R.C. and C. of E.
quarters the shepherds were briskly coupling.
Several of the newly-wedded are booked for
S’Africa. One of the order describes Johannesburg
as a new Paris. He tells his bride so, to put all fear
of the strange land out of her heart. She believes
him and takes a wardrobe equal to the hope.

’Ome. There is in the air a scheme to get the
Yallanges settled in their villa under the English
oaks, and to have the clans foregather there at
intervals of globe-gadding. But all depends upon
Miss Ruby s plans. She is just now on her way
from the Durbar, where her coon songs are said to
have charmed all ears.

“ All the Western Woolites are rallying for the
Flemington race meeting on the 28th and the
Polo programme of the following week. Polo is
a signal for petticoat and hose purchase. You
see, there is a continuous getting in and out of
lofty carts and other smart vehicles during the
progress of the play, and stepping over grass-
grown fields. Skirts are consequently hoisted
high and hose becomes of importance.
“From a golfing district comes the story of a
handmaid getting notice because her coquetry
interfered with dinner-parties, and had led to one
guest in particular making sheep’s eyes at her over
the mutton-broth. The mistress of the house didn’t
tell her maid what was the real trouble, but a
reduction in wages was suggested. The young
person was generous. ‘I’m sorry things are so
bad, but I like the place ; I’ll stay even at eight
bob!’ Then the mistress, completely flouted,
went and held counsel with her spouse. He was
brutal. ‘ Tell her to go straight. Say she isn’t
giving satisfaction! ’ The mistress faced the
situation again. Then the young person snorted:
‘ If you think I don’t know what’s up, you’re a
fool. It’s rank jealousy ; and I, pooh, I wouldn’t
give a straw for your red-nosed old men. You
can have the lot. I’ll go and get into better com-
pany.’
“ Talking of good-looking housemaids recalls
the fact that Melbourne has a humorous registry-
office agent, who boasts of gauging to a nicety the
home-conditions of applicants. The lady who
requires ‘ a strong woman for the kitchen, a stout,
reliable wench not afraid of work, ’ has, she guesses,
a Lothario for spouse, and means to keep him in
order if she can. It is a timid, gentle wife who
asks for a nice, clean, smart, pretty girl, as ‘ her
husband likes that kind.’

Peter Woods, said to have driven the first
steam locomotive which ran in N.S.W. (Svdnev to
Parramatta, 1855), died recently in Victoria. For
many years it was understood that the late Wm.
Sixsmith was the first driver, but his claim seems
to have fallen through.
Among bygone Victorian orators who
wrote their speeches and committed them to
memory, Chas. Gavan Duffy was the only one who
was ever letter-perfect in his part. His delivery
was that of a half-choked squeak, but as he was a
literary artist, he always scored when his addresses
appeared in print.
During the 46 years that he was a Bible
Christian minister James Rowe, who died in Ade-
laide lately, kept accounts which showed that he
had averaged barely £2 a week ! Mr. Rowe, a
knotty old Cornishman, was, with Chief-Justice
Way’s father, the pioneer of his sect in S.A., and
personally “established the cause” in Victoria.
Henry Lawson reckons his fall off the
cliffs at Manly was of material benefit to his
business prospects (reminiscent of the Paris
publisher who told an author “to try a little ad-
vertisement, ” and suggested that he might be run
over by a ’bus !) Since his fall, several other Aus-
tralian poets of renown have gone to live at
Manlv. So far there have been no further
casualties.

A Bruce Auction is proposed in aid of

Charles Nicholson, now in his 95th year,
is still a conspicuous figure in London. Having
taken his M.D. at Edinburgh, he emigrated
to Australia in ’34, and for 30 years was
a leading Sydney citizen and Parliamentarian.
He alone survives of the first Australian Parlia-
ment that met in Sydney in 1843. After serving
for some years as the first Chancellor of the
Sydney University, he returned to the Cold
Country in 1862.
Foxton (M.L.) claims to have the two
biggest children in Australasia in Ruby and Wil-
fred Westwood. Ruby is aged 9, weighs 16st.
121 b., is 4ft. 9in. height, with a chest measure-
ment of 52in. The boy is 4 years old, stands 3ft.
weighs lOst. 41b., and is 41in. round the
chest. The father, who is a storekeeper and flax-
miller, turns the scale at 12st., while the mother
weighs only 6st. 121 b. There are five other
children in the family, but none of them is re-
markable for size.
A Sydney broker back from Africa
“speaks of the strange passing of Baden Powell.
There is great prejudice against B.P. among
South Africans, who look upon him as a posturer.”
But the evidence against the military Barnum and
his circus “siege” was already convincing. The
other night The Bulletin dreamt it wandered
down York-st. and heard a voice raised in bitter
lamentation. The voice said : “ Give us back our
sword ! give us back our charger ! give back the
babies’ pennies ! ” Then there was a loud yell of
“ Bogus ! ” and the moon winked distinctly as The
Bulletin wandered on.
Byron Moore, a Victorian of 50 years
standing, has now turned his 70th birthday, it
seems. A wonderful veteran is the secretary of
the V.R.C. Placed in the forefront of the racing
world, he takes no interest in horses except as
manager of the ground where they provide the
entertainment. Couldn’t compile a good handi-
cap to save his life, but can compose an anthem,
if required. SHfas written a capital little book of
fairy tales, and conducted oratorio performances.
Versatile fellows of the Byron Moore stamp never
grow old, like their heavy, pompous contem-
poraries among the “representative* class.
There's nothing heavy about the septuagenarian
see. of the V.R.C. He wears light-grey clothes,
and a hat to match, and dispenses with the
burden of a stummick.
Old Dean Marryat, of Adelaide (who is a
nephew of Captain Marryat, the sea novelist, and
a brother-in-law of Governor Fox Young, who
accompanied Capt. Cadell in his first trip by
steamer up the River Murray), was in 1853 chap-
hinofth6 penal establishments at Darlinghurst
and Cockatoo Island, Sydney. He will never
speak of his experiences there, however. He has
now been in Adelaide for just half-a-century, and
he has been Dean for 16 years. He is very rotund
and very bald, and has a very cracked voice.
F. B Suttor, Vice-President of N.S.W.
Deg. Council, has held Ministerial office longer
than any other local M.P. He began with Parkes
m 1877, a.nd has been included in half-a-dozen
Cabinets since. He is 63 years of age. The only
sensational event in his political life was when
he formally discarded Freetrade and adopted
Protection. The vengeance of Parkes, who
preached Freetrade and ran a fine, large Protec-
tionist tariff himself, pursued him to Bathurst on
that occasion, and fairly flattened him out.

No previous Vic. Premier maintained such
solemn state when receiving deputations as does
Irvine. He surrounds himself with other Minis-
ters and departmental magnates, and at one
recent interview, on the subject of a small country
railway, _ was supported by Ministers Bent,
McKenzie, Taverner, the Secretary of the Lands
Dept., the Surveyor-General, Bent’s secretary,
and a dozen other functionaries. It only needed
a couple of coolies waving fans above his haughty
brow to make the ceremony as imposing as the
Delhi Durbar.
Oppositionists in Vic. Assembly complain
that Premier Irvine, when making important
statements to the House, invariably turns his
back on them, and addresses himself solely to
Sammy Lancaster and the Kyabram crowd at his
back. It is also impossible for the gallery
reporters to hear half what Irvine says.
Parliamentary courtesy demands that a speaker
should face the chair. Irvine affords Speaker
Gillies an excellent view of the place where he is
supposed to keep his well-advertised “backbone.”
Co-incident with the temporary
absence of lordly little “Johnny ” Clay-
ton from Melbourne Town Clerk’s office
there has disappeared from Council
circles the air of “culchaw” in which
his soul was wont to wallow. Under
his direction verandah and telegraph-
posts were adorned with calico-signs
stating that “Pedestrians are re-
quested not to expectorate on the
footpath.” When “Johnny” went a-
jaunting, giving place to democratic
Alex. Stewart, these were torn down
and enamelled signs bearing the legend,
“Do not spit,” &c., substituted. The
Post-office people, however, went one
better than “Johnny.” In the E.T.O.
lobby, where the shipping news is
posted, may be read the assurance:
“Gentlemen WILL NOT expectorate
on the floor.” And the shellback comes
there. to see if the brigantine Eliza
Ann is sighted, and spits cheerfully on
the floor, and wonders what on earth
“ expectorate ” means.
