Shiels, Victoria’s dismal financier, is striv-
ing hard for mastery in the Irvine Cabinet.
Irvine is anything but the stern, strong man that
his press hacks make him out to be. When Shiels
was taken into the Ministry, it was reported
about the lobbies by members that Irvine in-
tended to keep the doleful one in his proper place.
He was not to be the “ equal-in-all-things ” Trea-
surer he was in the M‘Lean Ministry. That
has proved to be all flam. Shiels is as much boss
in the Cabinet as Irvine himself. He has shown
this more than once in the House. For example,
he refused to be dictated to in regard to the
matter and manner of his budget statement. And
again, he deliberately arose and stated that if
he had been in charge of a certain measure instead
of the Premier he would not have permitted an
amendment which the Premier allowed.
Melb. barrister Box, whose
name is being mentioned among
possibilities for succession to
Judge Hartley Williams, made
his first rise at the bar in a novel
fashion. As a briefless junior,
he was drifting round the courts
one day during the initial pro-
ceedings in a very big and costly
suit, in which every barrister of
any standing in Vic. 30 years ago
had been engaged. A solicitor’s
clerk came up and asked whether
Box had a brief, which so tickled
that inexperienced sprig that
he laughed outright. “If you
haven’t,” remarked the clerk,
“you’d better take this one.
You're about the only man I can
find who isn’t in the case
already.” Box took a place
among the counsel, and this, his
first case, brought him in £l2OO
ere it finished.
Maoriland Police Commis-
sioner Tunbridge, who is retiring
from the position on the alleged
ground of ill-health, started as a
foot constable in the London
Police, and 26 years after was
occupying the position of chief
inspector in the Criminal Investi-
gation Dept. Then he came to
Maoriland and re-organised a
force that had sunk rather low
in public estimation. He was the
practical author of the Police
Provident Fund, out of which
decayed and injured “bobbies”
obtain pensions which make it un-
necessary for the Maoriland man
in blue to go through the pockets of affluent
drunks to provide for his old age. The real reason
for the Commissioner’s resignation is said to be
the action of a desperately officious Minister of the
buzzing blue-bottle variety in overriding his
decisions re a slight unpleasantness which occurred
in connection with the force in Nelson.

“ 8.A.L.” reminisces :
Par. re John Ross (Bulletin 14/2/’O3) calls to mind
Arthur Ash win, now on the East Murchison (W.A.), one
of the party who first overlanded stock from 8. A. to the
Territory. For this feat the S.A. Govt, of the day offered
a monetary reward, but meanly cancelled it whilst the
stock referred to were on the road. Ashwin afterwards
tried for gold in the Mac Donnell ranges, and with a mate
prospected Mt. Browne (Barrier) receiving the Govt, re-
ward for same. In late years he has done a lot of
pioneering in W.A., being one of a little party who
opened the Cue-Southern Cross, Kurnalpi-Mt. Margaret,
and Lawlers-Lake Darlot roads. Ashwin, contrary to the
general rule, bids fair to spend his declining years in
comfort.
In 1898 the election expenses of the
remarkable Geo. Fisher, M.H.R. (M.L.) amounted
to about three half crowns. At the last election
they were £9. He only held one meeting in a
hall. On that occasion the Choral Hall was
packed and crowds stood outside hoping to hear
“ Our George.” When he made his appearance
upon the stage he struck a dramatic attitude and
said, “ The heart ! the heart! the heart! ” all the
time placing his hand where that organ was
supposed to be. That was his speech and as it
brought forth vociferous cheering, he followed it
up by singing a bar of “ Sons of the Sea.” Then
he retired not quite gracefully—and was elected.
Lord Roberts’ alleged refusal to pay the
fees of his new title is really a good example to
democracies. When Premier Parkes took the
“G.”of his trumpery order he had to apply to
friends for money to pay the fees. Dibbs, when
he took his “K.” was very hard-up. Titles with
the charges attached to them are cunningly-
devised royal weapons. They bind flunkeys to
the court at the expense of other flunkeys. When
up-standing men refuse to pay the fees kings will
recognise that- the game is up. Every now and
again the Pope makes a “ Monsignor ” in Austra-
lia. The elderly bhoy has to plank his £25 or so
to get the stamped document in his hands.

“ R.C.” : Be late Bulletin par about the
new “St. Oliver.” Dr. Plunkett was an ancestor
of the earl of Fingall who has been so lucky in
Westralian mines, and of Father Plunkett, the
Redemptorist, who died at Sydney a year or two
ago. He was Archbishop of Armagh and in those
days (end of 17th century) when a man who
brought in a priest’s head would be paid for it, he
went about in secular clothes and was known as
“Mr. Cox.” He was hanged at Tyburn on a
bogus charge of treason. A few years ago I saw
his head in a convent by the banks of the Boyne.
Colonel Lamb, who lost an eye in the
Spion Kop fiasco, closed his military career the
other week by shooting himself whilst crossing
from New York to Liverpool, aboard a Cunard
liner. The Colonel’s final arrangements were
polite and practical. He wrote a letter to the
captain :
Sir—l am extremely sorry for what I am about to do,
and for the mess I have made in the cabin. I would have
dropped over the side instead,only I was afraid you might
pick me up before I drowned.—Yours, Thomas Lamb.
P.S.: There is money in my bag to pay for the damage
done to the cabin.
A recent print of the principal monuments
in Melbourne Cemetery recalls that there is no
suitable memorial over the grave of J. B. Thurston,
the first Colonial Secretary and third Governor of
Fiji, who was buried there eight or nine years
ago, while holding office as Governor. Thurston
was the ablest and most hard-working official that
Fiji has had, and the island colonists ought not to
allow his remains to rest in an unremembered
grave. The only other British Governor interred
in Melb. Cemetery is Chas. Hotham (Vic.,
1854-5). V
“ Onlooker ” from Maoriland :
What you say (Jan. 2) about Russell as leader of the
M.L. Opposition is perfectly true, but you err in sup-
posing that the party doesn’t know as much about it as you
do. Three years ago they conferred and decided to have
no leader. But Russell occupied the seat, and, aided and
abetted by Seddon and his crowd, quietly re-established
himself as leader, never objecting when they called him
leader ; allowing himself to be interviewed in that capa-
city, and on ail ceremonial occasions rising after the
Premier just exactly as of yore. Next session he was
compelled to announce in the House that he was no
longer leader, but, notwithstanding all that, the position
remained practically unchanged. Hints have been un-
availing. Hutcheson’s platform entreaty that he would
efface himself availed not; still, like the old man of the
sea, he clings round the neck of the doomed party. For,
let the party say what it likes in Parliament, Seddon still
maintains to the country that Russell is leader. The
only thing that will break down the legend is the appear-
ance of some other man in the leader’s seat. The
job is not a nice one, but Russell’s refusal to efface him-
self, his rejection of a means of escape, his unwillingness
to “place himself in the hands of his friends,” have gone
far to loosen those ties of personal friendship which made
the position so difficult before, for now the patience of
old members is about exhausted, while the new men are, of
course, free agents. Russell is far and away the best man
for Speaker, possessing presence, voice, experience, and
a soldier’s notion of discipline. Not so sure that Seddon
would not appoint him, for in big appointments that
bulk largely in the public eye he usually does the right
thing—witness Stout, Tunbridge, Edwards, Warburton—
and then proceeds to run riot among the small ones. But,
whether Russell sits in the chair or not, there are tl ose
who say that he will not sit in the seat reserved for the
Leader of the Opposition. If, as many suppose, his
reason for declining the Speaker’s chair is laziness and a
fondness for bed—or perhaps I ought to be fair to him and
say that he is 65 —this reason even more disqualifies him
for leadership against the untirable Seddon.

AN EXPLODED HORSE-MYTH
South Queensland papers have been re-
joicing for months over the break-up of the
drought in Western Queensland. Inland, how-
ever, large areas are devoid of grass; stations are
shifting their stock to relief country; bush towns-
ships are still existing on goat and pig-weed, and
a clear .unbroken February sky indicates only too
clearly a time ahead full of menace to the
grazier.
Sundry stations in N.W. Queensland,
elated at the present price of beef, are refusing to
sell meat to neighboring townships. Result:
Nobody goes without meat all the same, and meat,
though contraband, is much cheaper than hereto-
fore. Mr. Manager Grindem rides out one even-
ing and finds a local artist skinning a bullock in a
remote corner of a paddock. Artist takes up a
rifle—“ Look ’ere, Mr. Grindem, did anyone see
you cornin’?” Grindem shortly: “No!” Artist
(pointing his gun): “Well, then, clear out, or,
by Heavens, nobody ’ll ever see you goinV’
• * * *
The “reddest” day ever experienced in
Hay, passed into obscuration on Saturday (14th
Feb.). There was a little dust blowing about all
day, but at 4 p.m. there came up from the West a
solid block of red soil which slowly rolled over the
town and put it into total darkness. Lamps had
to be lighted, and even then one barber shut up
shop altogether because he could not see his cus-
tomer in the chair, and because the sand dried up
the lather as fast as it was put on the face. A
pathetic feature about the storm was the restless-
ness of the horses in their stalls. They whinnied
and squealed most piteously all the time the place
was in darkness. Hay is always next door to
Sheol, but that day the partition was taken down,
and the two places were one, and the demons
called in affably to converse. It was some hours
before the sluggish cubic mile of suspended hot
red smellful earth crawled on to curse some place
further east, but it felt like centuries. And no
one could drink with any satisfaction to quench
his misery, for the beer was hot, and it gritted
between your tee'.h, and the sand banked up in it
as you drank it.

“ A.F.L.” . Travelling through the mallee
one is struck by the new-chum methods of con-
serving water for stock and domestic use. The
Lands Department, when cutting up new country,
should survey and mark out the most suitable
spots for tanks, and should insist on a properly-
made tank, with connecting drains, and silt tank,
as a clause in each lease. Such a thing, if insisted
upon, should save the Government thousands of
pounds in water-trains. The average Mallee
farmer cannot think for himself, his thinking
machinery being rusted through long disuse, and
the Government which wants revenue from the
dry parts must show the settler how best to stay
on the land.

“ Scutum ” : The birds mentioned by
“E.C.” (B. 14/2’/03) are not protected at present,
except perhaps finches. Galalis, parrakeets, and
all kinds of parrots, except the kins: parrot, may
be trapped from Ist January to 31st December
inclusive; magpies and butcher-birds from Ist
February to 31st July. The only Australian birds
absolutely protected are the lyre-bird, black
cockatoo, bell-bird, emu, jackass, kingfisher,
regent-bird, rifle-bird, nankin crane, native com-
panion, fish-hawk and sea-gulls of every descrip-
tion.

“ Bobrid ” Concerning “Frank D.”
(B. 31/l/’O3) and his critics. Seems to me
that Frank was out on a “leg-pulling”
expedition. The cocksure critics are inter-
esting. “E.” writes: “Only rank new chums
would waste time fiddling about with the so-
called rabbit-traps sold by ironmongers to-day.
Squatters condemned them years ago, and there
are thousands of them stocked and hung up in
sheds on N.W. stations, while the managers go at
bunny with strips of wire-netting.” Very strange
then that the “so-called rabbit-traps” were
practically unprocurable either in Melbourne or
Sydney last winter —the demand far exceeding
the supply; and does “E.” think that trappers
(the discussion excludes “managers”) are such
bloated capitalists that they take round a convoy
with them ? There are now plenty of wire-netting
traps on the market but they were never intended
to catch rabbits —only “ flats ” ! The legitimate
trapper uses only the common spring-trap. Every-
one knows rabbits are “easily shot,” but it isn’t
sport, any more than “ sooling ” the dog on to your
mother-in-law’s hens, and a perforated rabbit isn’t
always marketable. As for digging rabbits out,
only the cove who cannot afford traps takes that
on ; I know plenty of burrows which a man could
not dig out in a week. By the way, how many
trappers take the trouble to bleed their rabbits ?
But it’s worth while since the whiter the flesh the
better for sale—at least, in the Cold Country.

A correspondent (Jonathan Wigg) to the
Sydney press suggests that the occurrence of opal
deposits in Australia tells a climatic tale :
Opal owes its origin to Nature’s wanton-
ness in causing an abundant rainfall, alter,
nating with long periods of drought. It
is evident that such climatic conditions
have prevailed in portions of Australia for
a vast length of time, and the opal is Na-
ture’s intimation of this fact. The opa-
liferous deposits in this country' are in.
variably old lake beds In the interior of
the continent so-called salt-lakes—t'.e.,
lakes which contain in solution various
mineral substances dissolved by inflowing
waters from the surrounding country—are
common. Their waters, highly alkaline,
contain silica in solution, the substance of
which opal is composed. On the shores of
such lakes crystals of lime sulphate are
plentiful during dry seasons. This mineral,
known as the miner’s “kopi.” is always
found associated with opal at White Cliffs.
. . . Every level marks a period of drought
in which the lake waters were dried up,
the dissolved silica deposited in a gela-
tinous state, and subsequently hardened
by further abstraction of its water. Opal
is now being mined at White Cliffs at a
depth of over 60ft-., a fact which will help
one to form a notion of the vast ages
during which our country has endured
the blighting career of the drought fiend.
“ Chass”: “D.H.R.’s” statement (Bulle-
tin, 14/2/’O3) that ears of wheat mentioned by me
would have ripened into smut would be a very
ingenious guess, only I was stripping the field the
ears were taken from, and they contained good
hard wheat on one hand and dry smut balls on
the other. “D.H.R.’s” theory that smut is
caused by moisture is discounted by farmers’
experience, for they invariably have most smut in
dry years. Present season has been the driest
and worse for smut experienced ’by S.W. Vic
farmers. Writer saw grains of wheat taken from
Dart’s Imperial seed, the flour part of which was
streaked black and white. The grains were grey
in color and very hard. How would the uneven
moisture and sun theory pan out in that ease.

“G.S.”: A tale of gruesome cruelty
comes from Bourke way. An Afghan, travel-
ling along the Darling, loaded his camel so that
it got a sore back. This the man utterly neglected,
and the sore became as large as an ordinary tea-
tray. At last, fearing punishment for his bru-
tality, the callous wretch sewed a piece of hessian
into the poor creature, thus covering the raw part
from observation. The camel died ; and, after
its death, the hessian-covered agony was dis-
covered. Not sa the Afghan. All efforts to find
him were futile.

Up till the end of 1902 only 74 inches of
rain had fallen on the Johnstone River, N.Q.
This was looked upon as having been the driest
year on record. Inland stations that had got lOin.
in the year were accounted thrice lucky. And
yet there was only a difference in position of two
or three hundred miles.

“A.H.J.”: That dog howl and rooster-
crow story (B. 31/l/’O3) reminds me of a dog we
once had. Used to follow an old rooster round
the run wherever he went. He wouldn’t touch
him —just followed him in the rear. If rooster
stopped, dog would step. At last, rooster would
get tired and take shelter under any barrel or
boards handy, whereupon dog would walk away
quite contented. This performance would be
gone through nearly every day, though we could
never find any explanation. Sly theory was that
the dog thought the rooster wanted exercise, but
another common view of the case was that he was
lost in admiration of the bird’s tail, and was, in a
manner, mesmerised by it.

BURNING-OFF!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Our Dad has bared his hairy amis
For eighteen months and more,
He’s rung the timber—every, stick ;
Felled gum-trees by the score ;
He’s had weary days, and sorrowful days,
But at last his heart grows light,
And he doesn’t regret a drop of his sweat —
For he’s burning-off to-night!
And the Lady Dark with her myriad eyes
May peer through the curtain’d gloom
At the Flame-King’s imps, as they snarl
and gnaw
The trunks to their ashen doom;
He has dragged them up, and piled them
up,
And now, by his watch-fires bright,
The world may know there’s an end to
his woe—
Dad’s burning-off to-night!
Our good old Dad ! He will stand and
stare
In a red log’s glowing core ;
And what do you think he’s watching
there
Through the reek and heat and roar ?
He sees a harxest, he sees a home,
The goal of a pioneer’s fight;
And it’s smoke—would you bet?—makes
his eyes so wet
As he’s burning off to-night!
RITA AND L.
Ernest Favenc writes :
Re your par. (21/2 *O3) about a singular yarn in the
London Jkwisii Chroxiclk. ‘ These exploring stories that
occasionally get foisted on to gullible editors are generally
too absurd to contradict, but this one about finding an
aboriginal Jewish tribe one thousand miles beyond the
Leopold Range is so absolutely absurd that one would
think the most credulous mortal would scarcely have
taken it seriously. One thousand utiles north of the
King Leopold Range would land you well in the Malay
Archipelago, south would bring you near the Great
Bight, west would sink you far in the Indian Ocean, and
east would drop you in Queensland. The goats and
Saturday observance business would not satisfy an ordi-
nary laughing jackass, and the Hebrew prayerbook is
beneath contempt. Still it is a great pity the record
of Australian exploration should be mixed up w ith these
audacious falsehoods, as there are so many people foolish
enough to believe anything they read in print. Sydney
Klein may he a business man, and, I should imagine, a
smart one, but of his merits as an explorer no one has
yet heard. “ There ain’t so sich a person ”as the aborigi-
nal Hebrew, and the varn is not even circumstantially
told.

Dear Bulletin.—A few hints to the
amateurs discussing tea-making in your columns.
Place the quart on two sticks to cause a
draugh underneath, and. put a short stick in
the water to prevent same from getting smoked,
iut two sticks on the fire. Boil till hydatids
and other microbes bubble up with the froth
which should be removed, if necessary, with
a stick. If there is more froth than one stick can
remove use two sticks. Insert the tea. Place
twostmks crosswise through the handles and lift
on the fire. 1 hen place two sticks crosswise over
the rim to cause tea leaves to sink rapidly. Have
a stick handy to keep the Big Flat-footed New-
ehum from upsetting, and then move your ears
rapidly to and fro as you pass it in.— R. W
Paterson.
* * * •
Written often that a snake exhausts its
venom temporarily, in one bite. This is, of
course, not so. Latest instance to the contrary
comes from Ararat, and is recorded in Melb. Age
(18/2/’O3). Rabbit pursued by 10-year-old boy and
his dog took refuge in a log. A snake, who in-
habited the log, bit the boy, the rabbit and the
dog, and all three died. The rabbit and the log
must be responsible for 75 per cent, of the cases of
snake-bite recorded.

donised West Coast, where in the
townships almost every second build-
ing is a pub. As the pub-keepers and
brewers take the lead in politics,
the Parliamentary candidate who
doesn’t “lubricate” freely has no
chance. This time, however, cold-
water Isitt contested the four West
Coast electorates, just to ensure a full
poll on the liquor question, and he
didn’t even visit the place. I was
amused at the pains Bung and co. took
to impress the voters not to take Isitt
“too cheap.” They didn’t mind so
much the fate of “Rowdy ” M'Kenzie
or “Jimmy” Colvin, but they feared
lest the solid little band of anti-
liquorites should, through the indiffer-
ence of the majority, succeed in carry-
ing Reduction or No Licenses. A
drowning man could not struggle
more fearfully than did these paunchy
liquorites. The legal provision for-
bidding pubs to do business for the
first half of the election-day is flag-
rantly violated. The police dare not
enforce the liquor laws down there
because everyone’s hand would be
against them if they did. The liquor-
interest bosses municipal and county
councils, hospital-committees, etc., as
well as race-meetings and all kindred
local affairs. Yet the place has the
usual proportion of churches and other
supposed adjuncts of high civilisation.
How things must have hummed in the
“good old days” when “Dick” Sed-
don kept a calico pub. at the Big
Dam ; when a drink cost a pinch of
gold, when the earthen floor would
pan ounces to the dish, when disputes were settled
with gloveless fists and boots ! What an interest-
ing autobiography would Seddon’J be!
“A.M.” to “ Nym ” (B. 7/2/’O3) : For
years, when resident of the Upper Clarence, we
used the spoon-bait, trailing it from the stern of a
boat; and we often caught in an afternoon 40 or
more perch'from to 41b. Many who might
see this can verify it. But perch when much
“ fished ” amongst in this manner become wary.
I have often seen them dart at the revolving bait
and turn. Have caught them hooked by the eye
and frequently by the side. We also caught flat-
head, but never cod, although we have tried where
they abound.

“Thurla”: “Bob R.” (B. 31/l/’O3)
speaks of the Cape marigold as being introduced
to Australia by soldiers from S’Africa. But any
time during the past 18 years this plant—Cape
weed as it is also called—has flourished vigorously
in the Gunnedah (N.S.W.) district.

“ Awakino " : Concerning billy tea ; even
if properly made, half the time it is spoilt by
being smoked. A lid does not always cure, unless
it is an exact fit 5 but a green branch laid across
the top of the open billy is a sure preventative.
At one time I thought everybody knew tin's tip,
but of late I have several times endured smoked
tea.
• * • *
“ A.C.” re moon reflections in mirror :
Explanation is to be found in any good physics primer.
The reason is much the same as the reason for the multi-
plication of images of a candle, placed between two
mirrors. When light strikes the surface of a mirror, part
is reflected ; part penetrates, is refracted (bent as an oar
appears to be in water), and is reflected from the silvered
surface. This is the principal ray forming the image.
But part of this ray is reflected downward again from the
first surface upon the second (silvered), to be again
reflected to form a second image. The multiplication is
infinite ; but the eye appreciates only a finite number.

“ Ninnat ” : Once a chronic camper-otrfc
has acquired a taste for billy-tea he will be satisfied
with no other ; but it is the addition of the sugar
—especially coarse brown sugar—that gives the
charm, not the boiling. Uhsweetened billy-tea is
nauseous.

“L.”: That poisonous fish (8., 14/2/’O5)
was evidently a toad-fish (sapo). They are often
hauled up in nets, sometimes among shoals of
garfish or pilchards. Boys and other fiends blqw
them up and use as footballs. Toad-fish is known
to those in the trade as a handy poison for eats
and such like.

....Maoriland Correspondent: Well, Freetrade Britain
is, compared to the rest of Europe and the United States,
the country where the big landlord appears in his most
-rulent form. Most Protectionist countries have a race
of small proprietors. And when George Reid took the
duties off silks, satins, jewellery, precious stones, pic-
tures, statuary, carriages, scent, pianos, plate, and so on,
and proceeded to tax the small, drought-stricken farmer
at the same rate as the rack-renting landlord, he did
a noble thing, didn’t he ? Make no mistake—the bulls of
the big squatter crowd was with George Reid all the
time. The farmers were largely against him, but that’s
quite a different thing. The squatters are mostly Crown
leaseholders, who don’t in the least object to see the
farmer taxed Fred. S. : You give a general effect of
smartness, but there’s nothing behind to validate y0u...,

expert man than killed by an inexpert woman. Where
a false sense of modesty makes a woman reluctant to be
operated on by a male surgeon she is often assured that
a lady doctor will act; the chloroform is given and
the male surgeon comes in and operates . S.G B •

. Mr. -Percy Bates is a Sydney tenor singer of
distinction, whose benefit (preparatory to study
abroad) takes place shortly. From six years of
age Mr. Bates has been a pleasing singer—as a
boy soprano first and latter as a tenor. His first
appearance as a tenor in Sydney was in 1894, and
since then he lias been very successful as a concert
performer.
MR. PERCY BATES.
Photo , by Peels, Sydney.
T. M. Smith writes from Greymouth
(M.L.) :
With reference to your paragraph re Sangal case
(31/l/’O3, page 13). The London Globe is wrong in stating
“ that no woman temporarily reprieved for a similar
cause has ever been hanged in England.” In the seventies
the notorious poisoner, Mary Ann Cotton, sentenced to
death at the Durham assizes, was respited for some
months, yet as soon as was possible after the birth of her
child, she was duly hanged in Durham gaol, the child
having been adopted by a lady. This woman was, I
believe, the last murderess examined by a jury of
matrons.
The Bulletin Recitek has been taken by
Bill and Jim straight to their warm hearts, and
already its contents have gladdened thousands of
audiences all over Australia. Most of the best
and most famous Bulletin recitation-pieces are
included in this fine collection—price ss. post*
free from Bulletin office.
Ernest Toy.
I made my first terrestial appearance at Bendigo
—not Charters Towers—23 years ago. I struck
the latter place when I was only a few months old.
An old. Palmer diggings-man gave me my first
impression of divine melody; after this, a local
barber gave me a few wrinkles , and then Man by
took me in hand in London. When I had put in
seven years’ rough work, Ysaye advised me to
study under Hollaender at the Sternsches Con-
servatoire in Berlin. There I became leader of
the orchestra and first violin in the quartette.
“ Then I obtained an engagement at Monte
Carlo, and, on my return in the Nice express, got
my first, and I hope my last, experience with the
train-robber. But the affair is pretty ancient
now, anyway.
“On my return to London I got several good
concert engagements and some ‘at home’ work,
h had offers from H. J. James, the conductor of
the Queen’s Hall concerts, and but for my father’s
death, which meant a return to Australia, I should
be at present touring Spain with Marix Loeven-
sohn, the ’cellist.
“London? Yes, all right when you’ve got it,
but it takes a bit of getting. I had splendid
letters of introduction to people when I came back
from Germany, but they wouldn’t even hear me.
You want a stiff upper lip, I can tell you. Of
course you can get plenty of appearances if you
like to pay for them. I could have played at St.
James’ Hall, or anywhere for that matter, if I’d
taken £5 worth of tickets.
“ You want to . show people something else
besides letters of introduction. I saw Florence
Schmidt there—her voice seems fuller and richer,
and she has a good footing. I also met Mrs.
Creed, Hetty Holroyd, Kate Rooney and Maggie
Sterling, who are all doing well; but there are
others . . .
‘‘ My violin ? Yes, it’s a Gasparo di Salo, 1869,
right enough. Former fiddle came off worse than
I did when I was attacked by train robbers, and
before I left London the Queenslanders there pre-
sented me with this one. Ross Robinson (who
has known me since I was a two-year old), Lord
Lamington, and good old Sir Henry Norman
contributed their bit. She’s a clinker— ‘ a blanky
poem in wood,’ as a Charters Towers enthusiast
told me last month.”
ARNOLD DENHAM.

Disorders of the stomach are, as a rule,
caused by a torpidity of the liver and bowels
and irregularity in living. When the diges-
tion is naturally weak, as is often the case
with people of middle age and older, too much
care cannot b@ used to keep the bowels
regular, for when they become constipated
the trouble is greatly aggravated. In fact,
constipation is the prime cause of nearly
all disorders of the stomach. Keeping the
bowels regular, however, will not alone cure
these disorders. Only easily digested food
should be taken, and that moderately and
well masticated, And at regular hours.
Fully six hours should elapse between meals,
so as to give ample time for digestion and
allow the stomach a period of rest before
more food is taken. The liver and whole
digestive apparatus needs toning up, for which
purpose take two Chamberlain’s Stomach
and Liver Tablets to begin with and then
one tablet each day for a few days until the
disorder is corrected, They should be taken
immediately after supper. In case one tablet
a day does not keep the bowels free, take two j
or if one each day causes the bowels to act
too freely, take only one every other day.

Nature cannot be outraged without affording Disease and Death an opportunity to defeat her
and Disease and Death are ceaselessly waiting for opportunities. Any weakness or interruption of
the natural action of the
KIDNEYS OR LIVER
is a chance they eagerly embrace. Nature is strong, but Disease and Death are strong also. Human
intelligence and Science are INature s allies in the struggle for victory. Ignorance and Stupidity are
on the side of Disease and Death.
The Kidneys. —To the Mffr.eys belong the duty of cleansing the blood from certain impurities.
The kidneys of the average person filter ami extract from the blood about three pints of water every
day. In this water should be dissolved about an ounce of urea, ten to twelve grains, in weight, of
uric acid, and other animal and mineral matter varying from a third of an ounce to nearly an ounce
If the kidneys are weak or diseased a quantity of this refuse matter is suffered to remain in the system'
and becomes actively poisonous, causing us to suffer from Rheumat sm, Gout, Lumbago
Sciatica, Persistent Headache, Neuralgia, AnsenDa, Gravel, Stone and Bladder
Troubles. The commonest indication that there is anything wrong with the action of the
kidneys is
PAIN IN THE BACK,
but a simple test as to whether they are doing their duty efficiently is to place some urine, passed the
first thing in the morning, in a covered glass, and let it stand until next morning. If it is then cloudy,
shows a sediment like brick-dust, is of an unnatural colour, or has particles floating about in' it, then
the kidneys are weak or diseased, and steps must at once be taken to restore them to health and
activity, or Bright’s Disease, Diabetes, or some other serious illness will result.
The Liver. —The liver deals with a different class of waste matter of the body. There is
perfect sympathy between the kidneys and the liver. When the one is sick the other is sick also. If
the liver is sluggish or diseased it not only affects the kidneys, but it causes biliary poisons to remain
in the system and produce Indigestion, Biliousness, Jaundice, Sick Headache, and Blood
Disorders.
Warner’s Safe Cure.
This remarkable medicine was discovered about thirty years ago. It is the only medicine known
to science which possesses the power to act beneficially alike upon the kidneys and upon the liver.
It restores those vital organs to health and vigor, and because it does this it necessarily cures
Rheumatism, Gout, Lumbago, Sciatica, Neuralgia, Persistent Headache, Anaemia,
Stone. Gravel, Bladder Troubles, Indiges ion, Biliousness, Jaundice, Sick Head-
ache, Blood Disorders, and any disease due to the presence of urinary and biliary poisons in the
system.

Mr. Chamberlain’s Red Mark.
It must be credited to the English newspapers
that they lied manfully and well on behalf of Mr.
Chamberlain’s tour in S’Africa. All party con-
siderations were sunk to assist his enterprise. The
puff preliminary, the trumpet anticipatory, the
fiction introductory, suggested the kindly press
treatment of a poor but well-advertised theatrical
“show.” If the English newspapers could have
made a success of the Chamberlain appearance
in the role of a Peace-maker on the S’African
stage, they would have done so. But, alas it
* e - The actor was entirely un-
fitted for his part. Enthusiastic falsehoods about
his first appearances were tried, but when
the theatre began to resound with hoots and the
stage to be littered with curses, the truth had to
be told. The “show ” was a failure. It is hard
to see how any reasonable men could have been
led to hope a single moment for any other result.
Mr. Chamberlain was the Man who Made the
War, the statesman who soaked S’Africa in
British and Boer blood, and put back English
moral civilisation a century by an attack on the
little farmers’ Republics. The ex-Radical, ex-
Kepublican, ex-business man, who had crawled
on his belly into the councils of the “Gentle-
men of England,” wished to make a
mark on history as one of them. He did
—a red mark. We will do him the
justice of believing that he was not aware how red
the mark would be, that he contemplated only a
three months’ war, and then so many thousands
of miles added to the British Empire in the name
But even when the foulness of
the deeds necessary to be perpetrated was clear to
him, Mr. Chamberlain persevered. This man,
who had made up his mind to wade through a
stream of blood to his end, was not to be deterred
when the stream turned to a torrent. In short,
tne plutocrats of the Transvaal goldfields wished
to destroy the white democracy of the Transvaal
in the interests of cheap labor ; Mr. Chamberlain
wished to make a mark on history ; and there was
war. For Mr. Chamberlain afterwards to send
himself to the stricken and bleeding community
into which he had plunged the knife to the haft,
as mediator, conciliator, peacemaker, shows how
great a fool a cute business-mail can be when he
enters a field where considerations of moral senti-
ment must be given some weight.
HAT S.

“A Mainlander,” wiiting up Hobart Gaol
in Tas. News, incidentally drags in a Dunedin
(M.L.) reminiscence :
Cyrus Haley was a prisoner. A man of education and
some means, he had been a popular resident of Auckland.
That city was suddenly thrown into a state of intense
excitement by the report that he had been arrested for
attempting to murder Mr. Thomas Russell, a prominent
lawyer, and the head of many large financial and other
institutions. There was a woman in the case, of course ;
hut there was more than that, too. There had been
many mysterious fires in Auckland. Tried for attempted
murder, Haley was found guilty ; also on two, indeed, I
think, three, other indictments, all for arson—burning
down the N.Z. Insurance (Jo.’s buildings in queen-street,
and burning down the Choral Hall, in Synions-street, and
burning down a kerosene bond off the Grafton-road. Of
every one of these crimes he was convicted, and his motive
was thesame in each—revenge on Mr. Russell, oras he was
generally called “Tom” Russell, who was pecuniarily
interested in every place destroyed. Three, if not four,
periods of twenty years was the sentence of the Judge,
the much-beloved Chief-Justice Sir George Arney. He
was sent for safety, with another notorious Auckland
criminal named Plummer, down to the Dunedin gaol.
I had known Haley in Auckland. When “doing” the
Dunedin gaol I was allowed to see him, and he asked me
to get him some shorthand books. His behavior was
good— he was placed to assist the warder at the front
gate of the gaol. Taking in a supply of meat from the
contracting butcher one morning, he suddenly turned,
threw pepper into the eyes of the warder, and bolted.
He was recaptured. A second time he bolted, a year or
so later ; was called upon to stop, but refused. There-
upon the warder coolly dropped on one knee and shot
him through the back. He fell dead on his face. In its
passage the bullet had passed through a packet of photo-
graphs he had in his breast, and, as shown to me after-
wards by the governor of the gaol, every one was tinged
with blood, for the bullet had gone through them. They
were portraits of his children.
It is reckoned that there are just now 900,0C0
male workers (a large number adults and heads of
families) unemployed in the United Kingdom.
ihe_ ever-increasing. U.S., German and other
foreign competition is the worst trouble, but the

of supplies, have also helped to make this
a bitter and terrible winter-time in Britain. And
meanwhile, for the gladdening of the British
workman, who sees more and more of the goods
f lu to ma,<e imported from the Land
ot the Wooden Ham, tilings like this constantly
appear m the English papers :
The cause of Cobdenism will not, says Public Opinion,
e assisted by the statement published a few days ago by
Mr. G. D. Kelly, secretary of Manchester Trades
council, as to his recent study of industrial conditions in
America. He goes so far as to say that in the U.S. wages
are in many cases double what they are in similar trades
in England, and this difference he attributes to the
operation of Protective tariffs. To what extent those
tariffs increase the cost of living, he does not appear to
show ; but, whilst he says that Protection enhances
wages by as much as 100 per cent, in certain trades, other
students are of opinion that 10 per cent, covers the in-
creased cost of living. If that be so, many wage-earners
are 90 per cent, better off in America than in England.
Mr. Kelly like a growing body of men who originally
believed that all economic truth centred in Cobden, ad-
mits that. experience is shaking his faith in the wisdom
of adhering unconditionally to “so-called Freetrade
principles.' In America, he found trade organisations
more perfect and in the enjoyment of greater liberty than
m England, and poverty, as we know poverty, is not as
obtrusive as it is in England.

A new book on “Self-Help” might be
written with this personal confession of a London
clerk to the Daily Mail as a text
When 16 years old I worked 10 hours a day in an office,
and another five hours at home learning German and
French. At 20 I was correspondence clerk at a salary of
£1 a week. To improve my position, I learnt two more
languages and made myself a walking encyclopaedia of
facts and figures. Net result: 30s. a week in my 25th
year. Then I did some home work to increase my income,
often slaving till the early morning hours, and at the age
of 30 was making £2 10s. a week. That was the largest
salary I ever earned, and upon the strength of it I
married. Now lam 55, and my present employers value
my services at £2 a week.
“ Quiz ” : T’other day I was on a Sydney
electric tram, travelling at limit-speed between
Bridge and Hunter-sts., when a fat, big-bingied
man waved frantically for tram to stop. Driver
picked him up and, turning to me, said : “ Ain’t it
sick’ning? Fancy ’im blocking me like that!”
“What did you pick him up for?” I queried.
“Is he a departmental man?” “Not much,”
the driver said. “ They ain’t got no bow-windows
on ’em like that in the department.” “Why?”
“ ’Cos they loses ’em chasin’ us poor chumps round
all day to see we earn our screws and don’t run
over drunks.”

SLOUCH HAT.
He had an iron
spoon at birth;
He was not born
to live in ease.
He is the Tiller

He is the Toiler
of the Seas.
Down where the
sun is dim and
pale,
And grey seas
rave round icy
lands,
He battles through the bitter gale,
And takes in sail with bleeding hands.
In drawing-rooms he is not met,
With Governors he does not dine;
His feet are in the furrows set,
His lamp is shining in the mine.
Slouch Hat has sweat upon its band,
The sweat of one who toils to win
A crust by scouring sea and land —
While Tall Hat rakes the harvest in.

The last 12 months’ record of a goods-clerk
on N.S.W. railways : Regular hours, 8.15 a.m. to
6 p.m. ; back after tea to 9.30 p.m.; Saturdays,
8.15 a.m. to 6 p.m.; Sundays, 9.30 a.m. to 1 p.m.,

A. J. Wilson, the wild-eyed stormy petrel
of English finance, who is now saying unpleasant
things about our stability, made himself similarly
disagreeable in ’92. And didn’t something or
other happen in ’93 ?
In M.L. recently the Arbitration Court
has upheld the Registrar’s refusal to register a
Firemen’s Industrial Union at Wellington, on the
ground that Seamen’s Unions are already in
existence there, and the men could join these.
Premier Jenkins (S.A.) has given up his
contemplated trip to England in the interests of
the Northern Territory Ninety Million-Acre Land
Steal Railway. Opposition leader John Darling,
however, sailed for the other side of the world
this week, and he will do all he can to secure the
“float.”
The syndicates are lying in wait in
Queensland. Electric tram rights in Rockhamp-
ton, Townsville and Charters Towers are three
steals contemplated. The middlemen of those
towns, with the Brisbane example before them,
should be electrocuted if they allow the deals to
come off.
According to the Journal of Maoriland
Department of Labor there are 3,393 men
employed on co-operative works in M.L., and the
outlook is such that Seddon promptly resumed two
big estates on which to settle these men when the
work is done. During the month 772 unemployed,
with 804 other people dependent upon them, were
assisted by the Govt. Only 38 were sent to
private employment, the other 713 being put on
to State works paid for out of loans.
One of the portions of Premier Barton's
last speech most carefully omitted in the Free-
trade daily paper • "'orts was the long list of
new factories that have been put up in and around
Sydney since the advent of the Federal Protective
tariff. They represented an expenditure of over
£1,000,000. The silence of the Sydney dailies on
this item was phenomenal. The great art of the
Sup-Press is to leave out all the strong parts of
your enemy’s speech, and then complain that the
speech is weak.

Public demonstrations in Queensland
(the first for years) indicate the increasing unpop-
ularity of the Philp Government. The business
people, enraged at the income-tax, are joining
hands with the workers who are disgusted with
the poll-tax, and are raising a vast uproar. If an
election took place this year, the poll - taxers
would certainly go down amid universal execra-
tion.
Telegrams from Tasmania allege that
Premier Lewis is almost certain to go down be-
cause he imposed an income tax to square the
public accounts. Probably if he added the deficits
to the public debt he would come back with
a big majority. Philp, of Queensland, who sat
securely enough when he only added the shortages
to the public debt, is likely, also, to find his
Waterloo by reason of his income tax.
Westralian Senators Pearce and DeLargie
have drawn the attention of the Prime Minister
to the number of Asiatics finding admittance to
W.A. in spite of the Aliens Immigration Act.
During 11 months of 1902 no less than 786 Asiatics
entered the State The administration of the Act
in W.A, must be seriously at fault.

I was right both times.” Such an interjection
right at the beginning of the harangue would
have knocked out anyone but the Wriggler.
A London correspondent to an Australian
Freetrade paper mourning over the crumbling
away of Cobdenism in the Cold Country:—
For the first time within my recollection or within the
scope of my reading, an British Government has entered
into a business and financial partnership with a private
firm, under which the latter is subsidised at the cost of
the taxpayer. The simple fact is that the British Govern-
ment is deliberately entering upon a policy of steamship
bounties, which, if persisted in, must eventually extend
to the whole of England’s mercantile marine.
George Reid made this luckless remark in
Westralia:
I believe that the public is more honestly, more equally,
reflected in the Government and in the politics of Aus-
tralia than in any other country in the world.
And the result of that honest and equal reflection
of the public in the Government of Australia is
that Barton, Kingston, O’Connor, Bill Lyne, and
George Turner run the show, while Reid plods
round the country with a futile yarn and a stale
joke and a dry dog.
The beginning of a Great Lie. Mr.
George Reid, in the Sydney D.T. on Monday
week:—
Some of the Acts of the Federal Parliament have pre-
disposed the British public to look rather sourly upon
Australia, and things Australian, at the present time. I
especially allude to the imprisonment of the six hatters.
It won’t take very long, at the present rate (“the
exclusion of the six hatters,” which was a false-
hood, has grown in a month or two to the “im-
prisonment of the six hatters,” which is a lie) for
the George Reid-DAiLY Telegraph version of the
hatters episode to state that the unfortunate men
were flogged and hanged, and their wives sold
into slavery. And that will be the Complete Lie,
and will be seriously believed by some. It’s not
Freetrade tactics to spring the Complete Lie on
the community suddenly, but to let it grow
gradually to its full height of mendacity.
Maoriland has set apart a special settle-
ment for its returned Cohentingenter, which is a
sensible and praiseworthy
method of dealing with
that enthusiastic, but not
too level-headed, person.
All the same, the Lord
High Jingo Seddon is
not going to make him a
present of the soil, so
that he can dispose
of it and have a
red and roaring time
on the proceeds,
and then come back
and camp once
more on the
Ministerial
doorstep. The
land, which is
situated in the
Auckland dis-
trict, has been
surveyed into
blocks of not more
than 320 acres each,
and is to be disposed
of by lease at a rental
of about 4 per cent, on
capital value. Improve-
ments worth 10 per cent, of
f the value of the land have to
be put on during the first
year ; another 10 per cent, worth
of improvements within two years.
Within six years, the permanent im-
provements erected must equal in value the
whole cost of the land. Also, the ex-Cohen-
tingenter has to reside on the selection, and
rear his family there, if he has any. Altogether,
the scheme seems carefully devised to anchor
down a troublesome mendicant at a safe distance
from the Treasury.
“Vul.” : A batch of Adelaide amateur
military—aw !—officah persons went out of their
way, t’other day, to show their contempt for the
common private fellah. The new staff-officer,
whilst conducting a class of instruction, requested
the officers to fall in with a squad of non-coms.,
but the superiah ones declined point-blank on the
ground that it was beneath their alleged dignity
to do so ; and on the instructor insisting, left the
ground. The amusing part of it is that, outside
the ranks, as often as not, the private is the boss,
and the superiah officah person is an inky-pawed
“menial” who follows him about with the ledger
and humbly says “ sir ” every two words. It may
surprise this box of tin-toy warriors to know that
even the ultra-superiah English officahs take their
place in the ranks for instruction in the elements
—otherwise, not knowing, how could they teach ?
The British Resident Commissioner in the
New Hebrides explains why it is dangerous for
many Kanakas now in Queensland to return to
their homes:—
A great many of the natives had left the villages on
account of having done something for which they
deserved punishment, and as these things are never
forgotten by the natives it would be awkward for these
men t'o return. _
In other words, the Commissioner _ state that
Queensland under the Kanaka system is the dump-
ing ground for Polynesian criminals.
It may be only the new-comer’s usual
“gallery” effort, but Victoria’s newest Larnds
Minister, J. W. Taverner, seems to be going into
the land settlement business with vim. Finding
nearly 1000 applications for land waiting m the
department he has determined to have 100, OUO
acres made available almost immediately, lnclud-
ing areas in the Mt. Fatigue, Fumma, and the
rich but heavily timbered Beach Forest districts.
Also, he proposes the sub-division of some 50,000
acres of “Blue Block” country between Hamilton
and Horsham (held under a grazing licence which
expires in June); but this is an item that does
not look too promising. Anyhow Taverner doesn t
share M‘Kenzie’s idea that the duty of a Lands
Minister is to get big areas for himself and his son.
The report of the Vic. Parliamentary com-
mittee in the M‘Kenzie case is a fine, unconscious
satire on the term “ adequate
rental,” as applied to grazing
areas. In one section of its report
the committee says:—
The block of 14,000 acres was put up
for tender, and Mr. M‘Kenzie’s tender of
£5 per annum was accepted. Adjoining
this 14,000-acre block was another block
of Crown lands, containing about 7300
acres. . . . On Bth Sept., 1902, Mr.
M'Kenzie offered £5 for a grazing license
from Ist Sept., 1902, to 30th June, 1903,
and two days later the offer was ac-
cepted. On 10th Sept, Mr. M‘Kenzie
definitely instructed his agents to dispose
of his grazing rights in both the 14,000-
acre block and and 7300-acre block. . . .
He did sub-let the united blocks to Mr.
Hugh Ross at a profit of nearly £lOOO.
Then, in their conclusions from the
evidence, the committee “ acquits
Mr. M‘Kenzie of obtaining any
grazing license for himself or for
any relativeat an inadequate rental.”
The land he licensed for £5 per
annum for two-thirds of it, and £5 per half-
year for the remaining one-third of it, was
sub let to Mr. Ross at £lOOO for 10 months. Yet
he had not obtained those blocks at an inadequate
rental l
The famous Cloncurry syndicate line that
was to revolutionise the Gulf country, turn Nor-
manton into a flourishing seaport, and make
Cloncurry into a busy mining town of 10,000 in-
habitants, still hangs fire. It hangs just as do
the Tasmanian Great Western railway, and
Snow’s Adelaide trams, and the other works of
Private Enterprise in general. The time for the
forfeiture of the Cloncurry syndicate's =£lo,ooo
deposit is rapidly approaching, and the Philp
Government said it would forfeit with Roman
sternness if the syndicate didn’t keep faith.
A curious sequel to the closing of the
Bowen (Q.) railway on the ground that it didn’t
pay working expenses is the application of a
station-master and an engine-fitter, formerly em-
ployed on the line, to lease the whole outfit and
run a fair service. The terms are not yet dis-
closed, but the applicants are willing to take over
the present rolling-stock, charge the same old
rates, and to keep the line in repair. They are
said to be highly qualified officers, and they know
the line thoroughly, so evidently they reckon that
it can be made to pay—if there is any serious
desire that it should pay.
Speaking at Melbourne, t’other night, G.
H. Reid stated that he had seen a newspaper
ad. wherein someone offered £lOO for a billet in
that city. This as an instance of the scarcity of
work there. Responding to an interjector, George
said the man must have come from N.S. W., where
he could save £lOO. But the ordinary man would
have stayed in N.S.W., where he had saved the
£lOO, and make another £lOO, instead of trying to
“blew” it on a presumably unremunerative job
in bankrupt Victoria. If, as Reid asserts, a
N.S.W. man will give £lOO to get to Victoria,
what has happened to N.S.W.?
Sydney D.T. (which, it is well to re-
member, assisted to drive George Reid from
office in N.S.W., on the ground of his political
dishonesty, when Bill Lyne and Randal Carey
were such dear friends) now solemnly lectures, in
the blessed name of political morality, Labor
leader Watson for suggesting that it is “bad
tactics’’for Mr. Reid to raise the tariff issue at
next Federal elections. Mr. Reid, the D.T. says,
is too noble a man to consider tactics as against
principles, and the D.T. says it is too noble a
paper to countenance such a thing. The humor
of this needs no explanation.
The pamphlet of the Jenkins land grant
railway makes big statements as to the possi-
bilities of the Northern Territory. It has 30 good
rivers, one has some codfish in it, still another
some perch, and all have grass in them, if nothing
else. There are also to be found several creeks,
and also some coal. Diamonds of untold worth
are somewhere about, and gold is rich—simply
rich —in Macdonnell ranges. In addition, there
are said to be emus, cockatoos, an occasional
kangaroo and wallaby, and quite a profusion
of native dogs, rabbits, locusts, and snakes. The
Northern Territory has never had such a “lift”
in its life before.
N.S.W. Full Court has decided that, in
spite of section 32 of the Industrial Arbitration
Act (that section says in effect that there shall be
absolutely no appeal from the Arbitration Court),
it has power to prohibit the Arbitration Court on
appeal. The Bulletin recollects an A.W.U.
correspondent feeling very scornful towards this
paper for maintaining what the Full Court has
now declared. It’s a very difficult thing to
establish a court of absolute and unchallengeable
jurisdiction : there are so many constitutional unfl.
legal obstacles. A Parliament may pride itself
on the fact that it has done so, but generally finds-
that it has made a mistake.
Lascelles, the Mallee boomster, has a.
grievance against Vic. railway department because
it won’t carry seed-wheat for his distressed
cookies gratis. According to Lascelles, it is no
wonder the railways don’t pay, when the managers
are not previous enough to realise what a great
increase of traffic the seed-wheat would bring the
railways in from next year’s crop, and how it would
pay well for the concession. But the trouble is that
the agriculturist has already got the wheat rate
down below paying point, and the joy of carrying
seed for nothing this year, in order that there
may be more wheat to carry at a loss next year,
doesn’t appeal to a department that is always
being asked why it can’t make the lines pay.
The officers of the Vic. railways are making
gallant efforts to carry out Bent’s 5-days-a-week
scheme. This is one locomotive man’s time for a
recent week:—Monday, 13 hours; Tuesday, 13
hours; Wednesday, 22$ hours ; Thursday, 9
hours; Friday, the day off ; Saturday, 13 hours.
The system that compels engine-men to work 22$
hours out of the 24 in order that one day a week
may be shown in the time-books as “off-duty”
opens up a lot of chances for the travelling public.
Apart from considerations of public safety, the

“ Chasselas ” : Noteworthy in Vic. the
number of electors who favor the abolition of the
State Parliament. It a vote were taken that
unsatisfactory institution would probably go by
the board. Naturally in this matter the people
are ahead of politicians and newspapers.
Suggested in some quarters that the pre-
sent unrest in Morocco is primarily due to an idea
among the Moors that the time is ripe now for a
movement to reconquer Spain. Great Moorish
families still cherish the title-deeds of the pro-
perties in Spain from which religious bigotry drove
them 400 years ago.
G. H. Reid, who obediently yelled in
chorus with the foreign importers over the case
of the imported hatters, is now trimming thereon
in his best yes-no style. Federal Labor leader
Watson has publicly demanded that George will
give a straight reply to three straight questions:
(1) Does he, or does he not, object to the clause in the
Immigration Restriction Bill relating to the importation
of contract labor ?
(2) If he does, why did he not say so at the time, and not
allow the clause to go through Parliament with Pumpkin
Smith’s blessing, which was the only criticism his party
bestowed upon it?
> (3) If he does not, what is he kicking up all the row
about ?
To this challenge Reid now says that he warmly
approves of the law in question, which is a very
nasty knock to the Freetrade press. All he objects
to is the fact that the Barton Government en-
forced the law.
One member's contribution to the recent
land debate in Yic. Assembly:—
“ One selector might get 50,000 acres that won’t carry
1000 sheep to the acre.” Members laughed. “What I
meant,” resumed the hon. member, “ is that one man
might get 5000 acres that won’t carry even 5000 sheep to
the acre." The laughter became louder. “I meant to
say," again resumed Mr. Thomson, “ that one man might
get 50,000 acres that won’t carry 1000 sheep, while another
man might get a block that won’t carry 5000 sheep to
5000 sheep.”
Then he stopped just at the most interesting
point. If he had continued he might have ex-
plained that 5000 selectors might get one sheep
that couldn’t carry an acre to the selector; and
that 5000 acres might be owned by a selector who
couldn’t carry one acre to a sheep; and that a
man with 50,000 acres might have a sheep that
couldn’t carry a selector to the public-house ; and
so on indefinitely.
G. H. Reid, at Melbourne ;
I have a dim recollection that I brought the Federation
movement down from the clouds, and enabled the people
to deal with it as a matter that directly concerned them-
selves.
Probably ’twas George’s modesty that prevented
him from mentioning the politician who shackled
the aforesaid movement with the 80,000 minimum,
and then “laid it out” with a “Yes-No ’’under
the ear. And doubtless it would have been
unbecoming in him to even allude to the cost and
vexation of the second referendum, entailed by the
above circumstances; or to the fact that he got
the proviso inserted that the capital must be at
least 100 miles from Sydney ; or to the faot that
he procured the rejection of the measure at first
by reason of the Braddon clause, and then pro-
cured its re-insertion. Even the owner of the
Unwetted Fiscal Dog hadn’t the impudence to
re-call the whole story.

Lately a Victorian railway employee was
charged with stealing-four biscuits, valued at a
penny, from a case in the goods shed, and was
fined .£lO. The same day another railway man
was charged with stealing five ofcandles,
valued at half-a-crown, from the same place, and
fined £5.
The Westralian postmaster who shook the
infernal machine that almost immediately after-
wards blew Mr. Lowmes’ arm off had heaps of
luck. When Lownes got hold of the thing he
could not get the top off the case, and pulled it
with all his strength. When it did give, the
movement fired the cap. and the dreadful thing
blew up. The miscreant who sent it was evidently
brought up on the cheap and lurid melodrama.
The machine was wrapped in a newspaper con-
taining a gore-and-yell yarn entitled “Guilty or
Not Guilty,” and the hunk of the yarn that still
adhered seemed to have some significance. It
ran so:
Desperate diseases require desperate remedies, and you
are the man I have pitched upon. Either make or un-
make yourself. It has employed my invention for some
time to find out a method to destroy another without
exposing my own life and deify the law.
Martin’s Apiol and steel pills are sold by
all chemists throughout Australasia. Not genuine
without the signature. Be sure you get Martin’s.
Sole proprietor, W. Martin, pharmaceutical
chemist, Southampton, England.*
The comments of J udge Docker, at Par-
ramatta (N.S. W.) Quarter Sessions recently, on the
moral depravity of the qpuntry, seem to have been
warranted in the case referred to at any rate. A
young man, 18 years of age, was charged with
indecent assault on a girl of 13. Evidence re-
vealed a shocking state of brothers and sisters
sleeping together in one room ; while the assaulted
one abode in another room with her parents.
There wasn’t a “drawing-room ” in the house, yet
there was a piano. Altogether an absence of
moral ideals pervaded everything, Medical evi-
dence suggested that the girl was rather in the
habit of being assaulted than otherwise. The
strange mixture of squalor, neglect, moral ignor-
ance and piano seem to show that a musical instru-
ment is no remedy for a soul diseased. The
accused was sent “forward” for six months to
reflect and become wiser.

Some statements made in the Victorian
Assembly about the State’s wonderful railways:—
Fifty of the 350 engines on the roads were not fit to be
in running. Day after day engines were hours late in
being brought out of the sheds, owing to their crippled
condition ; and on one day there was not a passenger
locomotive in fit order to take a train out of Melbourne.
The department was, therefore, forced to use goods
engines. Two engines had to be used to take a train to
Bendigo, and were put in the repairing sheds immediately
they got there. Two other engines had to be employed
to bring the train back, and they also had to be laid up
as soon as they reached Melbourne. In one case two bags
of bran ha!d to be put into the boiler to close up the leaky
pipes ; while in another instance it was found on a journey
that the wheel of an engine was loose.
It was Labor member Prendergast who drew this
picture, but Railway Minister Bent put the
finishing touches to it by. admitting that, out of
]the State’s 544 locomotives, only 354 were fit to be
bn the road. And the inopportune Shiels has just
annexed a large part of the Railway Accident
Fund, which looks as if it might be badly wanted
soon.

NORTH WEST COAST.
mHE VAX DIEMEN’S LAND COMPANY is prepared to
-*■ receive tenders for the renting of portions of their
estates situated at the Surrey Hills and to let parts of the
Hampshire Hills, and farms near Burnie.
The lands consist of rich basaltic soil, well-grassed and
timbered, and abundantly watered by numerous per-
manent rivers and streams.
The Surrey Hills Block, extending to 150,000 acres, has
late'y been re-surveved and divided into 11 Blocks vary-
ing in extent from 2000 to 24,000 acres, to suit the re-
quirements of those tendering.
The Hampshire Hills Block (10,000 acres) will also
shortly be surveyed, and will be dealt with in smaller
'°The lands are very favorably situated and are served
by direct railway communication with the Port of Bumie
whence there is "a regular Intercolonial Steam Service.
This railway passes through the full extent of the
lands to be let and provides ready means for the trans-
port of cattle and goods to Mount Bischoff, Zeehan, and
the Western Mineral Fields.
It can also be arranged to let, if desired, smalleruleared
farms near the Coast, or houses to suit requirements
would be built in or near Burnie.

Some Aspects of the Tariff Issue.
The cause of Protection would have no reason to
fear the result of an acceptance of the flamboyant
challenge of George Reid to make the tariff
the issue at the next Federal elections. What
measure of Protection exists now, exists despite
bitterly unpatriotic efforts of the Freetraders, and
largely because of the financial exigencies of the
Commonwealth. A Reid Government could only
interfere with it to a very slight extent, and, in
e , V , en f of ._ sucl > a Political calamity as Reid
rule, the tariff would be, at the worst, modified a
httle m the direction of favoring the foreigner
and throwing our native laborers to sand-hill re-
On tuI 01 t| S a 'k d soup-kitchens for maintenance.
On the other hand a straight fight over the tariff
issue—at the last elections there was no such
dominant issue, the Protectionists making them-
selves a party to an understanding that the
fisca issue should be the subject of a com) omise
for the sake of more securely laying the founda-
tions of the Commonwealth system of legislation
could hardly result in anything else than such a
decisive declaration in favor of a national policy
as would justify the Government in improving
and strengthening the existing barriers against
the products of foreign labor. It is, however
unfortunately, not at all a matter of certainty that
a direct contest on the high tariff v. low tariff
issue will be secured. There is a possibility, if
not a probability, that the existing Barton
Government, or whatever modification of it leads
the National party, will confuse the issue
with the question of preferential trade with
Britain. It is only from the linking of such
a proposal with high-tariff advocacy that
Protectionists have to fear. Without much
doubt the majority of the National Protection
party views the suggestion to make gratuitous
tariff concessions to Britain with disfavor. The
conclusion of a reciprocity treaty, if Britain
had adopted Protection, would probably be advis-
able. The granting to our most formidable
manufacturing rival of Freetrade privileges, or
concessions tending thereto, for no return at all,
would be an absolute negation of Protection. The
Barton Government, or whatever combination
succeeds the Barton Government when the
almost certain reorganisation of the Cabinet takes
place, may wreck the cause of Protection, and
help to power the mongrel “low-tariff” system
suggested by Mr. Yes-No Reid, by adding to the
Australian National platform an absurd and con-
tradictory proposal for fostering English manu-
factures at the expense of Australian labor. But,
failing that event, a tariff contest will surely
affirm high Protection.
• • • *
The present tariff, it cannot be too often pointed
out, does not provide high Protection. YVhat
Australia needs is a practica ly prohibitive tariff
on the lines of the M’Kinley 'Act in the U.S., a
taritr which will protect every industry from shoe-
making to pin-manufacture, from locomotive con-
struction to glass-moulding, that is reasonably
possible of success. Australia, with large coal,
iron, copper and other metal resources, but
with, on the whole, poor agricultural prospects, is
marked out by Nature for a manufacturing
country; and her rulers, by involving her deeply in
debt, have assisted to make the manufacturing road
the only one to prosperity, indeed the only one to
avoid ultimate bankruptcy. The Commonwealth
is in the position of a community with a vast
and varied estate, but under the burden of an
enormous mortgage. It must, to struggle back
to solvency, decide to make for itself —even if at
the expense of some unnecessary labor, and at the
sacrifice of some beauty of style in the product—
all or nearly all that it requires. A poor rack-
rented tenant finds it necessary to get his children
to be bootmakers, tailors, blacksmiths, and so on,
for the family, at the cost of much effort, and at
the loss of much elegance, simply because he
cannot afford to pay outsiders the money fordoing
the work. Australia is practically in that posi-
tion, and as a matter of self-preservation, let
alone of ultimately securing prosperity, must
decide very soon to live a self-contained life, to
make for herself practically all that she needs,
and to do without what she cannot produce
within her own borders by her own labor. The
way to that consummation is by a prohibitive
tariff —a system of Protection such as that which
has made the United States tho greatest country
of modern civilisation.
• * • •
The present Federal tariff falls very much short
of that. It fails to protect sufficiently the great
iron industries which could give employment to
at least 100,000 wage-earners in the Common-
wealth; it palters with the question of nurturing
into existence textile industries and still allows
not only the cottons but the woollen goods of the
people to be manufactured abroad; printing,
furniture-making, shoe-making, glass-working,
shipbuilding, carriage-making, the marino
transport trade all these as yet lack
efficient and thorough Protection. No
printed matter, iron goods (except a few
patented articles), textile fabrics, shoes, furniture,
glass-ware, or pottery should come into the
Commonwealth from abroad, except to gratify
some few faddists willing to pay dearly for
the privilege of importing. No carriage
between Australian ports should be allowed l y
vessels not wholly owned and manned in Austral-
asia, and, ultimately, by vessels not built in
Australia. Australian banking and insurance
institutions should be protected against the
competition of concerns owned outside. Pro-
ceeding further, by adequate protective legis-
lation in regard to patents and copyrights
such as exists in Germany and America, a
fair share could be secured for Australia of the
work in connection with the manufacture of
patented articles and the production of copyright
wxiks. . Adequate, scientific, comprehensive
Protection would provide for all that, and
m doing so not only add enormously to the
population and tax-paying ability of Australia,
but also make the task of meeting our oppressive
loans easy, by allowing practically all our exports
of staples to be devoted to the payment of interest
and principal, instead of, as now, being largely
absorbed in payment for goods which we could
manufacture ourselves,
• • • *
Such a system of National Protection only needs
properly expounded and championed by
public men to find ready and general acceptance
by all classes of the Australian community, except
that small section of linen-drapery soul and foreign
sympathies which gets its living by importing.
Put Protection has always in Australia lacked
adequate political and press championship. Our
public men have generally been bound more or
less to England, and consequently to the
English Freetrade fetish. Even so-called Protec-
tionist politicians, from lack of knowledge, from
enslavement to some paltry need of the moment,
from want of courage or from foreign sympathy—
or from a combination of all or some of these—
have, as a rule, been bad Nationalists. To cite
an instance: If in N.S. Wales there should be a
genuine Protectionist in politics, it is E. W
O Sullivan, at present Minister for Works. He
has climbed into prominence as an apostle of Pro-
tection and of high wages. Protection, and Pro-
tection alone, has given him political life. Yet at
a recent test of the genuineness of his faith, he
was guilty of a shameful desertion. For the new
reservoir for the Sydney water-works, construction
of which has just been put in hand, 75,000 barrels
of cement were required. The Federal Govern-
ment, to protect the Australian cement industry,
to allow here the payment of a high rate of
wages, imposed a protective duty of 2s. 6d. a
barrel on this article. When tenders were opened
for the supply of 75,000 barrels—the production
of which would have found employment for along
term for many hundreds of workers —local manu-
facturers offered at 12s. 6d. a barrel, the foreigners
at 13s. By the curious arrangements under the
Braddox clause of the Australian Constitution, of
the 2s. 6d. duty paid on the foreign importation,
three-fourths (Is. 10£d.) would go back to the
State revenue of N.S. Wales. Mr. O’Sullivan
proposed to purchase the whole supply from the
foreigner (out of loan money, by the way—the re-
bate in duty would, however, go to revenue), and it
was only’ in response to indignant representations
by the Chamber of Manufacturers that he modified
that intention and split up the order so that 25,000
barrels went to each of two local manufacturers
and 25,000 to the foreigner. The loss of wages to
the N.S.W. workers by this action of a so-called
Protectionist Minister was at least £lO,OOO. Had
he carried out his original plan, it would have
been at least £30,000. And the advantage to be
gained by this betrayal of Protection was, pre-
sumably, that of indirectly securing a certain
amount of loan-money for the current revenue
account. It was only by threats of a public agita-
tion that Mr. O’Sullivan was induced to abandon
his original intention of giving the whole order to
the foreigner, at a time when the clamor of the
unemployed filled the air and the Minister was
posing as the sincere friend of the workers.
* • • •
When in a matter of administration a so-called
Protectionist can thus seek to go behind the back
of the Australian Parliament, and nullify the
arrangements it has made for the Protection
of the Australian worker from the cheap
foreign laborer, it is clear that the National cause
needs to be on guard against secret as well as open
foes. But Protection will one day find in Aus-
tralia its M ‘Kinley ; Nature has clearly intended —
and political circumstances have strengthened the
intention—that Australia should commit itsdestiny
to a career as a great manufacturing country.
When a sincere, earnest and able politician recog-
nises that, and makes a prohibitive tariff the aim
of his public life, he will win lasting power for
himself and lasting prosperity for his country. It
is only the treacheries, the weaknesses and the
ignorance of so-called “Protectionist” politicians
that have allowed the Freetrade idea to survive to
this century in Australia.

BUCHANAN WHISKY.
A Parson’s Gratitude To His Doctor.
A good deed deserves thanks. If the deed is
one that has been of great help, one that has
rescued a man from imminent danger, then the
thanks should be of an overwhelming nature.
Gratitude for a service rendered shows appre-
ciation for it, and is moreover edifying to those
who have performed the deed. The Rev, T. B.
Swift, a well-known and highly respected reverend
gentleman, of Alexandra, Vic., has just expressed
himself in glowing terms for the great goodness
that has befallen him. For a longtime he suffered
all the agonies that constipation and indigestion
bring in their train. He long sought a cure for
the pain that tormented him, and although he
tried high and low for a remedy, none was forth-
coming At last, almost as though by the work-
ing of Providence, Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills
were brought to his notice. Then, and not till
then, did he recover his health and with it his best
joy and happiness. So pleased was he with his
complete cure, that he wrote to the proprietors at
once, wishing them to publish broadcast the good
news, in order that others might be similarly
benefited. “I am now in my eighty-first year,”
he says, “ and for 52 years I have preached the
Gospel. For years I suffered from constipation
and indigestion, and it was not until recently,

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Caulfield Futurity Stakes provided a new sen-
sation in bumping finishes. After Wakeful, the odds-on
favorite, had been apparently outpaced, the gay colors of
Emir, Sir Leonard, and Chantress were seen to be bunched
at the head of the field. Emir ran next to the rails. Sir
Leonard and Chantress were jostling together on the out-
side of the plucky two-year-old. Some 50yds from home
Emir got knocked out of his stride, and as the other two
strugglers passed him, a small boy fell out of the saddle.
It was Emir’s five stone pilot who had dropped like a
parcel on to the course. Meanwhile, Sir Leonard was
Seating Chantress by a neck, with Wakeful and the
riderless Emir three lengths behind them. In the hurly-
burly Chantress must have got her face jammed, smacked,
or what not, for she returned to the paddock bleeding
slightly at the mouth. Hence the protest against Sir
Leonard, which was dismissed.
• » • •
The Futurity Stakes result suggests that if Sir
Leonard and Chantress could show such a marked
improvement on their recent “ form,” other Newmarket
candidates may be expected to do likewise. There is no
“ real good thing ” with an undeniable claim to favoritism
for this year’s Newmarket. Chantress is a beautiful little
mare—the very cut of a Caulfield Cup winner—but over
the straight 6 furlongs at Flemington the longer-striding
Sir Leonard is likely to beat her again. And Sir Leonard
is no cert, with 9.8 to steady him. Purser looks none
the better for the fact that Wakeful, who beat
him on the previous Saturday, was vanquished in
the Futurity. Dulcimer’s chance has lost a little
of its “ papier’’-weight now that the Oakleigh Plate
running is known to have been even less genuine than it
appeared at the time. Present scribe believes in what
he has seen as regards Sir Leonard and Chantress, and
is impressed by what he has heard re Maharani, Tip Top
and Drawbridge. Preferring those five fancies to Purser,
Dulcimer, and several others, he guesses wildly at
1. Maharani
2. Sir Lrosard
3. Tip Top
4. Chantress.
One must guess at something, although anything may
win.
• • • •
As for the Australian Cup, it promises at time
Of writing to be a sufficiently interesting race. Among
the older horses Flagship, Footbolt, Australian,_ Vanity
Fair, Patronus, Acrasia, and Dulcimer are “in” it. The
St. Leger on Saturday should convey a notion as to the
relative strength of Abundance, Great Scot and Rienzi.
One of the 3-year-olds will probably win the Cup next
Tuesday—say Great Scot.
• • • •
Kiota, if he keeps well, will have very little to
beat over the big sticks at Flemington. The jumping
events don’t promise to be good sport, but the w.f.a.
races may provide a lot more excitement than usual—
which is not saying too much. Everything with any pre-
tensions to first-class character has been entered for two
r three of w.f.a. prizes, and will probably have a go for
one of them at least.

The added money for the four days of the
V.R.C. Autumn Meeting amounts to £ll,OOO or there-
abouts—an average of about £450 per race. And the
distribution of the coin is calculated to encourage the
man who has a good gee-gee and runs it straight. Un-
fortunately the honest intelligent owner is himself a rare
animal.

Fair, and will, in that case, be a Champion trial between

Essendon and the Champion.
• * * •
Manner in which big Sydney Tatt. s Mo.
follows the lead of his small shop brother is amusing.
Half-sov. will often bring a nag down two or three points
in the shop, and double that sum frequently promotes an
outsider to first favorite. Yet every week sees layers
making favorites on the course of nags the shops have at
shortest prices A case in point. Paradenyia did a
decent gallop—nothing out of the way ; but someone saw
it and talked, with the result that a few pounds went on
on the off chance, and the mare was soon very “ hot.”
Mo., as is his custom, opened up with Paradenyia
favorite, thus giving people behind the ultimate winner
Beaufleur a chance to back him down from tens to threes,
which they did with heaps of spirit. As there was little
or no coin on the favorite of their own creation the
raucous voiced fell in badly
• • • •
How Sydney shops bet. Recently a Randwick
trainer, desirous of backing a horse of his quoted 10 to 1,
sent a friend along with a fiver, the whole of which that
individual foolishly endeavored to “ get on ” at ’he first
shop. But they’d'only lay the odds to £l, and when the
“commissioner” fled next door he found 4 to 1 the
best available, and it was the same, or something close
thereto, everywhere else. The shop first entered had
sounded the alarm by telephone.
• • • *
True Blue—Specula colt Blue Spec got away
with Rosehill Hep., Saturday, but only after a tough
struggle from nearly 5 furlongs from home, and in
something the same* fashion as Acrasia won Saturday
before at Warwick Farm. Equator, Silver Ray, and
Redeemed were tackled and squelched in turn, and then
Wootton’s big deceiver, Marathon, came, full of ginger,
in the last 220 yards, but Blue Spec landed the prize well,
and evidenced grit of no common order. Though trying
often, and shaping well mostly, Blue Spec hasn’t been
first past the post since appropriating 2-year-old Maiden
Stakes at A. J.O. meet March last-
