What punters have to contend with. Heavily

was so lame that she had to be withdrawn. Mura never
got near the starting post, but her backers lost their
money through some rule of the Mo.-protecting Tatt.’s
Club.

At Christchurch, Saturday, M.L. champion
trotter Ribbonwood cut Australian mile down from 2.13
to 2.11, and 2 miles from 4.43 to 4.35 4-5. This has
brought additional interest to match ’tween Ribbonwood
and N.S.W. top-notcher Fritz. M.L. people believed
Ribbonwood capable of 2.10. Fritz hasn’t done better
publicly than 2.13, but those behind him insist that he’ll
improve materially when it is wanted. Ribbonwood is a
youngster, and Fritz an old ’un, and consequently less
liable to break.

A case of hard luck. At last race meeting at
Forbes (N.S.W.), Algy was the only acceptor for the
Welter Handicap. The jockey walked him leisurely
round the course, but on being weighed in be was found
to be short of weight, and it was declared no race.

’Murkan Jock Ames, in Sydney months ago,

nobody else could get home.

• • • •
Sydney trainer Fielder’s crack colt Famous,
backed early for Newmarket Handicap, and taken in
heaps of doubles with Australian Cup nags, was scratched
Monday, owing to his having broken away and getting
badly knocked about after running in Futurity Stakes,
Saturday. Famous, like most of his origin, is too un-
reliable to consider seriously, though he does brilliant
work on the track ; he first shaped miserably in St.
George’s Stakes, and then, after two or three excellent
gallops, was backed for Oakleigh Plate, but failed again.
Subsequently, on the track, he downed the Futurity
Stakes winner, Sir Leonard, and thus tempted further
support from his party. Probably it would be a good
thing for all concerned, bar Mo., if Famous never raced
again.

• • • •
“Le Chasse ” ; — Re Ancient Bookie Davis
(8., 14/2/’O3), he was certainly the first to earn the term
“ Leviathan,” and his career shows how much more
valuable pluck and shrewd horse sense are to a man in his
sphere of life than blue blood and scholarship. Davis was
originally a stonemason, and being employed to build the
subscription rooms at Newmarket, he put in his “ smoke
oh,” and other spare time, layinsr the odds in “ half-
bulls ” with his fellow-workmen. Then the moment came
when fortune swung her golden ball towards Davis, and
he gripped and held it fast, coming out of his first big
betting venture £57 ahead. Later on he plunged heavily
on Sir Tatton Sykes for the Derby, won his wagers, and
threw down the chisel and took up the pencil for good.
When the Act for the Suppression of Lotteries was passed
Davis, knowing that many men must gamble or die, saw
another chance to score, so he published his first list in
1846, at the Durham Arms, Strand, while at his head-
quarters, “ Barr’s,” in Long Acre, Davis and a staff of
clerks stood at huge bankers’ ledgers and entered bets.
So great was the confidence reposed in Davis, that a
winning voucher of his was as negotiable as a Bank of
England note.
• • • •
The real cause of deferring the visit of an Eng-
lish cricket team until, perhaps, the end of next year, is
(according to Captain Archie Maclaren) the diffi-
culty in securing suitable bowlers. It is no longer con-
sidered a picnic when an English team visits Australia ;
instead there’s a great deal of hard graft to be got through
to win test matches against Australia’s “ best ” ; and,
with that end in view, the cream of ’Onie trundlers don’t
care to take it on. Also, the counties won’t risk a journey
to Australia of the best English professional trundlers,
and, therefore, it’s more than likely that we will have
to wait a while yet before anything like a represen-
tative team is brought out.
• • • •
A. C. M'Laren is stated to have been “elected
to a seat on the hoard ’’ of a London firm of wine-mer-
chants. He is to have as much free time as he may
require for cricket, county or otherwise ; and to play the
game regularly in England means giving one’s whole
time to it from the beginning of May till the middle of
September—to say nothing of visiting Australia, South
Africa, &c., in the off-season.
• • • *
The proposal to widen the wicket to 9in. will
probably meet with considerable opposition in Australia,
where it would certainly tend to shorten the long drawn-
out matches. Of course this would liven up the game,
but if it reduced the four days’ match to, say, two or
two and a-half, there would be a slump in the gate
receipts. And with the local cricketing bodies, the gate
talks every time.

• • • •
At Christchurch, the “most English” of all
M.L. cities, on the third day of Lord Hawke’s team v.
Canterbury, Sims and Pearce were batting, and Bosan-
quet had just sent one to Pearce. Although the wicket
was all right, one of the bails was off, and Whatman,
keeping wickets, cried, “ Well bowled, Bose.v,” but Sims
called to Pearce to appeal. Umpire Spencer could not
see the wickets, and it was found that a fog had got into
C. Bannerman’s eyes also. A wrangle resulted, and Eng-
lish captain Warner said with all the loudness of excessive
irritation that it was “ the most disgraceful decision he
had ever heard on the cricket field.” Then, after the
match, and the defeat of the Maorilanders, it was said that
during the rest of the game Bosanquet and Whatman
kept up a running fire of gibes, putting the local men off
their game. The fat was in the fire, and for days there
was no other subject worth mentioning in the city,
and more excitement than over 50 elections or wars.
Two days later Warner offered an alleged apology in the
press, but as he admitted offence, and sought to condone
it by saying that it was a remark not meant for the ears
of the world at large, his apology only made things
worse. Then Bosanquet apologised pretty frankly, and,
on behalf of Whatman, explained that Whatman would
apologise to Sims for a certain insult if it could be proved
that he was guilty of the said insult, and as it was
practically whispered into the ear of Sims, it is a case of
one man’s word against the other’s. Then the employer
of Sims, a very influential person, and a real Sportsman,
wrote Sims, stating that he would refuse to allow Sims
leave for the Maoriland match, unless Whatman per-
sonally and directly apologised to Sims, and this letter
was faithfully chronicled by the excited newspapers. The
whole affair is quite the most unpleasant in the history of
Maoriland cricket. As one of the two Maoriland
matches is to be played in Christchurch, the visitors will
have a lively time. Meanwhile cricket here has a dark-
brown taste. And it all arose from the one-in-a-million
chance of two umpires caught napping, coming to pass.
It was all fat for the papers, and they saw in it all sorts
of horrors, from the disintegration of the Empire to a
solemn warning against the degrading effects of English
public schools. The politeness at the remaining matches
will almost certainly be painful and protuberant.
There is a deal of trouble imminent between the
Earning bodies of cricket in Australia—the game o
msy which has characterised the *?£ionB of t
es for years. The real trouble 18 the other Sta
will not recognise the Melbourne Cricket Club aa t
“authority,” and the M.C.C. having been the mouthpiece,
so to speak, of cricket in bringing English teams to Aus-
tralia, considers it is entitled to the “ boss-ship. So when
the N.B.W. Cricket Association or the &A. body proposes
to introduce anv new idea, it is promptly sat upon by t
Melbourne authority—which procedure, having been
tolerated for a long time, must ultimately have a climax-
The position has been strained recently, k®* e T,£ r J, /
Frank Iredale, who gave notice, at the last N.5.v».1..a.
meeting, “ that, when the proper time arrives, the Asso-
ciation take steps to send the next Australian Ai. to
England.” It is but the thin end of the wedge
to a mighty upheaval. Then, again, the S.A. autbon-
ties proposed a visit of that State’s “ best’ to Westralia
at Easter-time ; and when the Melbourne body heard of
it, it announced its intention of sending a team on the
same errand. There are many leeks to be eaten—whicn
body shall do the “ feasting ” remains to be seen. What
is wanted is an Australian Federal Cricket Association,
composed of an equal number of delegates from each of
the States, which shall be expected to gi ve final decisions
in all matters connected with the game, as well as hiil the
position of representative head in dealings with, the
English, S’African, and other bodies. While there is no
“ boss ” authority the old jealousy will remain.

They had been playing poker all night in a club
in a Maoriland town. Of the party was an American
traveller. His luck was appalling, but he never lost his
calm. He had one unvarying formula : “ I’ll see you.
What have you got?" “Three Queens.” ’ Take it
away." “ I’ll see you. What have you got * F our
Jacks." “ Take it away.” As soon as the bank opened
in the morning the traveller went to draw £2O against his
letter of credit. He filled in the application and handed
it to the teller. The latter, asking the Yankee how he
would take the money, said : “Four fives ? ” The drum-
mer, with his wonted phlegm : “ Take it away ! ”
• • * •
Provided you can streak, it is better to be a pro-
fessional cyclist than a Crown Minister in Australia.
Yankee flier Major Taylor got £l5OO to come to Aus-
tralia and ride only 16 races. Also, he was to keep all he
could win, and now he is to receive an additional £5OO to
remain till April 14. It is understood that for some years
this dark young man has never made less than £9OOO per
annum for rushing round on a machine before a multi tudeof
people. Meanwhile George Turner, Australia’s careful
financier, who handles millions, and who has more to
do with the happiness and prosperity of every man,
woman and child on the continent than any other indi-
vidual man, will receive £l5OO for his year of crushing
responsibility and the work that kills. Still Thb Bulletin
would rather be George Turner.

Major Taylor shunted his influenza very speedily,
and there were none of the usual after-effects about it
either. Last week, with four men taking half a lap each,
the black bikist got over a mile on St. Kilda track in
1.50 2-s—the world’s fastest behind single pace ; he
“ jumped ” the last ISOyds. in 11 2-ssea, and finished
wonderfully strong. How Don Walker figured that he
had a hope with Taylor surpasses this writer’s under-
standing.
• • • •
Story published in a religious paper of Major
“ Holy ” Taylor, cyclist: “ At one of the recent contests,
a well-known cyclist was somewhat severely injured by a
collision on the track. As soon as possible, Taylor and a
number of members of the craft went to visit their fallen
comrade in the hospital. Standing by the bedside, the
world’s champion took the poor fellow by the hand, and
said in a voice as tender as that of a woman, ‘Well,
brother, I guess that you don’t want us to talk to you.
About the best thing we can do is to pray for you.’ And
in a moment the man whom everybody in Sydney has
recently been talking about was upon his knees, and
offered a prayer that will never be forgotten by the
astonished cyclists who were present. The way he pleaded
for the restoration of ‘ our dear brother’ was touching in
the extreme.” The “astonished cyclists"? Our oath,
yes 1

N.S.W. League of Wheelmen received but 110
entries for the “ Sydney Thousand ” —a handicap which
ought to have set the cycling world on fire. Apparently
the Australian plugger prefers smaller prizes, but plenty
of them. Seems a pity that the cyclists aren’t as enter-
prising as the League.
That Melbourne bicycle meeting in aid of an
Injured Cyclists’ Fund raked in £l5O for the good cause.
Now that a start has been made the idea should be pushed
along vigorously, and N.S.W. League should take the
matter up and give a day’s racing, on which occasion the
Sydney Cricket Ground Trustees might give the ground
free and insist upon all members paying a shilling
entrance fee. Still, there’s not much chance of the
S.C.G. doing either—the octopus cannot change its
nature.
• • • •
Motor-paced bike show at Sydney Cricket
Ground ended Wednesday and Robl, Dickentmann
and Co. left Europewards Saturday, not over pleased
with financial results, but foretelling a possible good
time for their department ot the wheel game in
the future when Australians are educated up to
it. Last display saw the Teutons allow McDonald and
Goodson 440j'ds. each in 3m. McDonald was never
caught and got home brilliantly—a quarter of his start to
the good. Dickentmann was second. A punctured tyre
put Goodson out of it but he came strong in the mile
which fell to him by 6yds. after a hot tussle with
McDonald right through the final lap. Neither Robl or
Dickentmann had a chance at any stage. Among best of
all during the three days was a five miles event. Dickent-
mann had half a lap and Goodson and McDonald 1J laps
each. Goodson’s tyre collapsed just inside 3m. from the
start. McDonald (overhauled a mile from home) made a
good fight of it but lost his pace and Dickentmann
qualified for the coin or whatever there was in it. Public
stayed away considerably—evidently full. Paced racing
doesn’t go down in these parts.
* • • •
N.S.W. crack swimmer, George Read—now a
full blown pro.—easily beat Wheat State champion Moon
in 500yds. S.A. supremacy, last week, after leading from
go. T me (6.25) is only 4-ssec. behind “Joey” Nuttall’s
world’s best, and considerably better than anything ever
previously done in Australia, yet Adelaide swimming
scribes wrote that their fresh water was slow compared
with that of N.S.W., because of lime being present in
large quantities. Rats ! If Read hadn’t shed his amateur
mantle through competing in S.A. his 6.25 would have
stood a world’s record —2-ssec. better than Billington’s
(England) established last year.
• • • •
Thousand yds. supremacy of S. A., Monday night,
went to Read, who won by 60yds. from local crack Moon,
in 14*31. Australian record is 13 51 1-5, and belongs to
Read, who put up that time at Farmer’s (Sydney) baths
December 7,1901.

* • • «
Past week saw speed-swimmers, island-boy
Wickham and Dick Cavill. streak over short spurts
phenomenally. One day Wickham traversed 50 yards in
27sec. ; couple of days later he improved on that 3-ssec
and further on same afternoon put up 26sec. First lap
(25yds.) of last-mentioned swim was thrown rearward in
11 3-ssec. ; but Cavill lopped 3-ssec. off in a trial and is
thus responsible for possibly the fastest dash over 26
yards ever known, while Wickham's fifty tops all records.
• • • •
Sydney racing yachts competed on Saturday
last for the Beauchamp Cup, presented by the ex-Gov. to
the Royal Bydney Yacht Squadron. Said “squadron"
is some half-dozen boats which career up and down
Port Jackson every Saturday. Sydney yachting is di-
vided between two bodies, who take themselves quite
seriously, named the R.S.Y.S above, and the Prince
Alfred Yacht Club. They share equally the year’s yachting
programme and the friendship of the Royal family. They
possess separate commodores, vice-commodores, club
houses, and yachting get-ups, but unfortunately
they possess, also, the very same racing yachts; so that
when the R.B. Y.S. announces a race for the King Edward
Cup, the Saturday tripper on the Manly boats knows that
the skipper on the bridge is trying to dodge the same
little fleet of boats that the week before were seen chasing
each other round the Sow and Pigs for the Queen
Alexandra Plate competed for by the P.A.Y.C. In con-
sequence there is none of that rivalry existing in Hobson's
Bay between the different yacht clubs.
* • • *
Heavyweight bruising supremacy of the conti-
nent for £2OO a-side and a purse of £325 will be fought at
Sydney National S.C. Tuesday night next week between
Dan Creedon and present holder, Billy McCall. Both
have been through a thorough preparation and are very
confident. It's an unusually good match physically for
they are strong, well-built men about the same height,
and the difference in weight won’t exceed 2lbs. McCall
may start the better favorite as Creedon is the visitor,
but anvhow there’ll be heavy wagering. Creedon has
been fighting perhaps 12 years in all parts of the world
and is still forceful and active ; he is cleverer, too, than
when he went to 'Murka as middleweight champion of Aus-
tralia. McCall is a comparative novice, lacking the skill
and wiles of Creedon, but he is a plucky, tough, hard-
hitting fighter who can stand thumbing and do some of it
himself to the other man’s detrim.jt when opportunity
offers. It’s a fight that probably won’t last six rounds
but there’ll be no let on once it starts.

Waterloo and Botany (to a man almost) watched
their champions, Booth and Porter, scrap at Sydney
Golden Gate A.C., Saturday night, and roared satisfac-
tion or otherwise continuously. Porter punched
hard and often; Booth came rarely, but when
he did ’twas a stiffer thump than any Porter sent along.
Not once, from start to stop, did the yelling cease, and
when Booth, waiting and watching, found an opening
and dropped his man cold and limp, the scene fairly
beat anything of the kind writer ever witnessed. It
was no push c ash ; it was an inter-district fight between
representatives of centres that boast more bruise lovers
than any other four suburbs. Botany is sure to find
another ‘man for Booth, and before long, too; then
there’ll be another big battle worth some trouble to
look at.
• • • •
Ex-silver-digger Jim Hiscutt and clever, but
ineffective, fighter Montv Andrews shaped at Sydney
National S.C. last week. 'Each punched the other every-
where, but neither fell as the result of a blow. Hiscutt,
strung up high in mind, arm and leg, quivered again
with the excitement of the thing, and Andrews looked
fiercer than a bull pup, showing his teeth and throwing
loads of intention into what he did ; but, tho*h much
more skilful, he was as harmless almost as his
opponent. Hiscutt charged and ran against Andrews’
extended left frequently, and this damaged his
lips and put an eye badly in mourning. Never did so
many hooks, swings, jabs, &c., land on and near the
chins of two men with such poor results. Neither could
hit a hurtful punch. The battle went the full trip—2o
rounds—with Hiscutt forcing always, though completely
pumped out towards the end. Andrews won, but hap-
penings at any stage never justified the odds of 5 to 2 and
3 to 1 laid on him.
* • • •
Novice pug competition at Sydney Gaiety A.C.
ended remarkably. Each of two semi-final bouts and the
final were over inside the first round and all through
knock-out punches. There are few r parallels to this in the
history of bruise.
• • * •
S. A. Lawn-tennis Association this year hardened
its heart to drop J. R. Baker from the inter-State six.
Baker (who is now an Adelaide city councillor) has been
one of the leading players for some 15 years, though only
actual champion twice, and is the recognised local
authority' on the game. He came back into this year’s
team on a chance vacancy, to the general satisfaction.
Australia lost an able and resourceful
journalist in Mr. Joseph Evison, who died last
week of internal cancer at Bondi. The son of a
military officer, Mr. Evison saw some service in
China and India. Then drifting to Maoriland, he
was first an agnostic lecturer and then a journalist
—one of the positions he held being, curiously
enough, that of editor of a very pious and vigorous
Roman Catholic paper. In Australia Mr. Evison
was engaged for many years in literary work, as
leader-writer on the Australian Stab and in
other positions. He also contributed to Thk
Bulletin. Owing to his long and painful illness,
which incapacitated him from work, he was in
poor circumstances at the close of his life and The
Bulletin had collected a little money for him
from sympathisers; future receipts to the fund
will be handed over to Mr. Evison’s widow.
Mr. M'Mahon, of M‘Mahon’sPoint, North
Sydney, has called at this office and given an
amused but emphatic denial to the yam that
appeared in The Bulletin of Feb. 14— that he
hung a picture of Pastor Chiniquy in his drawing-
room in the belief that it was that of Marshal
M'Mahon, sometime President of the French.
The M'Mahon who was taken in by the swindling
picture-vendor dwelt somewhere in the neighbor-
hood of Bungendore. He will probably come
along with his contradiction next week.

“ Kahore ” writes :—
The impression conveyed by a recent personal item re
M.L. Speaker Maurice O’Rorke’s defeat is quite incorrect.
During last Parliament, and more particularly last
session, there never was enough noise to wake a man
from his afternoon nap. Fact is, O’Rorke never was a
strong politician. Despite the name, he had no “Irish ”
in his veins—never could “ show willing.” His position
as Speaker prevented him from being a strong party-
man, also from introducing deputations to harass Minis-
ters for roads and bridges, and howl for the main trunk
line ; and ill-health gave him further obscurity. Then
the Farmers’ Union Wanted to try its-strength, and the
Prohibitionists owed him one for his defence of the
publicans. But the folly of the average elector was the
chief factor in a good man’s defeats — -
Mrs. W. A. GROVE, Sterling Centre, Maine,
writes :
“ I was taken with a HARSH, DRY COUGH, and grew
steadily worse. My neighbors thought I was going into
Consumption. JAYNE’S EXPECTORANT CURED ME
IN LESS THAN TEN DAYS.”*
In a letter to his sister dated “Sydney,
Sept. 15, 1841,” Henry Parkes wrote : —“1 saw a
case in tire newspapers last week of a young
woman who was turned out of one of those
emigrant ships when the 10 days were up, and
was found by a policeman sitting on the Queen’s
Wharf and taken to the watchhouse. The next
morning she was brought before the magistrate
charged with being drunk ; and though she stated
that it was faintness, and that she was meditating
suicide when the policeman came to her, yet she
was sentenced on the oath of the policeman to sit
one hour in the stocks.”

Highly treasonable statement from Sydney
Daily Telegraph
This is not the place—although this might be a good
occasion—-for discussing the unreasonableness of setting
these limitations upon Russia’s advancement. It is
difficult to understand why Russia should be regarded as
a caged wild animal whose escape from confinement
would imperil the world’s peace and safety. Russia has
often been held guilty of acts, possibly provocative of
war, but lias never, at all events in modern days, deliber-
ately provoked a war, which is more than can be said of
France, Germany, Austria, and even England. If Russia
is to become a progressive and highly civilised nation she
must expand. As she cannot do this commercially, she
has to resort to diplomatic methods which are generally
not more dishonest than those which, under cover of the
protection of commercial and financial interests, are
resorted to by other nations such as England, Germany
and the United States. As for the Dardanelles, it is
difficult to understand why they should not be as much
an international highway as the Suez Canal.

“X.” Viewing the stately mummery o.
King Edward’s coronation procession in the bio
graph pictures, one is struck by the idea that the
general gorgeous effect is discounted by the pow-
dered wigs and trappings of servility worn by
postillions and footmen who figure in the show.
Thackeray’s jibe is recalled to mind. In “The
Newcomes,” published 1853, the satirist wrote :
The carriage of Sir Brian Newcome now drove up, from
which the Baronet descended in state, leaning upon the
arm of the Apollo in plush and powder, who closed the
shutters of the great coach, and mounted bj' the side of
the coachman, laced and periwigged. The Bench of
Bishops has given up its wigs; cannot the box, too,
be made to resign that insane decoration ? Is it neces-
sary for our comfort that the men who do our work in
stable or household should be dressed like Merry
Andrews ?
The Paradise of flunkeydom (which its name is
England) has somewhat modified its garish old
snobberies since 1853. A baronet’s menials are not
ordinarily dolled up to the same extent as of yore
when they take the h’air on master’s kerridge.
But the Merry Andrew costume that Thackeray
ridiculed is none the less essential to the
“majesty” of a royal procession. In a comedy
where every mummer wore a livery more or less
absurd, and the king himself sported a travelling
crown on his bald head, it was very necessary that
flunkeys should be extravagantly disguised, so as
to keep the king in countenance. Monarch,
peers, court officials, generals, and all engaged
upon the .gaudy. jobjbar. the .aaihuu.
men) were Merry Andrews of various degrees.
In selecting a man to contest the Victorian Leg.
Council seat, rendered vacant by the retirement
of alleged Senator Robert Reid, the Reform
League has shown its claws badly. It has been
strenuously denied that this combination of self-
seekers is tinged with Toryism. Yet the League,
after careful consideration in private, chose fa*,-
man William Cain, as a landlord after its own
heart. In his manifesto, Cain says he will sup-
port the Government in promoting economy in all
public expenditure—in fact, it seems that he
believes in robbing anybody rather than in paying
taxes; he is in accord with the proposal to cut
down the numbers in the Assembly to 56, but
does not think the Council should be reduced in
proportion. Also, he supports sweating in the
public service and Railway Department, but
opposes adult suffrage. Then Cain winds up
with some flapdoodle about the Govt, having
‘‘ fa ?ed difficulties which threatened the future of
the State in a manner which no previous Govern-
f;ad done.” About the other democratic
ideas that were embodied in the Reform Bill
Cain says nothing, but, judging from his general
attitude, he proposes to bury them in the same
grave with his brother Abel.

“ Quilp N.” writes :—
Re the loss of the Elingamite and subsequent dis-
crepancies between the engineers’ and deck-officers’
evidence. The engine-room staff would feel a slight
bump which would not be felt on deck. On coastal
steamers trading across shallow bars the engineers fre-
quently hear and feel the vessel scraping over the sand,
when there is not a tremor perceptible up above. When
a propeller-blade strikes even so slight an object as a
S-v.” cm- ' ° X ll ? e t) * ow r ' n £ 8 > n the engine-room like a bell,
lhe Elingamite s engines went half a turn astern, which
proves that the reversing-gear was in order. The theory
that kelp clogged the propeller-blades will not stand
investigation. It would take more than kelp to hold
powerful machinery such as the El in samite’s —she had
been logging 12 knots previous to the fog coming down—
and unless some of the fires were drawn the steam would
not be likely to fall to a dangerously low pressure, as has
been suggested. Rather, using but a little, the pressure
would be inclined to rise. It is most probable that the
engineers evidence is correct—the propeller fouled some-
thing, and the shock was not felt on deck. I have known
of a steamer grinding across a bar, and an old chain, half
buried in the sand, rattled against her plates. The
engineer on watch heard it clinking as distinctly as if
the sound had been in the engine-room. He was the
only one who heard it.
Tasmania struggles along with four paid
Ministers at £750 a year each, and some of the
‘‘reformers” are demanding that one-third of
these (i.e. one Minister and 33 per cent of another
Minister) shall be abolished, and that the salaries
of the remaining two Ministers and two-thirds be
further reduced. Now, what salary do these
reformers think would buy the services of a
competent manager for a financial institution with
£800,000 a year income and nearly £9,000,000 of
liabilities; or for an assurance office with 172,000
clients; or for an estate of 12,000,000 acres? No
doubt a manager for the A.M.P. Society or
Broken Hill, Proprietary mine-, could be got
for a few pounds a week, but what kind of
A.M.P. Society and what kind of mine
would there probably be after the few pounds a
week man had been in charge for a few years ?
And think of the folly, from the Tasmanian
reform ” standpoint, of paying Wellington the
salary he received to command the Allies at
Waterloo, when there were plenty of people ready
to take the job for £2 a week and the cocked hat.

“ N.” : Concerning Gavan Duffy : —
Though Duffv left his own elaborately chiselled monu-
ment,'in the “ Autobiography,” he has not drawn his
portrait—that shuffling ge ltleman who used to be noted
in Melbourne streets, shaking his head, and talking to
himself, as he went with his eyes on the pavement. He
arrived in Vic. just at the right time. The old semi-
nominee Parliament was moribund, and the new Consti-
tutional one about to spring into being. The outcome of
his colleagueship with O’Shanassy in two Ministries was
that the big O’Shan, said, “ Duffy is the biggest scoundrel
unhung.” However, it must have been in somewhat of a
Pickwickian political sense, for Duffy records in his book
that, after their final political parting, O’Shanassy called
on him in London. He introduced O’Shan, to some of
his fine friends there, in return for O’Shan.’s putting him
on his legs in Melb. Over 40 years ago the news (false)
of Duffv’s death was passed round in the Victorian
Legislative Assembly, to the surprise of nobody.
He had notoriously the most rieketty constitution in the
whole House. Notwithstanding, he despised injunctions
to take care of his health. After returning home at mid-
night from an exhausting Parliamentary debate he would
read till about daylight. He insisted on a big and late
dinner, and said to Sam. Winter : “ Never attempt to
dine in the middle of the day.” Did that prop him up
till eighty-seven ? As a politician Duffy had not much
sincerity ; but it should be always remembered to his
credit that he originated the Parliamentary agitation for
Federation. In 1857 he moved for the appointment of a
select committee of the Victorian Assembly V to enquire
into and report upon the necessity of a Federal union of
the Australasian States for legislative purposes, and the
best means of accomplishing such a union if necessary.”
The committee was appointed, and though its delibera-
tions came to nothing, Duffy toiled at his Federal idea
all his political life. There was a suspicion that Duffy’s
real motive was separation from England ; and the time
was not ripe for that. For what political work he did
Mr. Duffy was, unlike most politicians, well paid. In his
long life as a pensioner he overtook any difference
between what he had deserved and what he had drawn
as salary.

’S EXPECT OR ANT
THE WORSTCOUGHS.
In Hospital
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I was in G Ward—-a temporary Wreck—watching
the bars swinging. Over every bed, suspended
by a chain, is a short bar, by whioh patients who
cannot move their legs may draw themselves up
into a sitting posture. The patients set the bars
swinging to break the monotony; I’ve seen
nearly all the bars going like pendulums at the
same time. The motion soothes many : it never
soothed me.
• • * *
The old night-nurse in spectacles is making
beds, and getting a few convalescents out on to the
verandah. There seems no change in the ward,
save that a screen is drawn round the bed oppo-
site me. It tells me that a quaint and stubborn
old spirit passed out during the night.
There are forty-nine wrecks beside myself—
forty-eight since last night; but there is a man
walking lustily to his work this morning (and
averaging £3 a week) who, this night, will be
carried in and laid on the dead man’s bed, smashed
by a tram,
A cheerful young Swede who has lost a toe is
scurrying round in a wheel chair. He is a sailor.
He reminds me that there are several Swedes in
the ward, all of whom have met with accidents on
ships or wharves in the harbour. “We Swedes
hold on as long as we can—the others get from
under quick,” one explained to me.
* • • •
I think the popular idea is that hurt men suffer
great pain all the time till they get well. As a
rule, they don’t. I had a broken ankle, and can
honestly say that I scarcely felt it at all after the
first night. Toothache is ten times worse than a
broken bone. But here men suffer mentally ; I
believe the mental, in most cases, is much
greater than the physical suffering. Honest,
sober workmen are tortured by the thought of lost
time and wages, and by the forced inactivity. And
what of the wrecks (the sinners) brought to this
by their sins or because of woman’s work? You
see the majority of the accidents come about
through drink, and
The hell the Christian fears to name
Is Heaven to his fierce remorse.
“ This experience makes a man callous,” I said
to Dr. A , one morning.
“We are not callous,” he said ; “I often see
things that I feel keenly ; but we must appear
cheerful whatever happens. . „ . The trouble is,”
he added, reflectively, “ that we never know what’s
behind. We never know what’s behind.”
* • ’» *
Afternoon . . . visitors. . . .
Bill’s wife (poor, worried, hard-worked woman)
con es in her Sunday best, with a friend maybe, if
it’s Sunday, also Bill’s mates. “’Ullo, Bill !
how are yer poppin’ up?” and they all grin.
Bill’s wife—poor worn working woman—is rather
proud of the situation, if the truth were known ;
as thougli Bill had done something meriting
special recognition, instead of getting drunk and
being knocked down by a Newtown ’bus on
Saturday night.
And it’s pretty and refreshing to see Jim’s
sweetheart come to see him. How many first
sweet kisses are given, and how many love secrets
surprised in the casualty ward ?
There’s the sailor or foreigner who never has a
visitor nor receives a letter, but doesn’t expect
one, and so is contented. Yet I suppose the
sailor frets a bit for his mates in the fo’c’sle of
the ship that has sailed without him.
And there is the wreck who waits and hopes
and watches ever for the visitor or the letter that
never comes. He may have callers, new friends
and the old, half-forgotten friends, and has now a
chance to judge between the false friends and the
true. But he is past caring for these trifles ; he
scarcely heeds a word they say ; his eyes wander
over to the ward-door, watching for the one who
never comes. He watches till the last moment;
he watches after visiting hours ; he watches all
through “off’’days; he watches through impos-
sible hours of the night; and, in spite of medical
science, nursing, care, attention, and sympathy,
he wears himself out watching the ward-door for
the one who’ll never come.
“ We never know what’s behind.”
*•- • »
I think I know what’s behind it in many cases :
Woman’s Work. The accident brought about
through drink, and the drink the result of
woman’s work. I wonder how much it costs the
country to fix up woman’s work ?
Take the old hard-case who was carried out
swiftly just now, hidden in the black trough-
shaped stretcher. His wife nagged him all his
married life. She only thought of him for his
wages. He had been knocked down by a vehicle,
and for the first three or four days she insisted
that he was well enough to leave the hospital and
go to work. On the third or fourth he dressed
and made an attempt to leave the ward with her,
but, half-way down, he collapsed into a chair, and
was hurriedly undressed and tumbled into the
nearest vacant bed. The doctors didn’t know he
was so bad. Then, when she thought the case
was hopeless, her visits ceased altogether.
They kept him up with hypodermic injections ;
ahd, oh ! but the old soul was tough ! Helpless,
propped up on pillows, kept alive with broth and
the hypodermic syringe, you heard his old tele-
phone going all the time. His last remark was
that all men are fools, and he was severely
admonished for the dash. He wanted to get up,
but they wouldn’t let him. Then he wanted to sit
up with his feet on the floor, and they wouldn’t let
him. He said he’d smother if he lay down. They
put the boards up to keep him in, and he got
excited. The last I saw of him was his feet stuck
up on the edge of the sideboard in a very
immodest and defiant manner, to show the other
wrecks and the navigators in this channel of pain,
trouble and death, that old Hard-Front hadn’t
struck his colors yet.
Later on tne (tear old night nurse soothed him,
maybe he told her what was behind ; and, about
daylight, moving silently, she drew the screen
round the bed.
“ All men are fools.”
» * * •
Nurses ...
There were only two nurses there that I was
afraid of ; one was the head, and the other a
probationer, the most practical girl and the
greatest little bully that I ever came across —she
always gave me the impression that she’d spank
me, and spank me hard, if I her a back
word.
“ For (xod’s sake, girls,” I’d say to the nurses,
“go and have a rest, and let us have one 1 ” but
they took no notice. .
The little bully sticks a clinical thermometer
under my arm and claps the arm-down over it.
“Why don’t you fold your towel? she asks
sharply. „
“I—l beg your pardon, nurse, I forgot.
M Always fold your towel! ” she says, with a
snap.
I tty to say something funny as she takes out
the tnermometer.
“The under-head nurse says I’ve got no tem-
perature.”
“ Well, what then ? ”
“ Well, what’s the use of you trying to take it,
then ?
Most of the nurses are good-looking or pretty,
and some of them are so beautiful, with a strange
sort of spiritual beauty, that you never get tired
of watching them.
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.
When the Brisbane train had passed
Gatton on a recent trip a first-class passenger
suddenly broke his journey by stepping out into
space. Some allege thathehad just wakened up and
“ didn’t know where he are,” while others assert
that he jumped out. Anyhow, he hasn’t been
seen since, though the way of his going led to the
death of another man. Station-master Woodgate
of Gatton, on being wired, started along the line
to search for him, and while crossing a railway
bridge fell a distance of 60ft, and died shortly
after being discovered.


. A Great Sale.— Bacon and Co., Gunnedah,
report having sold for H. C. White, Esq., from his well-
known “Havilah” Estate, near Mudgee, 1165 cast stud
ewes, in lamb to Havilah stud rams, at 355. per head
cash ; purchaser, Thomas H. H. Goodwin, Esq., of Booloo-
cooroo, near Gunnedah. This is the first draft of cast
ewes sold from the famous Havilah stud for many years,
and Mr. Goodwin has secured the nucleus of what should
eventually prove to be one of the best stud flocks in the
Commonwealth, and is to be congratulated upon hi«
enterprise. The introduction of pure Havilah blood
sheep to our district will give small holders an oppor-
tunity of securing some of the progeny of this valuable
strain, which throughout Australia stands second to
none. In view of the drought so long prevailing, and un-
favorable prospects generally, this sale may be regarded
6- a reborn one.*
A cheerful critic protests against “ the pessimistical
moanings and groanings to which modern music is
inclined." 7
Melb. Just now Is suffering from the outbreak of bogus
pressmen who, more persistent than most of their class,
'ontrive to obtain tickets of admission to the most select
3 pots of public entertainment. Not long ago one push-
r'ul individual secured his name “on the doors’’ of one
ir two theatres on the shadowy ground that he was con-
nected with a journal that was going to be published. It
has not yet materialised. It would appear as if there
would shortly be more press than public at some of the
open-air shows
Mr. Oscar G. Roberts, of the well-known firm
of Fairfax and Roberts, who is lecturing around
Sydney’s environs on “Sight Failure: Its Cause and
Jure,” tells some remarkable yams about astigmatism.
Sir Edward Landseer, according to Mr. Roberts, used to
paint an animal with his left eye and hand, while with
his remaining optic and the other hand he was hard at
work on another portion of the picture. Gladstone had,
according to Mr. Roberts, the same extraordinary gift,
and use to write all his letters in duplicate, one with each
eye and hand, and in the course of his long life saved the
cost of several copying presses and alsp the leakage that
comes of trusting documents to be copied by a confi-
dential clerk.*

Menzies Alpha (W.A.) is a gradually improving
proposition. The four rich chutes of the Lady Shenton
are gradually dipping south into the Alpha property, and
will eventually all get there at depth. The Shenton,
under great disadvantages as to transport, firewood, &c.,
has distributed £120,000 in divs., so that the outlook for
the Alpha looks highly promising. In January mine pro-
duced 805 oz. from 421 tons treated. Co. is, however,
financially weak and badly equipped, and would have to
be reconstructed to be put on a satisfactory basis. The
two mines are under one manager, and the underground
workings are connected, so that possibly the best policy
would be for the two co.’s to amalgamate; such a step
would materially improve the position of both concerns.
• • * •
Queensland Menzies (W.A.) has just had its
best half-year since 1898, the last year in which it dis-
tributed 28. per share. As the co. also added a 6-head
battery, self-feeders, rock-breaker and rock-drills out of
profits, it is now better equipped than ever, for clean
working. The developments both above and below the
446 ft. level are highly satisfactory, and good ore is being
broken from both the hanging and footwall lodes. The
ore reserves are bigger than ever, so that regular divs.
seem to be assured for some time to come. As co. isnow
treating about 750 tons monthly for a yield of fully
1200ozs. it looks as if current year’s divs. would tot up to
3s. a share.
s s » 1
The Golden Pole, Davyhurst (W.A.) has struck
a supply of water estimated at 5000 gallons daily in its
main shaft at 300 ft. This mine is regarded as one of the
best outside of Kalgoorlie, all the stone taken out in
development having averaged 3oz. over the plates. The
Kalgoorlie syndicate, which owns the show, appears to
have been born tired, as it has taken three years to Sink
the main shaft 300 ft. A large quantity of similar stone
to that treated is developed, and it is now proposed to
erect a m il, and treat the stone on the spot instead of
paying heavy charges for carting. Up to now the Davy-
hurst district has been regarded as barren of water, and
this find may put new life into prospectors. If this mine
had been held by an energetic co. it would long ere this
have been a large and regular producer.
• • • *
The little Victor mine, Mt. Usher, on the old
Crocodile field, Rockhampton (Q.), seems a property better
than many in that district. In the hands of a syndicate
which divides up every available cent after each crush-
ing, vyith development work consistently left till rooting
out stone makes a little more absolutely necessary, and
with cost of crushing at a public mill tacked on to cartage
and unduly heavy working expenses accruing from abso-
lute lack of any machinery whatsoever, the Victor has,
for the last couple of years, paid its way, besides yielding
steady dividends of a very gratifying character to the
syndicate. Crushings run consistently over the ounce
from battery, with about 10 per cent, of concentrates
ranging between 3 and soz. The expensively equipped
Mt. Usher shows up very badly in comparison.
* • » •
London mining papers of 16th January contain
the following cable from Langford, chairman of Briseis :
“ After making a most thorough examination my opinion
is Fraser's policy excellent. Doing splendid work. The
present output is easily obtained. There is no doubt as
to wisdom of change of management. Am writing fully."
Which seems rough on Hawley, the late manager.
Langford’s approval of change of management had the
immediate effect of knocking down the shares, and they
have been on the down-grade ever since. They are being
panned out wholesale in Melbourne. The “ wisdom ” of
the change of management is not obvious to the dis-
interested outsider, and, so far, would-be boomsters have
not profited by it. Hawley was told that he must
increase the output of tin, which he declined to do, as
any effort in that direction would have materially inter-
fered with preparations for systematic operations. Im-
portant negotiations are said to be proceeding between
Briseis and Brothers Home co’s for joint working, which
ought to be mutually advantageous. Without some such
arrangement Briseis will soon be in trouble as to the dis-
posal- of debris from a large section of its property.
Briseis is unquestionably a big proposition, if rightly
handled, but looks in peril of being wrecked on Boom
Bluff. ' '

Castlemaniacs have had their nerves badly
shaken by Cumberland and Phnenix. Battery started a
few days ago. Champagne flowed freely enough to run a
sluicing claim. Share values corresponded with the
ebullitions of carbonic gas, but what a difference there
was in the morning, when the first clean-up took place.
Writer worked in this ground 25 years ago. It is as
patchy as Joseph’s coat! For that reason, stock may
become a good gambling one

“Merudic” writesln “B. 7/2/03,” referring
tq the |ease title question, you say no one has yet cited-
afi instance where a lease was unjustly forfeited. Some
years back the machinery, &c., of the Clunes Co.,
which had been shut down, were sold by auction, and
bought by Messrs. Mi.ler and Co., machinery merchants*
Ballarat, for purpose of removing and re-selling 'the
machinery, and with no intention of working the mines.
Thinking, however, there might yet be something in
them, it was decided to try the South Clunes property
before removing the plants, and a considerable amount of
monej' was spent, which proved that there was yet plenty
of payable stone. But the mines had been so worked in
the past, that heavy expenditure On pumping machinery,
and a long period of unproductive labor were necessary
before-payable results could-be hoped for. Efforts had
been made to obtain capital in Melbourne, before
selling, in ord r to carry on, but without avail, and
for some years Mr. T. Cornish had tried in London with-
out success. Eventually Mr. John Lowles was i duced to
take the matter up, and a sum of £2500 was remitted to
his son here to commence work with. While these
negotiations were going on, application for forfeiture had
been made, and deputations from Clunes had waited
on the Minister urging it. The leases were forfeited,
although the Minister was aware the money was here and
the applicant for forfeiture could show no bona fides and
only promised to obtain capital within a certa n time. The
result was that all machinery was removed, and the mines
have bean idle ever since ; the £15,000 subscribed in Lon-
don were put into a Maldon mine, which has been a failure.
Had this money been spent on the South Clunes there is
little doubt it would have been working to-day and
returning profits. Messrs. Miller and co. crushed a large
quantity of stone, and proved that there were large quan-
tities left above the old workings, if the water could be
coped with (this, of course, was only a question of funds).
I think this is one instance, and people can draw their own
inferences. What is complained of is the absolute power
left in the Minister’s hands. He may be strictly
honorable, but, on the other hand, he may not;
and in case he should not be (recent events show
Ministers are not always all that could be desired), it is
as well that his powers should be limited. I can give you
one or two other instances of injustice with regard to
lease matters that have occurred during seven years resi-
dence here, and am of opinion that an inquiry into the
past working of the Dept, would, on the evidence that
could be adduced, lead to some extremely interesting
revelations.- [“ Mundic” is evidently not aware that the
English-owned Port Philip Co., after making enormous
profits out of thq property, refused to use funds in hand
to develop the m»f They thought so little of it that
they used reserve funds to buy a mine in another part of
the world. By resorting to all manner of devices, they
retained possession of the property for many years, trust-
ing to the pluck and enterprise of local co’g to find some-
thing new. “ Mundic " disproves his own case in his
admission that the local man who purchased the leases
made an effort to work them. Writer agrees with “ Mun-
dic ” that an inquiry as to why the Clunes leases were
allowed to be shepherded so long would remit in some
“interesting revelations.” Next case of injustice,
please 1]

Recent Trisco Chronicle, commenting: on the present?
of Royalty to artists, relates that Melba, when in Yankee
land, used to exhibit a brooch given her by the late
Queen Victoria on the occasion of her visit to Windsoi
Castle. The brooch is said to be of the description that
Billy, the milk-youth, givesi his donah, and theCiißONicu-
states that it used to be a standing joke with Melba tt
ask people to guess its value.
Deceased pantomimist Charles Lauri’s first engage
ment was with Hollingshed at the old Westministei
Theatre. On applying, he m(et at first with a refusal
but, while talking to Hollingshed, he lifted his fooi
and scratched his ear therewfch in such an off-hand
everyday fashion, that he was engaged on the spot to
play the “Cat ” in “Dick Whittington.” He continued
to play animals till his death. Panto runs in the Lauri
family; the great grand-father and great grand-mothei
were pantomimists, and famous in their day. The story
of Charles Lauri shows that the mummer has at least some
advantage over, say, the clerk. The unemployed book-
keeper who asked the A. M.P. Society for a billet and was
refused, might scratch his ear with both feet sim-
ultaneously on the steps of the big office in Pitt-street for
a whole day without improving his prospects in the
least.
Senator Higgs in Melbourne Argus :— * One commences
to understand the reason of Mr. George Reid’s popularity.
Music, sir, music. Mr. Reid minus the music would not
attract so many, but Mr. Reid plus a free organ recital
for those who do not want a reserved seat will fill the
Melbourne Town Hall to its utmost seating capacity. Is
it not too bad on the part of a politician of Mr. Reid’s
standing to compete against the theatrical and kindred
professions? Instead of his advertisement appearing in
the Argus under the heading of ‘ Meetings, &c,’ It is
placed in the category of Amusements,’ in the same
columns as Mr. Harry Rickards’ screaming farcical
comedy • Mrs. Murphy’s Second Husband,’ and other ad-
vertisements concerning dancing, gipsy fortune-telling,
and face massage, showing clearly that Mr. Reid hopes to
get the amusement-loving public to give him a hand in
his forlorn hope of trying to down the Barton Admin-
istration."
What strikes one as being most human in the farcical
character of Sites in the “Wrokig Mr. Wright" is the
aggressive robustness of that 45-year-old. How he flares
up at the suggestion of his having left town in search of
health 1 Slaps his chest and asks whether he looks like a
sickly man. Health ! —he’s got “ health for sale.” At
the age of 45 (the writer speaks feelingly) a full-blooded
Sites invariably slaps his shirt front if anyone ventures an
aspersion on his manly vigor. He wants it distinctly
understood that he never felt so strong in his life before,
and never intends to weaken. He has arrived at the
time for emphasising his immortality. And his young
friends, who will be 45 themselves some day, are expected
to take the matured rooster seriously, i
A beer-drinking contest was advertised to take place
in Adelaide on Saturday, Feb. 14, but the weather was
so rainy and cold that the momentous event was post-
poned. The publican stated that he had a man who,
under favorable conditions, could drink 19 pints of ale in
three hours. Nothing very wonderful. One of the old-
time comps, in the Advertiser office is said to have drunk
18 pines in one hour.
Albert Marsh—presumably the same who was the
original blood-curdling “ King of Crime " in Sydney—
lately produced a new three-act farce at one of the
English sea-side theatres. The easte included Charles
Glenney, G. W. Anson, Miss Itatfia Conti, and Pollie
Emery, all of whom are well-remembered in Australia.
Miss Conti is least remembered. She came with the
Broughs some years ago. Played the heroine in “ The
Happy Life,” and was one of the wrangling sisters of
Brough’s serio-comic Napoleon.
Maggie Stirling’s fine voice, considerably polished by
Marchesi, appears to be making its way in England. She
got glowing notices for her singing at a Richter and Hallfe
Concert at Manchester, with the result that a select
musical cult in London has begun to bring her along.
During the years since she left this country, Maggie
Stirling has had to battle hard for a chance to get herself
really known. Her occasional “ successess ” —reported
by cable—were worth very little to the struggling mezzo-
soprano. Now she has at least made a favorable im-
pression.ln quarters where influential people pass judg-
ment.
“ Cascart ”: I saw “ Zaza" a second time last night.
Went to the amp hitheatre, which was packed to the
ceiling, and mostly with women. Women, women every-
where. I counted 21 in one row, with but one man
amongst them ; another row contained 30 women with
but two men ; and in another row, from end to end,
there were 45 women and nine men. What is there in
this play that fasesnates women so much ? Is it the un-
veiled naughtiness of the play, or the presentment of a
heroine who emerges Purified from a burning fiery fur-
nace of improper Love. Every girl has a purely theoretical
admiration for the heroine who is Purified.
“Melb.”: Opinion is pretty general that there is no
warrant for sending Miss Dolly Castles to Europe. There
are 40 voices in this city and suburbs as good. The fierce
light pf Melba, and the lambent flame of Dolores, have
tried Amy Castles. She came out worthily, but vet is not
a “cert” for the very front, although I will say she is
intrinsically better than Lalla Miranda or Frances
Saville. Dolly just has obstinate pluck. <
Dolores, following Melba at a distance of three weeks or
so, scored a tremendous success in the West on her
return visit. Crowds were turned away from her first
concert at* Perth after £250 had been wedged into the
hall. The Melba boom, with its resultant booming of
Dolores, considerably impoverished the market for ordi-
nary shows.

Re Elton and Lauri (“ Haresfoot,” notwithstanding).
Lauri at his best has never approached Elton at his best
in the creation of parts in comic opera. For example,
compare Elton as Lurcher, Polydore Poupart, the thief
in “Ermine,” in “Marjorie,” and,as Jack Point, with
Lauri in “La Poupefc, “ Geisha," “Ma Mie Rosette,”
“ Rose of Persia.” Lauri at his worst is an artificial
boisterous humorist, with a good singing voice and clever
feet, and an irritating habit of not allowing his audience
to see the joke for themselves—in fact, the apotheosis of a
clown. Elton never clowned even when under great
temptation, as in “ Morocco Bound.” Finally, as to
artistic restraint. Compare Elton’s Duke of Plaza-Toro,
in which the actor allowed Gilbert’s humor to perco ate
through him and to emerge unadulterated, with the
lamentable blasphemy of Lauri’s boisterous Ko-Ko—as
played in Melbourne, anyhow. —Esmi.
The sad little Victorian town of Ararat seems to be
rather an unfortunate place for “poverty-pointers” to
wander into. Recently a “ Royal Dramatic Company ”
that had been playing to withered wheatfields, sun-dried
sheepskins, and sand-filled tanks up in the Mallee coun-
try drifted Ararat-wards to make a rise. It had just left
without the rise when the Woods-Williamson co. arrived
to give “ The Christian ” and “ A Woman’s Sin,” but on
the second.night the town-clerk and the management
disagreed badly about something, and there was trouble.
When the play-going crowd arrived it found the hall in
darkness and Alf ”on the steps explaining what he
thought, of Ararat officials. Mayor Wild came along and
tried to square matters, but A.'W. only tossed his long
hair a little farther back, and refused to be comforted.
However, after a long and fierce interview out in the
dimly-lighted streets, Hall Caine’s powerful work started
about 9 p.m. But Ararat didn’t care much about Hall
Caine’s powerful work by this time. What it had
expected to see was Alf. Woods pounding the Mayor and
town-clerk simultaneously in the street ; and the loss of
that free show grieved it badly.

In “All About Burns, Philp and Co.
Limited,” the enterprising firm in question has
turned out one of the handsomest and most useful
booklets of its kind that The Bulletin has seen.
As indicated by the title, the origin, progress,
and present development of the firm’s business
are primarily dealt with, but beyond this there is
much to interest and instruct. First, is pre-
sented a synopsis of the leading agencies held by
B. P. & Co., including—besides the Australian
Inter-State Coastal, and Pacific Island Lines—the
Oceanic Line (to U.S. via Maoriland), the Occi-
dental and Oriental, the Pacific Mail, the Queens-
land (London via Torres Straits), the Nippon
Yusen Kaisha, the Eastern and Australian (to
Japan), the British India, and the Netherlands
B'hursday Island to Singapore, via Timor and the
utch Indies). Full information is supplied, as
regards these and other lines, concerning vessels,
passages, ports of csll, and all particulars needful
for intendmgtravellers. Interesting descriptions—
freely illustrated—are given of the numerous
places where the firm’s branches are located, and
British New Guinea is dealt with at considerable
length. There are also maps of the Pacific
Islands, Australia (showing steam-routes), Torres
Straits, the China Straits, New Guinea, the Malay
Archipelago, and a couple of comprehensive route
charts covering the greater portion of the globe.

Somebody named M. M. Phillips, “ Vice-
President of the Metropolitan Committee of the
Australian Natives’ Association,” is of opinion
that “in the present financial condition of Aus-
tralia it is inadvisable to incur the cost of creating
a new Federal capital,” and proposes to raise a
discussion as to the advisability of altering the
Constitution to allow of Sydney being the capital
for the next 50 years. Well, that sounds all right
as far as it goes, but it sets one wondering whether
Mr. Phillips’ tender consideration for “our pre-
sent financial condition ” may not have something
to do with a fear that —borrowing having been
sternly discountenanced by the Federal Labor
party —the wherewith to erect the new capital
may not be looked for from a Federal graduated
land and income tax. We have heard and seen so
much of the man who had a tender consideration
for his country when such consideration meant
extra tenderness for himself, that we look with
suspicion on the man who would be extra tender
in a tearful voice. Also, why leave the expense of
erecting the capital to the next generation in such
a mean fashion —it will have to bear enough of
the cost, anyway, before all is paid up to pawn-
broker Bull in the next couple of hundred years.

APPENDICITIS PREVENTED.
Liver, Kidneys and Bowels Regulated.
IT is now common knowledge that the
term Appendicitis is applied to in-
flammation of that portion of the
bowel known as the appendix, and is
generally caused by the accumulation of
faecal matter, which in course of time
becomes hardened into a concrete mass.
Every stage of life, every condition of
existence, is susceptible to this disorder.
The infant, the adult and the senile,
the rich, the middle class and the poor,
the King and the peasant, are all subject
to imperfect nutrition, faulty assimila-
tion, and the ailments arising therefrom.
The Court Chemist to
His Majesty King Edward VIL,
And to the late German Emperor Frederick, King' of Prussia, and to
other Foreign Courts, Mr. Frank R. Squire, of San Remo, while
on his holiday in England, writes;
“ London, July 9th, 1902.
“Messrs. S. Kutnow & Co., Ltd.
“Gentlemen, —I thank you for your promptitude in sending your Kutnow’s
Powder asked for; it is one of the most useful remedies I know, and I never like to be
without it. “ I am, Gentlemen, yours faithfully,
“ FRANK R. SQUIRE.”
Success in business or the profession,
or in any walk in life, depends very
largely upon the state of health.
All who work with the brain value
health, and all who value health take
Kutnow’s Powder. , *
To be brief, the chief merit of Kut-
now’s Powder is the remarkable power it
possesses of effectively cleansing the
bowels, stimulating the liver, clearing the
kidneys, irrigating the organs, and ex-
pelling all poisonous and putrefying
deposits which may have accumulated in
the system.

A Tea-Time Trifle.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
"Were you never in love with Mrs. Pagette?”
said Mollie over her clasped hands to me. “It
would be a sin whose iniquity would plead for
itself.”
“ Once,” I said sentimentally, “ I was.”
“ I thought so,” said Mollie, delightedly, help-
ing herself to sugar. “And now I want to hear
all about it.”
“Oh,” said I, a trifle blankly, “ do you?”
*' Yes,” said Mollie determinedly.
“There’s really nothing much to tell,” I said
rather foolishly.
“ Well ? ” from Mollie firmly.
“Eh?” I said.
“ I’m waiting,” she said reproachfully.
“Oh, well,” I began weakly.
“When did it start?” Mollie interrupted,
crumbling a biscuit.
“ Oh, at a theatre-party,” I began vaguely,
“and I think she asked me to fasten a bracelet.
She had the dearest, dimpled Wrists ” —I noticed
Mollie glance surreptitiously at her own. “Just
here,” I added, laying a hand on hers.
“ Don’t be absurd,” she answered with dignity,
drawing away. But she didn’t seem displeased.
“ Go on,” she said encouragingly.
“ Certainly,” I replied, edging my chair nearer
and leaning over for her hand.
“No, not that way,” she laughed, flushing.
“Besides, those palms are not a screen. About
Mrs. Pagette—after the theatre ? ”
“ I saw her home,” I replied, eyeing the palms
with disgust. “She said pathetically that she
was lonely.” (Here I may be mistaken in think-
ing Mollie sniffed.) “They had only been mar-
ried a year,” I put in hastily, “and her husband
was paying a flying visit to Maoriland.”
“Urn,” said Mollie, taking another lump of
sugar. (She hates her tea sweet).
“ Next day I called,” I said tentatively.
“Um !”
“ And the next.”
A pause. “ And—the next ? ” drily.
“ I suppose so,” desperately.
“Yes?”
“Well, I went about with her. You know—
the usual thing.”
A negative raising of eyebrows.
“ Oh, there’s nothing to tell,” I said irritably.
“ By Jove, there’s not.”
“Well?” rather icily.
“ There’s nothing,” I returned injuredly, tilting
my chair back and drumming on the table. “ I
suppose I thought she meant to amuse herself.
But all the time I could never understand her in-
nocence quite. So we went steady and we trotted
round together like —like brother and sister.” A
sarcastic smile from Mollie opposite (it brought
out no dimples) was irritating.
“It was bewildering,” I went on, tilting
further, “ running round in a circle and always
arriving at the place you started from.”
“I know that feeling,” said Mollie, sweetly.
“George Danvers taught me that.”
“ The dickens he did,” I said, coming down
with a bang.”
“ Go on,” said Mollie, beaming.
“ Well, I did,” I said viciously, “and it didn’t
come to much. Before the beginning came the
end. One day I went, and I found her crying in
a ohair, and (reflectively)she was awfully pretty! ”
(No help from Mollie). “And (desperately) if a
fellow finds a pretty woman crying, it knocks
him off his perch and he’s got to comfort or kiss
her, or something.”
“ Indeed ! ” in a frozen voice.
“ Well, I tried it on anyway,” I said swiftly,
“ and ”
“Succeeded?” from Mollie, with the tiniest
yawn.
“No, by Jove, I didn’t,” I said, ruefully, one
hand furtively at my ear. “She blew my world
to pieces. How dared I? How wasa fellow to know!
And she thought I was a friend ? I thought so,
too, but didn’t venture my opinion. And she was
only crying because Jack was coming home, and
she was so glad. That knocked me over. Not
that I was ever hard hit (stealing a glance oppo-
site) but my vanity had miscalculated. I had the
sense not to apologise and—came away. She has
forgiven me now, to the extent of a bow.” (A
?ause.) “ But I never really admired fair hair,”
added, tentatively, “or the ingenue type.
And (an afterthought) her hands are not very
beautiful, and (confidentially) she often had a
cold.”
A smile crept suddenly round Mollie’s mouth.
It’s her best point, and she knows it.
“ And you know,” I said, lowering my voice
and edging nearer, “I never liked her China-blue
eyes.”
“ Why, they’re brown,” said Mollie, lifting
eyes into which the smile had danced to mine.
“Are they? I forgot,” I said, hypocritically,
bringing my chair quite round.
I have often wondered since if those palms were
a complete screen.
