The M.L.A.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There was a magnetism in the land that drew
him from the town, a desire for ownership, for the
rhythm of regular work—an instinct, perhaps
hereditary, that a wrestle with Nature at first
hand, clearing, delving, ploughing, was what his
best self craved for. And together with this
longing had grown up a hatred of the city, its
chaffering, bargaining, buying and selling. He
was not dismayed at the giant timber on his sixty
acres, for his imagination saw it cleared, fenced,
a house and barn erected, and the live stock in
the yard. Had he been mathematical he might
have counted the labor of it all, the months,
years, lifetime of toil before him ; but his measure
was a rubber rule which made time elastic.
In the first struggle of work there was a fierce
joy as the pent-up energy found outlet. Each
thundering crash of a grubbed tree seemed the
surrendering cry of a conquered enemy. The work
quickened the circulation of his sluggish city
blood ; his chest expanded with the deep inspira-
tions of physical effort; a thrill of content
possessed him, a love of toil for its own sake.
Yet, busy as his hands might be from jackass-cry
to curlew’s wail, years passed and the labor was
not done. And with the years his youth had
gone; the beauty of his wife had faded, and his
interest in the outside world had shrunk. He had
no time to read, to visit, to think. His mind
stopped working, like a clock run down, and dealt
only in reminiscences. A chance stranger with
the ideas of the day found him impatient and
dogmatic. Year by year the horizon had drawn
nearer and nearer, till his whole world was
bounded by the fence of his sixty acres.
In a different way the Bush put its brand on
the wife. Naturally coquettish, with a love of
company and dainty things, she had at first re-
garded the country life as a kind of picnic, and an
.esthetic haze softened the rude outline of slab-hut
and stump-sprinkled paddock. For long she
strove to keep up a neat and even coquettish
appearance, to gain a word, even a look, of ad-
miration from her husband ; but her reward was
meagre. His work tired him to utter weariness ;
he devoured his food ravenously and in silence;
and in the evening when she would have gossiped,
retailed the little incidents of the day as she did
her sewing, her husband dozed torpidly in his
chair, his remaining energy busy with digestion.
So the time came when? with the work of three
women on her hands, she cared less and less how
she looked. It was so easy to put off the mending
and making of her clothes ; easier to go barefoot
than to darn stockings ; easier to bind her hair
in a rough knob than to dress it properly. No
one noticed.
There were two boys, who, from the time they
could run, had no playtime but such as they could
steal from chipping, chopping, or hunting cows.
Their little lives were one long task never done,
a task to be dodged, if possible, a task hated be-
cause of its monotony and everlastingness. On a
day the fattier spoke: “Do you think we can
manage it, Ann ? ”
“ We must manage it, Dad. The boy wants
more schooling ; that’s certain. And, there’s my
•bjitter-money I have been saving for the buggy ;
he might have that.”
“ I thought you were so set on the buggy, Ann ;
you have talked of it for years.”
“So I am, God knows! We have walked all
our lives, or jolted in the cart; and it’s many a
time I’ve thought, as folks dashed, by in their car-
riage, ‘ I’ll ride like that when I’m old, and spread
dust on other people.’ But, never mind ! The
boy must have his chance. Let it go. I’m a
silly old woman. The cart has been good enough,
so far ; let it do to the end.” There was a tremor
in the wife’s voice. “ And there’s Tom’s money
for the apprenticeship. We might borrow that
for a time.”
“Oh, yes! Tom can wait. He will do well
enough at home. What does he want with engi-
neering ? Isn’t the farm good enough ? ”
But Tom would not wait. He was indignant;
his usual stolid acceptance of parental authority
ceased abruptly. “It’s not fair, Dad ; here I’ve
been waiting for three years, and you know I
shall soon be too old. I don’t growl at Fred being
the favorite—l suppose he is clever; but if you
gave him the last sixpence in the world he’d not
as much as sav ‘Thank you.’ And I'm not going
to be a bushie all my life. I’d rather carry a
swag.”
“Then carry a swag, you young good-for-
nothing ! Won’t be a bushie, eh ? Then what am
I and your mother, Mr. Toff ? ”
The father spoke with a sharpness not tempered
by the thought that much of his son’s grievance
was just. His slowly-developed plans had not
included Tom, who was not brilliant, and had
given none of those signs that in the younger
brother, Fred, had aroused in the parents’minds a
long-dormant ambition. They felt intuitively
that for them life had been a mistake for which
there was no remedy ; they had given their all for
sb little. Fred, at least, should make a better
bargain, and his success should be a consolation to
their old age. Unfortunately, by this plan Tom
suffered, and the father’s anger with himself made
him doubly angry with the lad.
“ I didn’t say nothing about you and mother,
cried Tom passionately ; but the old man turned
away impatiently and would hear no more. And
at tea-time, even his mother looked sourly at
Tom, saying not a word Prepared to sacrifice
herself, she could not understand the slackness of
others. Going supperless to his room, the boys
Resolution took shape during his sleepless night,
and before sunrise he was trudging down the track
with a swag on his back, and only the mocking
wy cf the goburra to wish him God speed.
• • • *
Years passed since Tom had shouldered bluey
/nd disappeared in the mighty West. I rom
time to time they knew at the homestead that he
must have earned a cheque, for small remittances
came by post- from places bearing strange and
outlandish names, with a- few weirds added, and
they were always the same. “ Ihe enclosed is to
help mother to buy her buggy.’'
Fred saw some of these letters, and laughed at
the writing or spelling, while he found a plausible
excuse for taking the contents ; but gradually the
letters were less frequent. Then they altogether
ceased, and Tom was heard of no more.
It seemed for a long time that the money spent
on Fred’s education was to yield small tangible
result. He found no steady employment m the
city, bei g full of schemes that came to naught,
and big words which always resulted m extracting
the last shilling from the old people. Still, with
the hope of devotees, they never lost faith, t red
was to be a great man, the man his father might
have been had he had the chance ! They knew of
his doings vaguely, and read with pride the news-
papers he sent them in which his name appeared
as a speaker at public meetings. “ He is getting
on, Ann,” the father would say ; and the day
would be brighter to them as they went about
their work. Only when his wants were urgent
did bred come to see them, and it seemed as
natural for them to give as for him to take. It
was after one of these journeys that Fred met his
father in the garden.
I tell you what it is, Dad ; I must go into
I arliament. An election is coming on, and I’ve
a splendid chance if I had a couple of hundred for
expenses.”
A couple of hundred, Fred ! You know your
mother and I have not ten notes in the world.
Your education, and all the rest of it since, has
been very expensive.”
“ But you might raise a bit on the farm—only a
matter of form, you know, I’m certain to win,
and <£3oo a year will soon pay it off.”
.I ve never borrowed, lad. I’ve starved many
a tune rather than owe for a crust. And a mort-
gaged farm is a farm gone; I have seen it so often.
1 hey are never paid back. The worry of it would
kdl your mother.”
I hat’s all prejudice, father. It’s done every
day. And what was the use of educating me if I
was not to have a chance?” Fred spoke resent-
fully, and puffed vigorously at a big cigar. The
father walked by his side, carrying on his bent
back a small bag of potatoes up to the house.
But, despite his words, there was a glisten of
pride in the father’s eyes as he looked at the man
at. his side, with black coat, white shirt, and
shining boots.
“I’ll speak to mother.”
The mother was spoken to, was over-persuaded ;
and with tears in her eyes she gave a half-consent.
The election was fought and lost, and another
election, following quickly, was fought and won.
The old people were full of joy. “Fred an
M.L.A.! Taking his place with the best of
them!” Even the mother, forgetting her buggy,
drove proudly into the township on market-day
in the old cart. No more was thought of the
mortgage. “Of course, Fred would see to that
now”—and, two years passing, it had almost
dropped out of memory. Then—perhaps it was
the depression in the land market, perhaps arrears
of interest not paid by Fred, or perhaps the
advent of a new manager—for some reason the
bank became suddenly urgent for its money, and
sent a peremptory notice to the old man. He—
doubting, wondering, at one moment assured, at
another despondent—posted the letter to Fred.
At first they were sanguine. “ Was not Fred an
M.L.A. and a power in the land ? ” But a week
elapsed, during which father and mother scarce
slept, before this answer came:
Dear Father. —I wish you could have made some pro-
vision to meet this claim. As things are, Ido not know
which way to turn for money, and, of course, my position
has to be kept up. Perhaps, on the whole, it would be
better to let the old place go—it was never much good—
and make a new start. I might be able to do something.
Your affectionate son, Fred.
The mother turned very white, and gripped the
table hard as she stood and listened. “ Make a
new start!” she gasped. “How dare he say
that ? Our life is here ; our blood and sweat have
made the place what it is. Make a new start! ”
she repeated ; “ when I move again it will be to
my grave.”
“It’s very rough, Ann,” stammered the old
man ; “ but perhaps the boy’s right—he must have
a lot of expense ; and be sure we shall never come
to want now he is an M.L.A.”—and, with tears
in his eyes, the father pronounced the final words
with a kind of triumph.
With the advent of the man who took possession
the mother fell sick. Perhaps if Fred had come
to see them it might have been better with her.
For the rest, not a reproach did she utter. “ I
suppose the poor boy’s busy. A member of
Parliament must have such a lot to do.” Nor
when her state became critical would she have
him sent for. “It might upset him ! ”
• • • *
A year later, by the influence of Roseworth
Richards, M.L.A., an old man of the same surname
was admitted to the Govt. Benevolent Asylum,
and R. Richards, M.L.A., caused an allowance of
eighteenpence a week to be paid to the inmate.
With bowed head and a sickening sense of
wounded pride the old man put on the livery of
charity “He had made a new start.” But when
the first pang was over, the thought of his son
“amongst the best of them” gave him a dogged
courage to hold his head high even on the shaded
bench where he sat the day long with his fellow
unfortunates. At times, one of these obtained a
newspaper, and the old man showed a childish
eagerness to hear the news of Parliament. When
it chanced that his son’s name occurred in some
debate, his eyes brightened ; but he gave no hint
of the relationship. “It would be hard on the
boy,” he muttered to himself, “if it were known
his father was in the poorhouse.”
The parliamentary progress of R. Richards,
M.L.A., was rapid. In less than three years he
was chosen a member of the Works Committee,
and his income rose to £BOO a year. It chanced
that while the pleasure of his second success still
warmed him the son met the father in the pre-
cincts of the House.
“ You here ! Why did you come ? What’s the
matter ? ”
“I got a day out, sonny; and I thought I’d
come and hear you all talking in the big place,”
the old man answered diffidently.
“ Oh, that’s it! I thought—but no matter. I
tell you what it is, Dad ’’—and the son lowered his
voice so as not to be overheard —“I’m not quite
so hard jammed for cash as I was, and I mean to
put your pension up a bit; but not a word, mind.”
“ Mum’s the word, sonny! mum’s the word !
Do you take me for an old fool! And never you
mind the extra money. 1 don’t want it. A
shilling will buy my bit of ’bacca ; and you must
be under no end of expense now. ”
Till the old man worked his way to a front seat
in the gallery he failed to hear distinctly, and
even then he caught only disjointed sentences.
But his face glowed as he heard his son was speak-
ing on old-age pensions.
“We must think of those who have borne the
heat and burden of the day. All have helped in
their degree to build up the wealth and grandeur
of this great country. Who dare say that the
reward of each is fairly apportioned? Is not
wealth too often the badge of selfishness and
greed, or an accident of fortune that no foresight
can control? The honorable member talks of
‘thrift’; lam sick of ‘thrift.’”
The face of the old man glowed as he harkened,
and in his excitement he nudged first one neigh-
bor and then another. “ Ain’t he just grand ! ”
“Listen to that now!” The usher warned him
to keep quiet. He was silent for a time, till the
peroration of the-speaker caused a fresh outburst.
This time the usher led him from his seat, and
unresentingly, the old man walked away. “ What
did it matter? His boy had finished ; and wasn’t
he wonderful; just wonderful! ”
In answer to an urgent telegram, R. Richards,
M.L.A., hastened to the death-bed of a pauper in
the asylum. When the attendant had left them
he bent over the patient.
“ I hope I didn’t trouble you—l asked them to
send. Come closer, sonny ! I want to tell you
something—l never spent the half-crowns, I saved
them for you ; you will find them under my
pillow. I knew well enough you must be under a
power of expense.
“You have not said anything, father?” in-
quired the M.L.A. anxiously.
“Do you think I’m as dotty as that? Never
you fear! No ! Mum’s the word ; mum’s the
word to the last.”
* » * *
“Did you know him?” inquired the attendant
as he drew a sheet reverently over the bed.
“Oh yes, pretty well. He was an old pensioner
of mine—a kind of distant relation. Down on his
luck and that sort of thing these last few years.
I did what I could for him.”
“ Very good of you, sir.”
“ Oh, help a lame dog, you know,” said the
M.L.A. “ One does what one can.”
N.S.W.
HENRY FLETCHER.
Portleigh’s “Indian."
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Early one morning, accompanied by Paddy, a
Diamantina blackboy, I crossed the heathy
waste which skirts our part of the coastline, for
an hour’s sport among the rabbits. Where a
grass-grown roadway made a tempting stamping-
ground for “bunny” I took shelter. Paddy
touched my shoulder.
“Look out, Boss!” he said. “Fork come
up ! ”
“ Fork” is the nearest he can get to “ fox,” and
it was to a fox carrying some white object that he
drew my attention. I raised the gun and fired.
The fox dropped its burden, and disappeared in
the undergrowth. The burden proved to be a
goose, recently killed. Paddy lifted it from the
ground.
“Coose belongin’ to Portleigh,” he said.
Portleigh is a new-chum—a Londoner, and my
nearest neighbor. No doubt, as Paddy said, the
bird was his; I had remarked geese near his
place often. I told Paddy to take it there, and
explain how we found it. He tucked the bird
under his arm and departed.
The Portleigh family was at breakfast when he
arrived. To them entered Mary-Jane, maid-of-
all-work, who said :
“There’s an Injun at the back-door, ma’am,
with a message ; but I can’t make nothing of
what he says.”
“Confound him I” said Portleigh.
“Don’t let your breakfast grow cold, Henry,”
said his wife. “Let the man come up and
deliver his message.”
Mary Jane retired and presently returned with
Paddy, whose eyes wandered over the well-spread
table. He drew the bird from beneath his coat.
“ Coose,” he said. “ Fork bin kill-um.”
Mrs. Portleigh looked at the bird, but failed to
recognise it.
“ What does he mean ? ” she asked.
“ Well, I fancy he’s one of those Indian hawkers
we hear so much about, my dear,” replied Port-
leigh. “No doubt they sometimes have to take
poultry in exchange for their goods. Do you want
to sell the goose. Blackie?”
“Baal,” said Paddy; “ fork bin kill-um.”
The lady looked puzzled. “Fork?” she said,
and glanced at the silver upon the table.
“Hindus are particular, I believe, in the way
they kill the creatures they use for food,” said
Portleigh. “You killed him with a fork, eh
Blackie?”
“Fork bin kill-um. Fork! All asame as
dingo.”
“I see. You gave him stingo with a fork.
Well, it’s as good a way to kill a goose as any
other, I suppose.”
Paddy looked perplexed.
“The bird seems fat—and fresh,” remarked
Mrs. Portleigh. “Give the man three shillings
for it, my love, and let him go.”
Portleigh drew some silver from his pocket and
proffered the amount named to Paddy, who looked
wistfully at the coins but made no motion to
accept them.
“ Fork bin kill-um,” he repeated.
“The fact of the bird having been killed by a
fork adds to its value, eh?” said Portleigh, “Well,
then, here’s four shillings for you.”
To finally refuse riches thus thrust upon him
was more than Paddy could do. He took the
money, and with it purchased a red cotton hand-
kerchief and a damaged concertina which, much
to my satisfaction, would emit no sound save an
asthmatic wheezing.
When I met Portleigh a few days later he said :
“By-the-way, Banks, did you hear how my
wife was taken in by an Indian hawker? The
fellow actually sold her one of her own geese—
killed with a pitchfork, he said —and she hadn’t
the slightest suspicion that she’d been ‘had’ until
some time after the man had gone. Pretty rich,
wasn’t it? But, by George, if that’s the way these
hawker fellows carry on, I don’t wonder at there
being an outcry against their admission to the
States. ”
R. V. L.

PEARS
The Holy Twilight.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was the Holy Twilight,
The last of the Last Day ;
The sun had shrunk from heaven,
The moon had fled away.
Archangels wept above her,
The Earth that was so great,
The Regent of the star-worlds.
The Star Inviolate.
Orion, slowly turning,
Set three lights at her head,
Three lights in silence burning,
Three lights above the dead.
It was the Holy Tsvilight,
Between the night and day,
And there was time for sorrow,
And there was time to pray.
There came a wind of darkness,
There came a wind of fire, \
And smote on land and ocean
The Star of our desire.
I stood upon an island
Where Glory had her birth,
The last of all the green land
That once we called the Earth,
Five little children, singing,
Were waiting on the sands,
White roses on their foreheads,
And lilies in their hands.
And “ Who are you, my Sweet One ? ”
And “ Who are you, my Dear ? ”
“We are the kind deeds you have done
Many and many a year.”
Seven kings in raiment royal
Their gold crowns offered me—
“Oh, hide us from God’s anger,
And take us on the sea ! ”
There came along a Poet,
And very poor was he ;
He brought a bird upon his hand,
A dog beside his knee.
The five sweet angels, smiling,
Set all the kings aside—
Said : “ We have room in plenty,
And you shall with us bide.”
And Israfel, the Singer,
Spake, smiling quietly:
“ The birds shall go to Heaven—
They are good company.”
The wild beasts in the forest,
They gazed with wistful eyes ;
They had no Guardian Angel,
No friend in Paradise.
Thcri gleamed from that last forest,
As they paced up and down,
The spotted leopard’s topaz eyes,
The lion’s eyes of brown.
Then spake a bold Archangel,
His voice was brave and high—
“ These also are Thy creatures,
O God—and must they die ? ”
God laughed at His Archangel,
The beasts came at His nod —
For there are many forests
In the wide lands of Gol.
It was the Holy Twilight,
The last of the Last Day,
God send us time for sorrow !
God send us time to pray !
N.s.w.
VICTOR J. DALRY.
“A Bit of a Gonoph.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Wish you’d back the dray into the fire a bit,”
said Charley, “ it’s goin’ ter be darn cold.”
“We’ll have to lift her bodily then, or you
won’t be able to lie down.”
The reason was that the trooper had handcuffed
Charley to the dray-wheel, while he went out to
let his horse get a bit of grass. The horse would
have to stand by a tree, too, through the night,
for he couldn’t be trusted in hobbles.
Charley was our cook, and we had 800 bullocks
going down to Overland Corner. He had only
been with us three days—three days out of Tere-
bong. Jimmy Brown got drunk there, and
nothing would shift him while his cheque held.
Then Charley turned-up, and, after one day’s
experience with him, Jimmy’s absence was not
regretted.
The trouble was a very simple affair, as Charley
put it. “I come in dead broke, trusting to old
Silas to see me through, and he wouldn’t stand me
so much as a pinch o’ sugar. Had given it best,
he said ; he’d been had too often ; and my boots
were broken right across, and the straps had cut
the blankets through. It was as easy to open
Silas’ store as pull aside the tent-flap. His money
was stowed safe enough, but I didn’t want that;
a rig-out an’ some rations an’ a bit of weed —Silas
owed me that much, anyhow. But I nicked two
pair o’ pants and gave one lot to Jemmy Rickson;
they put me away.”
“An’ you’re wearin’ the others?” said the
trooper.
“Yes, an’ there’s the blankets, an’ the hat, an’
the boots ; Silas can swear to ’em all. And now
why not peg it back through the night ? Your
horse is fit, and I’d rather pad the hoof than lie
here like a chained dingo.”
The trooper funked the night trip. “I’ll loose
you now,” said he, “ and you can sit down an’
pitch till next watch. Then I’ll put the bracelet
round yer leg and fasten t’other end to me own
with a dog-chain, and we’ll both sleep comfortable
enough.”
But Charley didn’t sleep well. When I came
off watch' at twelve he was sitting up on his
blanket smoking, the Old Man alongside him.
I heard the Old Man say, “An’ how the devil ye
face it, I can’t understand” ’* Charley answered :
“ A couple o’ years’ll do it. I’ll live through
that. ”
“Two years! an’ for less than ye’d earn in a
fortnight ! It beats me, Charley—beats me
clean. ”
“There’s two things agen you, boss—agen your
understanding, I mean. First, you’ve never been
there, and I have, half-a-dozen times ; second,
you never knew the pleasure of handlin’ other
people’s stuff.”
“Ay!” said the Old Man, “that’s agen me,
true enough ! ”
Then he put the logs together, shoved on the
billy, and, after a spell, brought over a pot of tea,
and got Charley to talk some more. Queer talk
of the life a man who has got the “gonoph’s”
kink in his nature is foredoomed to live. Twenty
years of it, in and out of Berrima, Pentridge,
Darlinghurst ; yet half the time of liberty, spent
in good, capable, honest work. His voice grew a
little bitter towards the end, and be whispered
fiercely : “If he’d started in to-night neither of
us would have seen Teribong, an’ I should have
been heard tell of shortly. ”
I thought the Old Man ought to have spoken
out then, for it was plain he meant something,
and a little puff of hope would have drawn the
devil out of Charley. I wouldn’t have insured
that trooper’s life for next day’s journey.
But the Old Man was always better at doing
things than talking about them. He told us at
two o’clock he was going to look ahead a bit and
mightn’t be back till late. Then he rode away
into the night. The trooper wasn’t in much hurry
to get away. He fastened Charley to the wheel
again at daylight, and gave his horse an hour’s
good grazing. “It’s under 30 miles,” he said,
“ an’ we’ll do it easy by sundown. I’ll carry your
swag.”
“Oh, yes,” answered Charley, “we’ll do it;”
but he didn’t smile much, and he tucked a big
hunk of damper and beef inside his shirt.
They got away at 8, a sorry sight in the big
country—trooper up, spick and span, on his well-
groomed horse ; Charley down below, handcuffed
to the stirrup. Most men would feel bad that way
before they had done half of 30 miles.
Charley meant trouble from the start, but, as he
told months after, he had to foot it 20 miles before
his chance came. Trooper made him fast to a
nine-inch sapling while he boiled the billy about
12, and after that they had to cross a lignum
swamp full of dead logs and crab-holes where a
horse was bound to stumble over or into some-
thing.
He did stumble, and then, as the trooper told
the tale, “ seemed to lurch right over, and I went
out of the saddle as if the devil kicked me, and
me head must have hit the log hard. .. .” That
was all he knew. It was by good luck that he
was permitted to tell it.
For as Charley tramped along the determination
grew in him to do no more “time.” He watched
his chance, and when it at length came in a log-
littered, crab-holey patch, he put his shoulder right
beneath the trooper’s foot and gave a quick heave.
That was the devil’s kick. (Don’t try it unless
you are in earnest).
Half-a-minute later he had unlocked the hand-
cuffs, hitched up the horse, and transferred pro-
prietary rights in the service revolver. He was
going right on for robbery, too, and didn’t know if
he’d stop there. There was nothing at the end but
the rope or a bullet. He meant to get all the
start he could. If the trooper didn’t turn up he
might reckon on two clear days. His wrist was
sore and his feet burned, his legs ached. Black
gall had been in his soul since daybreak. But the
trooper looked pretty ghastly, stunned, white ; a
trickle of blood on his cheek. If he would only
make a move ! But he didn’t.
First movement was in the scrub and close at
hand. The Old Man and Silas the Jew were
riding cautiously, and their horses’ hoofs made no
sound on the soft soil of the dry swamp.
Charley had just shoved the revolver back into
its pouch when they were upon him.
The Jew tumbled down in a hurry : “Ith he
dead ? Ith he dead ? S’help me, but it’s a pity.
And to pinch you, Charley ! Didn’t I give you the
clotheth and the blanketh, an’ thame day they
went through me for five-and-twenty pair, thame
as on Jemmy, the drunkard. But ith he dead ? ”
He was only conveniently dazed and sat up a
few minutes later. Aud then things were ex-
plained. The Jew was rather convincing than
satisfactory. The case was dead, anyhow.
“ Charley,” said the Old Man, when they had
travelled a mile or two together, “ it’s cost me £5O
to square the Jew.”
“ Then I’ll do a whole year’s graft for you for
tucker. Two if you like.”
“ You’re as good a man as ever followed cattle,
Charley! ”
“ I can knock most of ’em.”
“ Then while I’m in the world you’re with me,
lagged for life, d’ye see ! ”
I met the Old Man trucking at Wagga thirteen
years later, and Charlie was out on the common
in charge of his camp.
F.M.
Conjugal Amenities.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Twice that day his wife surprised him reading a
note from another.
“You will know that letter backwards soon,” she
said as he thrust it away the second time.
“It is from Ted,” he said, passing a note to
her.
“The other looked smaller,” she said, jealously.
He laughed at her foolishness.
* * • •
She called him softly in the night.
“Gilbert, Gilbert!” she whispered, and gently
shook his shoulder. He feigned sleep, and
watched —the note from the other crushed in his
hand, since he dared not leave it about.
She rose quietly and lit the lamp, her eyes
spear-points on the calmly-breathing figure in the
bed. Creeping to his clothes she carried a hand-
ful of his letters to the light, and searched them.
Two small notes fell to the ground. She swooped
upon them, glanced at and threw aside the one
from Ted, and with trembling fingers opened the
other.
It was a sonnet addressed “To My Wife” in
her husband’s writing ! Like magic the letters
vanished, and the light went out. She kissed her
husband very tenderly, and slept the sleep of the
happily-deluded.
He raised the note in his hand to his lips, and
slept the sleep of the well-beloved.

Japanese health authorities have been
tackling the problem of keeping the navy men
(20,000 of them) healthy. Smallpox has been
absolutely abolished by repeated vaccinations and
beri-beri almost so by improvements in diet.
There were 2000 cases a year of beri-beri a decade
ago ; now the number has decreased to six a year.


Purifies the Blood
Bushmen Musi Drink Bad Water. This Makes Bad Bland. Sickness
Follows. How to Make the Blood Pure.

Mr. Alexander Johnson, of Bourke,
New South. \v ales, sends us his photo-
graph and says:
u For many years I have been en-
gaged in shepherding,
and other occupations so well known
to bushmen. Often the drinking-
water was very bad, and I was ex-
posed to other hardships of bush life.
I found all the men around me using
Ayer’s Sarsaparilla, sol tried it. For
cleansing and purifying my blood, and
for giving me new life and energy, I
found it the most wonderful medi-
cine I ever used. I do not see how
bushmen get along without it. I
advise all who read this to keep a
bottle of this grand medicine on hand
all the time, for it’s a great health
preserver.”
“The World’s Greatest Family Medicine.”
